Question: 55 CertyIQ
You have an on-premises datacenter named DC1 that contains two routers.
You have an Azure subscription. The subscription contains a virtual network named VNet1 and a zone-redundant
ExpressRoute virtual network gateway named GW1 that uses the ErGw3Az SKU. GW1 is attached to VNet1
DC1 is connected to VNet1 by using an ExpressRoute Standard circuit named Circuit1. The DC1 routers are
configured as endpoints for Circuit1. Circuit1 traffic traverses two physical links.
During a link outage, the connection takes three minutes to fail over.
You need to ensure that failovers between the links take less than one second.
What should you do?
A. For Circuit1, select FastPath.
B. On the routers, configure Bidirectional Forwarding Detection (BFD).
C. For GW1, change SKU to UltraPerformance.
D. For GW1, set Active-active mode to Enabled.
Answer: B
Explanation:
The correct answer is B. On the routers, configure Bidirectional Forwarding Detection (BFD).
Here's why:
The primary goal is to reduce the failover time between the two physical links of the ExpressRoute circuit
from three minutes to less than one second.
Bidirectional Forwarding Detection (BFD): BFD is a low-overhead protocol used to detect faults in a network
path. It works by sending frequent "hello" packets between the routers at each end of the ExpressRoute
circuit. If a router stops receiving these packets from its peer, it quickly detects a failure and can initiate a
failover. This rapid detection is significantly faster than relying on the default keep-alive mechanisms. BFD for
Azure ExpressRoute enables fast failure detection by establishing sub-second detection for router failures.
By configuring BFD on the on-premises routers that serve as endpoints for Circuit1, the failover time can be
drastically reduced to below one second, meeting the requirement.
Let's examine why the other options are incorrect:
A. For Circuit1, select FastPath: ExpressRoute FastPath is designed to improve data path performance by
allowing network traffic to bypass the ExpressRoute gateway. While it can enhance throughput and reduce
latency, it doesn't directly address the failover time between the two physical links of the circuit. FastPath's
performance benefits focus on data transfer, not link redundancy.
C. For GW1, change SKU to UltraPerformance: Changing the gateway SKU to UltraPerformance will increase
bandwidth and improve performance, but it won't reduce the failover time between the two physical links in
DC1.
D. For GW1, set Active-active mode to Enabled: While active-active mode offers redundancy at the Azure
virtual network gateway level, it addresses failures with the Azure gateway itself rather than the physical
links within the ExpressRoute circuit connecting to the on-premises datacenter. It will not affect the failover
time between the DC1 routers.
Therefore, configuring BFD on the on-premises routers is the most effective solution to achieve the desired

fast failover time within the ExpressRoute circuit.
Supporting Documentation:
About ExpressRoute BFD: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/expressroute/expressroute-bfd
ExpressRoute FastPath: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/expressroute/expressroute-about-fastpath

Question: 57 CertyIQ
Note: This question is part of a series of questions that present the same scenario. Each question in the series
contains a unique solution that might meet the stated goals. Some question sets might have more than one correct
solution, while others might not have a correct solution.
After you answer a question in this section, you will NOT be able to return to it. As a result, these questions will not
appear in the review screen.
You have two Azure virtual networks named Vnet1 and Vnet2.
You have a Windows 10 device named Client1 that connects to Vnet1 by using a Point-to-Site (P2S) IKEv2 VPN.
You implement virtual network peering between Vnet1 and Vnet2. Vnet1 allows gateway transit. Vnet2 can use the
remote gateway.
You discover that Client1 cannot communicate with Vnet2.
You need to ensure that Client1 can communicate with Vnet2.
Solution: You reset the gateway of Vnet1.
Does this meet the goal?

A. Yes
B. No
Answer: B
Explanation:
The proposed solution of resetting the gateway of Vnet1 will not enable Client1 to communicate with Vnet2.
Resetting the gateway might address connectivity issues within Vnet1 itself, but it doesn't directly address
the routing requirements for P2S VPN clients to reach peered networks.
The core issue is that the P2S VPN client (Client1) needs to know how to route traffic destined for Vnet2
through Vnet1. Virtual network peering alone doesn't automatically propagate routes to P2S clients. Even with
gateway transit enabled, the P2S client doesn't inherently pick up the routing information for Vnet2.
To solve this, you need to configure custom routes on the VPN client to direct traffic destined for Vnet2's
address space through the VPN connection. This usually involves manually adding routes on Client1 or
pushing the routes via a custom script or configuration during the VPN connection establishment. Resetting
the gateway does not provide a mechanism for route propagation to P2S clients. Therefore, the solution does
not meet the stated goal.
Reference:
About Point-to-Site VPN routing:
Configure custom routes for P2S VPN:
Question: 58 CertyIQ
Note: This question is part of a series of questions that present the same scenario. Each question in the series
contains a unique solution that might meet the stated goals. Some question sets might have more than one correct
solution, while others might not have a correct solution.
After you answer a question in this section, you will NOT be able to return to it. As a result, these questions will not
appear in the review screen.
You have two Azure virtual networks named Vnet1 and Vnet2.
You have a Windows 10 device named Client1 that connects to Vnet1 by using a Point-to-Site (P2S) IKEv2 VPN.
You implement virtual network peering between Vnet1 and Vnet2. Vnet1 allows gateway transit. Vnet2 can use the
remote gateway.
You discover that Client1 cannot communicate with Vnet2.
You need to ensure that Client1 can communicate with Vnet2.
Solution: You enable BGP on the gateway of Vnet1.
Does this meet the goal?
A. Yes
B. No
Answer: B
Explanation:
The proposed solution of enabling BGP on the gateway of Vnet1 will not enable Client1 to communicate with
Vnet2. Here's why:
1. Problem: Client1 connects to Vnet1 using a Point-to-Site (P2S) IKEv2 VPN. The core issue is that
routes from Vnet2 are not being propagated to the P2S VPN clients connected to Vnet1. Virtual

network peering allows communication between the virtual networks themselves, but it doesn't
automatically extend this reach to P2S VPN clients.
2. Why BGP is insufficient: BGP (Border Gateway Protocol) is used to exchange routing information
between different Autonomous Systems (AS). In Azure, it is typically used between VPN gateways
and on-premises routers or between different VPN gateways in different regions. Enabling BGP on
the Vnet1 gateway won't automatically advertise Vnet2's routes to the P2S clients. P2S clients rely
on routes pushed to them during the VPN connection establishment. BGP is more relevant for Site-to-
Site VPN scenarios.
3. The Correct Approach: To enable Client1 (and other P2S clients) to reach Vnet2, you need to
manually add a static route to the P2S configuration on the Vnet1 gateway. This static route would
specify that traffic destined for Vnet2's address space should be routed through the Vnet1 gateway.
4. Alternative approaches: An alternative approach might involve deploying an NVA (Network Virtual
Appliance) capable of acting as a router and advertising routes learned from Vnet2 to P2S clients via
a routing protocol. However, for a simple scenario as described, the manual static route addition is
the most straightforward solution.
5. Authoritative Links:
About Point-to-Site VPN routing: This documentation clarifies how routes are managed in Point-to-
Site VPN connections.
Configure VPN gateway transit for virtual network peering: This document explains the use of
gateway transit, but it does not mention how P2S client routes are impacted, reinforcing that manual
routes or other mechanisms are required for P2S.
In conclusion, the solution is incorrect because enabling BGP on the gateway alone doesn't propagate Vnet2's
routes to the P2S VPN clients connected to Vnet1. The appropriate approach is to add a static route to the
P2S configuration.

Question: 60 CertyIQ
You plan to deploy Azure virtual network.
You need to design the subnets.
Which three types of resources require a dedicated subnet? Each correct answer presents a complete solution.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
A. Azure Bastion
B. Azure Active Directory Domain Services (Azure AD DS)
C. Azure Private Link
D. Azure Application Gateway v2
E. VPN gateway
Answer: ADE
Explanation:
The correct answer is ADE, as these resource types require dedicated subnets in Azure virtual networks for
proper functionality and security.
A. Azure Bastion: Azure Bastion is deployed within a dedicated subnet. This subnet must be named
AzureBastionSubnet. Azure Bastion enables secure RDP/SSH connectivity to VMs in a virtual network without
exposing them to the public internet. A dedicated subnet provides a secure perimeter for the Bastion host,
allowing it to mediate connections while isolating it from other network traffic.
https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/bastion/configuration-settings#subnet
D. Azure Application Gateway v2: Azure Application Gateway (v2 SKU specifically) also requires its own
dedicated subnet. This subnet facilitates the management and isolation of the Application Gateway's
infrastructure. Azure Application Gateway acts as a web traffic load balancer, managing HTTP(S) traffic for
web applications. By placing it in a dedicated subnet, you ensure that the gateway has enough IP addresses
and that it's isolated from other resources to enhance security and performance.
https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/application-gateway/configuration-infrastructure#size-the-subnet
E. VPN Gateway: A VPN gateway, whether a route-based or policy-based configuration, resides in a specific
subnet named GatewaySubnet. This dedicated subnet provides the VPN gateway with the required resources
for establishing secure, cross-premises connectivity between an Azure virtual network and an on-premises
network. A GatewaySubnet is essential for supporting both VPN and ExpressRoute gateways within the same
VNet. The 'GatewaySubnet' name is mandatory and it must contain enough free addresses for the Gateway.
https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/vpn-gateway/vpn-gateway-about-vpngateways#gwsub
Why B and C are incorrect:
B. Azure Active Directory Domain Services (Azure AD DS) requires a virtual network, but not a dedicated
subnet in the same sense as the previous services. It does need to reside in its own VNet if you do not already
have one.C. Azure Private Link creates private endpoints within your VNet/subnet that you select. It leverages
private IP addresses and requires you to explicitly define the specific subnet to place the endpoint. While
important for securing resources, it isn't a "dedicated" subnet in the sense that the subnet name is fixed, such
as with Bastion or VPN gateways.

Question: 68 CertyIQ
Note: This question is part of a series of questions that present the same scenario. Each question in the series
contains a unique solution that might meet the stated goals. Some question sets might have more than one correct
solution, while others might not have a correct solution.
After you answer a question in this section, you will NOT be able to return to it. As a result, these questions will not
appear in the review screen.
You have two Azure virtual networks named Vnet1 and Vnet2.
You have a Windows 10 device named Client1 that connects to Vnet1 by using a Point-to-Site (P2S) IKEv2 VPN.
You implement virtual network peering between Vnet1 and Vnet2. Vnet1 allows gateway transit. Vnet2 can use the
remote gateway.
You discover that Client1 cannot communicate with Vnet2.
You need to ensure that Client1 can communicate with Vnet2.
Solution: You download and reinstall the VPN client configuration.
Does this meet the goal?
A.Yes
B.No

Answer: A
Explanation:
The solution proposes downloading and reinstalling the VPN client configuration to enable Client1, connected
to Vnet1 via P2S VPN, to communicate with Vnet2, which is peered with Vnet1, allowing gateway transit from
Vnet1. This solution is correct.
Here's why: When a VPN client connects to a virtual network that is part of a peered network and is
configured to use the remote network's gateway for transit, the client needs to have the updated routes from
both networks. The initial VPN configuration that was set up might not include the routes to the peered virtual
network (Vnet2 in this case).
Downloading and reinstalling the VPN client configuration ensures that the client receives the latest routing
information from Azure. This new configuration will now include the necessary routes for Vnet2, enabling the
client to send traffic to Vnet2 through Vnet1's gateway. Without the updated routing information, Client1
would not know how to reach Vnet2, even if peering and gateway transit are correctly configured at the
virtual network level. The reinstall process effectively redistributes the client configuration package, allowing
the VPN client to recognize the routes for the remote network.
Therefore, reinstalling the VPN client configuration after enabling gateway transit between the peered virtual
networks resolves the issue and enables Client1 to communicate with Vnet2.
Here are some authoritative links for further research:
About VPN Gateway P2S client configuration: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/vpn-gateway/vpn-
gateway-howto-point-to-site-resource-manager-portal
Configure VPN Gateway transit for virtual network peering: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/vpn-
gateway/vpn-gateway-peering-gateway-transit
Question: 69 CertyIQ
You have an Azure virtual network named Vnet1 that hosts an Azure firewall named FW1 and 150 virtual machines.
Vnet1 is linked to a private DNS zone named contoso.com. All the virtual machines have their name registered in
the contoso.com zone.
Vnet1 connects to an on-premises datacenter by using ExpressRoute.
You need to ensure that on-premises DNS servers can resolve the names in the contoso.com zone.
Which two actions should you perform? Each correct answer presents part of the solution.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
A. Modify the DNS server settings of Vnet1.
B. For FW1, configure custom DNS server.
C. For FW1, enable DNS proxy.
D. On the on-premises DNS servers, configure forwarders that point to the frontend IP address of FW1.
E. On the on-premises DNS servers, configure forwarders that point to the Azure provided DNS service at
168.63.129.16.
Answer: CD
Explanation:
Here's a detailed justification for why options C and D are the correct actions to take to ensure on-premises
DNS servers can resolve names in the Azure private DNS zone contoso.com, and why the other options are
incorrect:

Justification:
The core problem is enabling on-premises DNS servers to resolve records within the Azure private DNS zone
"contoso.com." Because this zone is private, it's not directly accessible from the public internet or the on-
premises network. We need a way to relay DNS queries from on-premises to Azure, and then have Azure
resolve the internal names.
C. For FW1, enable DNS proxy: Enabling DNS proxy on the Azure Firewall (FW1) is crucial. This makes the
firewall act as a DNS resolver on behalf of the VMs in Vnet1. Without this, the firewall won't forward DNS
queries to the Azure DNS service, which has the necessary information about the "contoso.com" zone. The
Azure Firewall can leverage Azure's DNS infrastructure for resolution.
D. On the on-premises DNS servers, configure forwarders that point to the frontend IP address of FW1: This
is the other essential piece. You configure your on-premises DNS servers to forward queries for the
"contoso.com" domain to the front-end IP address of the Azure Firewall (FW1). When the on-premises DNS
server receives a query for a name in "contoso.com," it sends that query to FW1. FW1, with its DNS proxy
enabled, then forwards the query to Azure DNS for resolution. Azure DNS resolves the query using the private
DNS zone information.
Why other options are incorrect:
A. Modify the DNS server settings of Vnet1: While you might think about this, the virtual machines are
already using the Vnet's default which points to Azure's internal DNS. Changing this might break name
resolution within the Vnet. More importantly, this does not allow external on-prem resolution.
B. For FW1, configure custom DNS server: Setting a custom DNS server on the firewall would change the
DNS that the firewall itself uses to resolve names. However, on-prem machines are unaware that the
contoso.com domain can now be resolved by the Azure Firewall.
E. On the on-premises DNS servers, configure forwarders that point to the Azure provided DNS service at
168.63.129.16: This is incorrect for two primary reasons. First, directly using 168.63.129.16 is not supported in
all scenarios, particularly when dealing with private DNS zones. The firewall acts as a secure and managed
proxy to the Azure DNS service. Second, the on-prem DNS servers would need to pass through the
ExpressRoute connection which they may not be authorized to do.
Authoritative Links:
Azure Firewall DNS Proxy: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/dns-proxy
Azure Private DNS Zones: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/dns/private-dns-overview
Hybrid DNS Resolution: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/architecture/reference-
architectures/networking/hybrid-dns-infra
Question: 70 CertyIQ
You are planning the IP addressing for the subnets in Azure virtual networks.
Which type of resource requires IP addresses in the subnets?
A. internal load balancers
B. storage account
C. Azure Virtual Networks NAT
D. service endpoint policies
Answer: A
Explanation:

The correct answer is A, internal load balancers. Internal load balancers in Azure require IP addresses from
the subnet in which they are deployed. When you create an internal load balancer, you must assign it a private
IP address from the subnet. This IP address acts as the front-end IP for the load balancer and distributes
traffic to the back-end pool instances.
Storage accounts (B) don't directly consume IP addresses within subnets. They have public endpoints or can
be integrated with private endpoints, which then consume IP addresses. Azure Virtual Network NAT (C) is
used for outbound connectivity and while it uses an IP address, it's typically a public IP or a private IP from a
dedicated subnet for NAT, not IP addresses within a regular application subnet. Service endpoint policies (D)
are access control mechanisms and don't themselves consume IP addresses. They determine which subnets
can access specific Azure services. Therefore, only internal load balancers explicitly require IP addresses
from the virtual network subnet for their functionality.
Further Reading:
Azure Load Balancer: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/load-balancer/
Azure Virtual Network: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/
Azure Private Endpoint: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/private-link/private-endpoint-overview

Question: 73 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT -
You have two Azure virtual networks named VNet1 and VNet2 in an Azure region that has three availability zones.
You deploy 12 virtual machines to each virtual network, deploying four virtual machines per zone. The virtual
machines in VNet1 host an app named App1. The virtual machines in VNet2 host an app named App2.
You plan to use Azure Virtual Network NAT to implement outbound connectivity for App1 and App2.
You need to identify the minimum number of subnets and Virtual Network NAT instances required to meet the
following requirements:
✑ A failure of two zones must NOT affect the availability of either App1 or App2.
✑ A failure of two zones must NOT affect the outbound connectivity of either App1 or App2.
What should you identify? To answer, select the appropriate options in the answer area.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
Hot Area:
Answer:

Explanation:
1. Minimum Number of Subnets:6
Azure Virtual Network NAT (Network Address Translation) allows outbound internet access for resources in a
private subnet.
Each subnet that requires NAT must be explicitly associated with a NAT gateway.
If there are six subnets requiring NAT, then six subnets are needed.
The selection of 6 means that at least six different subnets need NAT configurations.
2. Minimum Number of Virtual Network NAT Instances:6
Virtual Network NAT is a fully managed Azure service that replaces Load Balancer outbound rules for
outbound internet connectivity.
The number of NAT instances required depends on the scale and redundancy needs.
Azure recommends deploying one NAT gateway per subnet for efficient traffic management.
If there are six subnets, and each subnet requires its own NAT instance, then six NAT instances are needed.

Question: 76 CertyIQ
You have a hub-and-spoke topology. The topology includes multiple on-premises locations that connect to a hub
virtual network in Azure via ExpressRoute circuits.
You have an Azure Application Gateway named GW1 that provides a single point of ingress from the internet.
You plan to migrate the hub-and-spoke topology to Azure Virtual WAN.
You need to identify which changes must be applied to the existing topology. The solution must ensure that you
maintain a single point of ingress from the internet.
Which three changes should you include in the solution? Each correct answer presents part of the solution.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
A. Add user-defined routes.
B. Add virtual network peerings.
C. Replace the user-defined routes used by the current topology.
D. Create virtual network connections.
E. Remove the existing virtual network peerings.
F. Redeploy GW1.
Answer: CDE
Explanation:
The correct answer is CDE. Here's why:
C. Replace the user-defined routes used by the current topology: When transitioning to Virtual WAN, the
existing UDRs that manage traffic flow within the hub-and-spoke network become irrelevant. Virtual WAN
handles routing automatically through its central routing infrastructure. Therefore, you need to replace these
old UDRs with configurations supported by Virtual WAN, such as route propagation and associations.
D. Create virtual network connections: Virtual WAN connects virtual networks (VNets) using virtual network
connections. These connections establish the connectivity between the VNets and the Virtual WAN hub. Each
spoke VNet will need a connection to the Virtual WAN hub to enable communication. This essentially replaces
the peerings in the original hub-and-spoke model.
E. Remove the existing virtual network peerings: Virtual WAN inherently manages the connectivity and
routing between connected VNets. Therefore, the virtual network peerings that previously connected the hub
and spoke VNets become redundant and must be removed to avoid routing conflicts and ensure Virtual WAN
has full control over the network topology.
Why other options are incorrect:
A. Add user-defined routes: While UDRs are still supported in VNets connected to a Virtual WAN, they are not
the primary way to control routing between VNets connected to the Virtual WAN hub. Virtual WAN uses
routing policies and route propagation, which simplifies routing management. Adding UDRs in this case could
create routing conflicts.
B. Add virtual network peerings: Virtual WAN is designed to replace the need for manual virtual network

peering.
F. Redeploy GW1: Application Gateway does not need to be re-deployed. You can point it to a new backend
like backend pool that contains virtual machines deployed in the Virtual WAN network or leverage the Virtual
WAN hub to direct traffic to on-premises or internet resources.
Supporting Documentation:
About Virtual WAN
Virtual WAN FAQ
Question: 77 CertyIQ
You have an application named App1 that listens for incoming requests on a preconfigured group of 50 TCP ports
and UDP ports.
You install App1 on 10 Azure virtual machines.
You need to implement load balancing for App1 across all the virtual machines. The solution must minimize the
number of load balancing rules.
What should you include in the solution?
A. Azure Application Gateway V2 that has multiple listeners
B. Azure Standard Load Balancer that has Floating IP enabled
C. Azure Standard Load Balancer that has high availability (HA) ports enabled
D. Azure Application Gateway v2 that has multiple site hosting enabled
Answer: C
Explanation:
The correct answer is C, Azure Standard Load Balancer with High Availability (HA) Ports enabled. Here's why:
HA Ports are specifically designed to simplify load balancing scenarios involving a large number of ports.
Instead of configuring individual load balancing rules for each of the 50 TCP and 50 UDP ports (totaling 100
rules), HA Ports automatically handle traffic for all ports. This satisfies the requirement to minimize the
number of load balancing rules. By using HA Ports, only one load balancing rule needs to be configured, vastly
simplifying the setup.
Azure Application Gateway (options A and D) is primarily designed for web application traffic (HTTP/HTTPS)
and offers features such as Web Application Firewall (WAF), SSL termination, and URL-based routing. While it
can handle some non-HTTP traffic, it is not the most efficient or cost-effective solution for balancing a wide
range of TCP and UDP ports like in this scenario. Furthermore, Application Gateway would require multiple
listeners or site configurations, increasing complexity and the number of rules.
Floating IP (option B) is related to allowing virtual machines to maintain a single public IP address even if they
move between Azure infrastructure. While helpful in some network scenarios, it doesn't address the core
requirement of minimizing load balancing rules across numerous ports. The number of rules needed remains
the same.
HA Ports on Azure Standard Load Balancer are tailored for scenarios where an application needs to listen on a
wide range of ports, making it the most efficient solution in this case.
For further research, you can refer to the official Microsoft documentation on Azure Load Balancer and HA
Ports:
Azure Load Balancer documentation: Provides a comprehensive overview of Azure Load Balancer, including

the Standard tier features.
HA Ports documentation: Explains the purpose and configuration of HA Ports in Azure Load Balancer.
Question: 78 CertyIQ
DRAG DROP -
You register a DNS domain with a third-party registrar.
You need to host the DNS zone on Azure.
Which three actions should you perform in sequence? To answer, move the appropriate actions from the list of
actions to the answer area and arrange them in the correct order.
Select and Place:
Answer:
Explanation:
Step 1: Create a public DNS zone.
Create a DNS zone -
1. Go to the Azure portal to create a DNS zone. Search for and select DNS zones.

2. Select Create DNS zone.
3. On the Create DNS zone page, enter the following values, and then select Create.
Step 2: Identify the FQDNs of the name servers.
Retrieve name servers.
Before you can delegate your DNS zone to Azure DNS, you need to know the name servers for your zone.
Azure DNS gives name servers from a pool each time a zone is created.
With the DNS zone created, in the Azure portal Favorites pane, select All resources. On the All resources
page, select your DNS zone. If the subscription you've selected already has several resources in it, you can
enter your domain name in the Filter by name box to easily access the application gateway.
Retrieve the name servers from the DNS zone page. In this example, the zone contoso.net has been assigned
name servers ns1-01.azure-dns.com, ns2-
01.azure-dns.net, *ns3-01.azure-dns.org, and ns4-01.azure-dns.info:

Azure DNS automatically creates authoritative NS records in your zone for the assigned name servers.
Step 3: Modify the NS records for the domain.
Delegate the domain -
Once the DNS zone gets created and you have the name servers, you'll need to update the parent domain with
the Azure DNS name servers.
Each registrar has its own DNS management tools to change the name server records for a domain.
1. In the registrar's DNS management page, edit the NS records and replace the NS records with the Azure
DNS name servers.
2. When you delegate a domain to Azure DNS, you must use the name servers that Azure DNS provides. Use
all four name servers, regardless of the name of your domain. Domain delegation doesn't require a name
server to use the same top-level domain as your domain.

Question: 80 CertyIQ
Note: This question is part of a series of questions that present the same scenario. Each question in the series
contains a unique solution that might meet the stated goals. Some question sets might have more than one correct
solution, while others might not have a correct solution.
After you answer a question in this section, you will NOT be able to return to it. As a result, these questions will not
appear in the review screen.
You have two Azure virtual networks named Vnet1 and Vnet2.
You have a Windows 10 device named Client1 that connects to Vnet1 by using a Point-to-Site (P2S) IKEv2 VPN.
You implement virtual network peering between Vnet1 and Vnet2. Vnet1 allows gateway transit. Vnet2 can use the
remote gateway.
You discover that Client1 cannot communicate with Vnet2.
You need to ensure that Client1 can communicate with Vnet2.
Solution: You resize the gateway of Vnet1 to a larger SKU.
Does this meet the goal?
A. Yes
B. No
Answer: B
Explanation:
The proposed solution of resizing the Vnet1 gateway to a larger SKU does not address the core issue of
Client1's inability to communicate with Vnet2. The problem stems from routing configurations and the
limitations of Point-to-Site (P2S) VPN connections in Azure, particularly when used in conjunction with virtual
network peering and gateway transit.
Here's a detailed breakdown:
1. Problem: Client1 connects to Vnet1 via P2S VPN. Virtual network peering exists between Vnet1 and
Vnet2 with gateway transit enabled. Client1 cannot reach Vnet2.
2. Root Cause: P2S VPN clients typically receive routes only for the virtual network they connect to
(Vnet1 in this case). They are not automatically provided routes to peered networks (Vnet2) through
the gateway transit. The resizing of the gateway does not alter this fundamental routing behavior.
3. Why resizing the gateway is irrelevant: Gateway SKUs primarily influence throughput, the number of
supported connections, and feature availability (like ExpressRoute coexistence). They do not
automatically propagate routes to P2S clients for peered networks.

4. Correct Approach (Alternatives): To enable Client1 to communicate with Vnet2, several options are
available, none of which involve simply resizing the gateway:
Custom Routes: Configure custom routes on Client1 (or push them via the P2S configuration) to
explicitly direct traffic destined for Vnet2's address space through the Vnet1 gateway. This requires
knowing Vnet2's CIDR block.
Azure Route Server (ARS): ARS simplifies route exchange between Azure VNets, SD-WAN
appliances and ExpressRoute. It can be used to dynamically learn and propagate routes between
Vnets including the Vnet behind P2S VPN.
Azure Virtual Network Gateway as a Router (Not Recommended for Production): While technically
feasible, using the Vnet1 gateway as a router and manually configuring routing tables is complex and
not recommended for production environments due to management overhead.
NAT (Not Recommended): Network Address Translation (NAT) could allow Client1 to access Vnet2,
but it breaks end-to-end addressing and is generally discouraged.
5. Lack of Route Propagation: The P2S VPN gateway does not automatically advertise routes to the
peered Vnet (Vnet2) to the connected clients (Client1). The P2S clients need to explicitly know about
the peered Vnet's address space and how to reach it.
6. Gateway Transit Mechanism: Gateway transit allows Vnet2 to use the VPN gateway in Vnet1. It does
not automatically configure the VPN clients connected to Vnet1 to route to Vnet2. The traffic from
the P2S Client needs to have a route to Vnet2 via Vnet1 gateway.
7. Authoritative References:
About VPN Gateway settings: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/vpn-gateway/vpn-gateway-
about-vpn-gateway-settings
Configure VPN gateway transit for virtual network peering: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-
us/azure/vpn-gateway/vpn-gateway-peering-gateway-transit
Azure Route Server: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/route-server/
In conclusion, resizing the Vnet1 gateway does nothing to resolve the routing issue preventing Client1 from
communicating with Vnet2. The problem requires a routing solution, like custom routes or Azure Route Server,
to propagate routes to the P2S client. Therefore, the answer is definitively "No."
Question: 81 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains the virtual networks shown in the following table.
You plan to deploy an Azure firewall named AF1 to RG1 in the West US Azure region.
To which virtual networks can you deploy AF1?

B. Vnet1, Vnet2, Vnet3, and Vnet4

D. Vnet1 and Vnet2 only
E. Vnet1, Vnet2, and Vnet4 only

Question: 82 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT
-
You have two Azure App Service instances that host the web apps shown the following table.
You deploy an Azure 2 that has one public frontend IP address and two backend pools.
You need to publish all the web apps to the application gateway. Requests must be routed based on the HTTP host
headers.
What is the minimum number of listeners and routing rules you should configure? To answer, select the
appropriate options in the answer area.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.

Answer:

Explanation:
1. Listeners :2
A listener in Azure Application Gateway is responsible for accepting incoming requests based on a specified
protocol (HTTP/HTTPS) and port.
Listeners bind to a frontend IP configuration and define how requests should be processed.
If there are two different frontend configurations (e.g., different domains, ports, or protocols), then two
listeners are required.
2. Routing Rules : 2.
Routing rules define how the incoming requests should be processed after being received by a listener.
Each routing rule specifies:
The backend pool (where the request should be forwarded).
The path-based or host-based routing mechanism.
If there are two different routing rules, they might be set up to:
Route traffic based on different URLs (e.g., /api vs. /app).
Forward requests to different backend pools (e.g., WebApp1 and WebApp2).

Question: 83 CertyIQ
Your company has four branch offices and an Azure subscription. The subscription contains an Azure VPN gateway
named GW1.
The branch offices are configured as shown in the following table.
The branch office routers provide internet connectivity and Site-to-Site VPN connections to GW1.
The users in Branch1 report that they can connect to internet resources, but cannot access Azure resources.

following requirements:
• Minimize downtime for all users.
• Minimize administrative effort.
What should you do first?
A. Recreate LNG1.
B. Reset RTR1.
C. Reset Connection1.
D. Reset GW1.
Answer: C
Explanation:
C: Reset Connection1.
Minimize Downtime:
Resetting the connection (Connection1) typically involves a quick re-establishment of the connection between
Branch1 and Azure resources. This action is likely to result in minimal downtime compared to other options
like recreating or resetting other components.
Minimize Administrative Effort:
Resetting the connection is a straightforward administrative task. It doesn't involve recreating or
reconfiguring larger components like gateways or routers, which would require more time and effort.
It also avoids the need for deeper troubleshooting or configuration changes that could introduce additional
risks or complexity.
https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/vpn-gateway/reset-gateway

-
You have an Azure subscription that contains a virtual network named Vnet1 and an Azure SQL database named

Question: 86 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT
-
You have an Azure subscription that contains the virtual networks shown in the following table.
You have a virtual machine named VM5 that has the following IP address configurations:
• IP address:10.4.0.5
• Subnet mask:255.255.255.0
• Default gateway: 10.4.0.1
• DNS server: 168.63.129.16
You have an Azure Private DNS zone named fabrikam.com that contains the records shown in the following table.
The virtual network links in the fabrikam.com DNS zone are configured as shown in the exhibit. (Click the Exhibit
tab.)
VM5 fails to resolve the IP address for app1.fabrikam.com.
For each of the following statements, select Yes if the statement is true. Otherwise, select No.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.

Answer:
Explanation:
1. Updating the IP address configurations of VM5 to use a DNS server address of 10.4.0.2 will enable the
virtual machine to resolve app1.fabrikam.com.
NO
Setting a custom DNS server IP (10.4.0.2) does not automatically guarantee name resolution unless:
The DNS server at 10.4.0.2 has a record for app1.fabrikam.com.
The virtual network is correctly linked to the Private DNS Zone.
2. Enabling a virtual network link for Vnet3 in the fabrikam.com DNS zone will enable VM5 to resolve
app1.fabrikam.com.
YES
In Azure Private DNS, a virtual machine can only resolve DNS records in a Private DNS Zone if its virtual
network is linked to that zone.
If VM5 is inside Vnet3, and Vnet3 is linked to the fabrikam.com DNS zone, then VM5 can resolve
app1.fabrikam.com.
Conclusion: If Vnet3 is linked to the zone, VM5 can resolve the DNS name.
3. Adding an A record for app1.fabrikam.com to the fabrikam.com DNS zone will enable VM5 to resolve
app1.fabrikam.com.
NO
Adding an A record is necessary for name resolution, but it alone is not sufficient.
The virtual machine must be in a VNet linked to the Private DNS Zone.
If VM5 is in a VNet that is not linked, it will not resolve the DNS name even if the A record exists.

Question: 87 CertyIQ
Your company has five offices. Each office has a firewall device and a local internet connection. The offices
connect to a third-party SD-WAN.
You have an Azure subscription that contains a virtual network named Vnet1. Vnet1 contains a virtual network
gateway named Gateway1. Each office connects to Gateway1 by using a Site-to-Site VPN connection.
You need to replace the third-party SD-WAN with an Azure Virtual WAN.
What should you include in the solution?
A. Delete Gateway1.
B. Create new Point-to-Site (P2S) VPN connections on the firewall devices.
C. Create an Azure Traffic Manager profile.
D. Enable active-active mode on Gateway1.
Answer: A
Explanation:
Here's a detailed justification for why deleting Gateway1 is the correct initial step when migrating from a Site-
to-Site VPN setup to Azure Virtual WAN:
The existing network infrastructure utilizes a hub-and-spoke topology with Gateway1 acting as the VPN
gateway within the Vnet1 hub, connecting the five offices via Site-to-Site VPN tunnels established through
their individual firewalls and a third-party SD-WAN. The goal is to replace this setup with Azure Virtual WAN,
which provides a centralized network management platform for connecting branch offices and remote users
to Azure. Virtual WAN inherently provides its own VPN gateway functionality within the Virtual Hub.
Given the current configuration using Site-to-Site VPNs with Gateway1, transitioning to Virtual WAN
necessitates removing the existing Azure VPN gateway (Gateway1) to avoid conflicts and ensure proper
routing within the new Virtual WAN environment. Virtual WAN essentially handles the creation and
management of VPN gateways within its virtual hubs, negating the need for the pre-existing Gateway1. You're
essentially migrating the VPN gateway functionality from a VNet gateway (Gateway1) to a Virtual WAN hub
gateway.
Option B, creating new Point-to-Site VPN connections on the firewall devices, is incorrect because Point-to-
Site VPNs are designed for individual user connections, not site-to-site connectivity between offices and a
central Azure hub. Option C, creating an Azure Traffic Manager profile, is irrelevant as Traffic Manager
focuses on load balancing and traffic routing across different Azure regions or endpoints, not on establishing
core network connectivity between branch offices and Azure. Option D, enabling active-active mode on
Gateway1, might seem relevant for redundancy, but it doesn't address the fundamental requirement of
migrating to the Virtual WAN architecture. Gateway1 is being replaced by Virtual WAN’s built-in gateway
capabilities.
Therefore, the first crucial step is to dismantle the previous VPN architecture centered around Gateway1,
making option A the correct initial action for a clean and conflict-free migration to Azure Virtual WAN. After
deleting Gateway1, you would proceed to create an Azure Virtual WAN, connect the branch offices using
Virtual WAN's Site-to-Site VPN functionality, and configure routing policies within the Virtual WAN hub.
Supporting links:
Azure Virtual WAN Documentation: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-wan/virtual-wan-about
Virtual WAN Architecture: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-wan/virtual-wan-faq#what-is-the-
architecture-of-virtual-wan

Migrate to Virtual WAN: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-wan/how-to-migrate-hub-spoke
Question: 88 CertyIQ
You are planning the IP addressing for the subnets in Azure virtual networks.
Which type of resource requires IP addresses in the subnets?
A.internal load balancers
B.Azure DDoS Protection for virtual networks
C.service endpoint policies
D.service endpoints
Answer: A
Explanation:
The correct answer is A, internal load balancers. Here's why:
Internal load balancers (ILBs) operate within a virtual network (VNet). They require a private IP address from a
subnet to serve as their front-end IP. This IP address is the entry point for services within the VNet. When
traffic is sent to this IP address, the load balancer distributes it among the backend instances (e.g., VMs) in
the configured backend pool. This assignment of a private IP allows services within the VNet to access the ILB
and its distributed applications.
Azure DDoS Protection for virtual networks (option B) doesn't directly consume IP addresses within your
subnets. It's a service that provides protection against distributed denial-of-service attacks and is applied at
the VNet level, operating mostly outside the subnets themselves.
Service endpoints (option D) enable secure and direct access to Azure services from your VNet. Service
endpoint policies (option C) further refine this access. While these endpoints interact with subnets, they don't
require IP addresses within the subnet to be assigned to the endpoint itself. The resources accessing services
via service endpoints still need IPs, but the endpoint itself doesn't "consume" an IP from the subnet's address
space. The subnet trusts certain service endpoints, and traffic destined for these service endpoints is then
routed optimally via the Azure backbone network without going through the public internet.
In summary, the essential functionality of an internal load balancer is tied to a specific IP within a subnet to
function properly.
Supporting Links:
Azure Load Balancer: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/load-balancer/
Internal Load Balancer: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/load-balancer/load-balancer-internal
Azure DDoS Protection: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/ddos-protection/
Service Endpoints: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/virtual-network-service-
endpoints-overview
Question: 89 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription mat contains tour virtual networks named VNet1, VNet2, VNet3, and VNet4.
You plan to deploy a hub and spoke topology by using virtual network peering.
You need to configure VNet1 as the hub network. The solution must meet the following requirements:
• Support transitive routing between spokes.
• Maximize network throughput.
What should you include in the solution?

A. Azure VPN Gateway
B. Azure Route Server
C. Azure Private Link
D. Azure Firewall
Answer: B
Explanation:
B. Azure Route Server.
Azure Route Server simplifies dynamic routing between an NVA and Azure VNets by automatically
exchanging routes using Border Gateway Protocol (BGP). This eliminates the need for manual User-Defined
Routes (UDRs) and the associated administrative effort.

Question: 92 CertyIQ
SIMULATION
-

Username and password
-
Use the following login credentials as needed:

To enter your password, place your cursor in the Enter password box and click on the password below.

Azure Password: xxxxxxxxxx
-
If the Azure portal does not load successfully in the browser, press CTRL-K to reload the
portal in a new browser tab.
The following information is for technical support purposes only:
Lab Instance: 12345678
-
You need to ensure that subnet 4-3 can accommodate 507 hosts.
To complete this task, sign in to the Azure portal.
Answer:
Explanation:
Step 1: Navigate to Azure Virtual Networks
Open the Azure portal.
Go to Virtual Networks.
Step 2: Select the Virtual Network
Choose the VNet containing the subnet you want to modify.
Step 3: Access Subnet Settings
Under Settings, click Subnets.
Step 4: Select the Target Subnet
Choose the subnet to be modified (e.g., subnet 4-3).
Step 5: Modify Subnet Address Range

The number of required hosts determines the CIDR notation of the subnet.
Example Scenario:
If 507 hosts are required, a 9-bit address range is necessary.
The correct subnet size is /23 (512 addresses).
Example: 10.0.0.0/23.
Subnet Sizing Considerations
Azure reserves certain IPs in every subnet:
First and last address for protocol conformance.
Three additional addresses for Azure service usage.
The smallest subnet range allowed in Azure is /29 (8 IPs total, 3 usable).
Example of subnet allocations:
/22 → 1024 IPs
/23 → 512 IPs
/24 → 256 IPs
/29 → 8 IPs (only 3 usable).
Step 6: Save Changes
Click Save to apply the new subnet address range.
Why is this Important?
Ensures enough IPs for network resources.
Prevents IP exhaustion in growing networks.
Optimizes Azure services that rely on subnet IP allocation.
Question: 93 CertyIQ
You are planning the IP addressing for the subnets in Azure virtual networks.
Which type of resource requires IP addresses in the subnets?
A. internal load balancers
B. Azure DDoS Protection for virtual networks
C. service endpoint policies
D. service endpoints
Answer: A
Explanation:
Internal load balancers in Azure require IP addresses from the subnet in which they are deployed to function
correctly. This is because an internal load balancer needs an IP address to serve as its frontend, allowing

traffic to be distributed to the backend pool of virtual machines within that virtual network. The frontend IP
address acts as the access point for services running on the backend VMs. The subnet provides the range of
IP addresses from which the load balancer's frontend IP can be allocated, making IP address availability a
crucial prerequisite for its deployment. Without a valid IP address within the subnet, the internal load balancer
cannot accept and distribute traffic.
Azure DDoS Protection plans, service endpoint policies, and service endpoints themselves do not directly
consume IP addresses from the subnet in the same manner as an internal load balancer. DDoS Protection
protects the entire virtual network but operates at a higher level than individual IP addresses within subnets.
Service endpoint policies and service endpoints secure and route traffic to Azure services (like Azure Storage
or Azure SQL Database) without assigning IP addresses to a specific resource within the subnet. They define
access control and network routing rules but don't require dedicated IPs from the subnet's address space for
their core function. Service endpoints utilize the virtual network's address space but do not directly consume
specific IP addresses within the subnets for their operation. Instead, they leverage network routes and
firewall rules based on service tags.
Therefore, only internal load balancers necessitate IP addresses from the subnet's range, making it the
correct answer. Other options focus on network security and connectivity at a different layer of abstraction.
Relevant Documentation:
Azure Load Balancer: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/load-balancer/
Azure Virtual Network service endpoints: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/virtual-
network-service-endpoints-overview
Azure DDoS Protection: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/ddos-protection/ddos-protection-overview

Question: 106 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT
-
You have an Azure subscription that contains a virtual network named Vnet1. Vnet1 has a /24 IPv4 address space.
You need to subdivide Vnet1. The solution must maximize the number of usable subnets.
What is the maximum number of IPv4 subnets you can create, and how many usable IP addresses will be available
per subnet? To answer, select the appropriate options in the answer area.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.

Answer:
Explanation:
Usable IP Addresses: 3
In networking, the number of usable IP addresses in a subnet is determined by the subnet mask.
Every subnet reserves two IP addresses:
Network Address (first IP, used to identify the subnet)
Broadcast Address (last IP, used to send data to all hosts in the subnet)
If only 3 usable IP addresses are available, this means the subnet must be small.
A /30 subnet (255.255.255.252) provides 4 total IPs, out of which 2 are usable for hosts.

IPv4 Subnets: 32
The number 32 refers to the number of subnet divisions.
Subnetting divides a larger network into smaller subnetworks to optimize IP allocation and network
performance.
A /27 subnet (255.255.255.224) supports 32 subnets and allows 30 usable IP addresses per subnet.

Question: 108 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT
-
Your company has 40 branch offices across North America and Europe.
You have an Azure subscription that contains the following virtual networks:
•Two networks in the East US Azure region
•Three networks in the West Europe Azure region
You need to implement Azure Virtual WAN. The solution must meet the following requirements:
•Each branch office in North America must have an ExpressRoute circuit and a Site-to-Site VPN that connects to
the East US region.
•Each branch office in Europe must have an ExpressRoute circuit and a Site-to-Site VPN that connects to the West
Europe region.
•Transitive connections must be supported between all the branch offices and all the virtual networks.
•Costs must be minimized.
What is the minimum number of Virtual WAN resources required? To answer, select the appropriate options in the
answer area.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.

Answer:

Explanation:
1. Virtual WAN: One Standard Virtual WAN.
Virtual WAN (VWAN) is a Microsoft Azure networking service that simplifies large-scale branch connectivity,
hybrid networking, and site-to-site VPNs.
Standard Virtual WAN supports additional features compared to Basic Virtual WAN, such as:
Hub-to-hub routing
Secure virtual hub (firewall integration)
ExpressRoute integration
Point-to-site VPN
Selecting "One Standard virtual WAN" means:
The deployment is utilizing advanced connectivity options.
There is only one WAN instance managing network traffic.
2. Virtual WAN Hub: Two Virtual WAN Hubs.
A Virtual WAN Hub is a regional hub in Azure Virtual WAN that acts as a central connection point for branch
offices, VPNs, and ExpressRoute.
Having two Virtual WAN hubs means:
The organization is deploying in two Azure regions for redundancy and load balancing.
Multiple locations or branch offices may be connecting to separate hubs to improve performance and
reliability.
3. Virtual Network Gateway: Four Virtual Network Gateways,
A Virtual Network Gateway is used for:
VPN Gateway (site-to-site, point-to-site, and multi-site VPN connections).
ExpressRoute Gateway (private connectivity to on-premises networks).
Selecting four virtual network gateways means:
Multiple VPN and/or ExpressRoute gateways are set up for handling large-scale connections.
There may be multiple regions involved or high availability (HA) architecture.
Question: 109 CertyIQ
DRAG DROP
-
You have a DNS domain named contoso.com that is hosted by a third-party domain name registrar.
You have an Azure subscription.
You need to ensure that all DNS queries for the contoso.com domain are resolved by using Azure DNS.
What should you create in the registrar, and what should you create in Azure? To answer, drag the appropriate
options to the correct targets. Each option may be used once, more than once, or not at all. You may need to drag
the split bar between panes or scroll to view content.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.

Answer:
Explanation:
Registrar (A delegation): You must configure your domain registrar (where you purchased the domain name) to
point to the Name Servers provided by your Azure DNS zone. This process is known as delegation, which tells
the internet that Azure is now authoritative for your domain's DNS records.
Azure (A public DNS zone): Within the Azure portal, you create a public DNS zone to host your domain records
(such as A, CNAME, TXT, etc.). Once created, this zone provides you with a set of name servers that you then
plug into your domain registrar.
Question: 110 CertyIQ

-
You have an on-premises network.
You have an Azure subscription that contains the resources shown in the following table.
You need to implement an ExpressRoute circuit to access the resources in the subscription. The solution must

Which type of peering should you use for each connection? To answer, select the appropriate options in the
answer area.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
Answer:
Explanation:
Private Peering (Vnet1): This is used to connect to your private IP addresses inside your Azure Virtual
Networks (Vnets). It is the standard way to extend your on-premises network into Azure for internal resources
like virtual machines.
Microsoft Peering (SQL1): This is used to connect to Microsoft public services. Since Azure SQL Database
(often referred to as SQL1 in these scenarios) has a public endpoint, you utilize Microsoft peering to access it
over the ExpressRoute circuit rather than over the public internet, ensuring a dedicated and secure path.
https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/expressroute/expressroute-circuit-peerings

Question: 111 CertyIQ
You are planning the IP addressing for the subnets in Azure virtual networks.
Which type of resource requires IP addresses in the subnets?
A.storage account
B.internal load balancers
C.service endpoints
D.virtual network peering
Answer: B
Explanation:
The correct answer is B, internal load balancers, because they require an IP address within the subnet to act
as the frontend IP for the load balancer. This IP address is used by clients to connect to the load balancer,
which then distributes traffic to the backend pool of virtual machines or other resources.
Storage accounts do not directly consume IP addresses within a subnet. While storage accounts have public
endpoints by default, and can be integrated with virtual networks using service endpoints or private
endpoints, the service endpoints (option C) themselves don't consume IPs, they configure the storage account
and the network to allow traffic to securely pass between them. Private endpoints, however, do consume IPs
but aren't listed as an option. Thus, option C is still incorrect.
Virtual network peering (option D) enables connectivity between virtual networks but does not require
individual IP addresses within the subnets being peered. Peering establishes a link that allows traffic to flow
between the address spaces of the peered networks. Peering relies on routing configurations and network
policies.
Internal load balancers need a dedicated IP address to function as the entry point for traffic within the virtual
network. They act as a central point of contact and distribute traffic across the backend servers. Without an IP
address assigned to the internal load balancer, it cannot be reached, and traffic cannot be routed. The IP
address sits in the same subnet of the resources it is balancing, and is considered part of that subnet's
address space.
Here's an explanation using cloud computing concepts: Internal load balancers act as a network service
within the virtual network, requiring an IP address to be discoverable and reachable. This IP address is
essential for the load balancer's role in distributing traffic among backend resources.
For more information, refer to the following Microsoft Azure documentation:
Azure Load Balancer: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/load-balancer/
Azure Virtual Network Peering: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/virtual-network-
peering-overview
Azure Storage Service Endpoints: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/storage/common/storage-
network-security

VNet1. VWAN is connected to the on-premises networks and VNet1 in a full mesh topology. The virtual hub routing

In Azure, certain resources cannot share subnets or have specific naming requirements. To meet a standard
hybrid networking scenario, you need:
Subnet 1 (Workload Subnet): For standard resources like Virtual Machines (VMs).
Subnet 2 (Delegated Subnet): Required for services like Azure Container Instances (ACI) or Azure App Service

Question: 114 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains a virtual network named VNet1.
You deploy several web apps and configure the apps to use private endpoints on VNet1.
You need to identify which DNS records the web apps registered automatically.
Where will the records be created?
A.an Azure DNS zone named privatelink.azurewebsites.net
B.an Azure Private DNS zone named azurewebsites.net
C.an Azure Private DNS zone named privatelink.azurewebsites.net
D.an Azure DNS zone named azurewebsites.net
Answer: C
Explanation:
The correct answer is C. an Azure Private DNS zone named privatelink.azurewebsites.net.
When you create a private endpoint for Azure services, including web apps, a private DNS zone is used to
resolve the private IP addresses of the service endpoints within your virtual network. These DNS zones allow
your VMs and other resources within the VNet to reach the services using their private IPs, bypassing the
public internet.
For Azure Web Apps, the naming convention for the private DNS zone is privatelink.azurewebsites.net. Azure
automatically creates and manages these DNS records in the private DNS zone associated with the private
endpoint. This ensures that when a web app uses the private endpoint, DNS resolution for the web app's
hostname (e.g., myapp.azurewebsites.net) within VNet1 resolves to the private IP address associated with the
private endpoint, instead of the public IP address. This keeps the traffic within the Azure network.
Options A and D are incorrect because azurewebsites.net is not the naming convention used for private DNS
zones related to private endpoints. Option B is incorrect because while Azure Private DNS zones are used, the
specific zone for web apps is named privatelink.azurewebsites.net to identify it as a private link DNS zone.
Azure DNS zones are typically for public resolution and not private resolution within a VNet, hence they would
not contain the records created for Private Endpoints.Further Reading:
Azure Private Link DNS integration: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/private-link/private-endpoint-
dns
Configure Azure DNS Private Zones: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/dns/private-dns-getstarted-
portal

Question: 116 CertyIQ
You are planning the IP addressing for the subnets in Azure virtual networks.
Which type of resource requires IP addresses in the subnets?
A.storage account
B.internal load balancers
C.service endpoints
D.service endpoint policies
Answer: B
Explanation:
The correct answer is B. internal load balancers.
Internal load balancers (ILBs) in Azure require IP addresses from the subnet in which they are deployed. The
ILB's frontend IP configuration necessitates an IP address within the virtual network subnet. This IP address
serves as the single point of contact for applications within the virtual network.
Storage accounts, service endpoints, and service endpoint policies do not directly consume IP addresses
within subnets in the same manner as internal load balancers. While storage accounts have public endpoints,
they don't necessitate a specific IP address reservation within a subnet. Service endpoints provide secure and
direct connectivity to Azure services by extending a virtual network's private address space, but they
themselves don't require dedicated subnet IP addresses. Service endpoint policies are used to filter outbound
traffic to service endpoints and also do not consume IP addresses. Therefore, option B is the correct answer
because deploying an internal load balancer within a subnet requires an IP address from that subnet to serve
as its private frontend IP.
For further information, refer to the official Microsoft Azure documentation on Azure Load Balancer:
Azure Load Balancer Documentation

Question: 118 CertyIQ
You plan to implement an Azure virtual network that will contain 10 virtual subnets. The subnets will use IPv6
addresses. Each subnet will host up to 200 load-balanced virtual machines.
You need to recommend which subnet mask size to use for the virtual subnets.
What should you recommend?
A./64
B./120
C./48
D./24
Answer: A
Explanation:
Here's a detailed justification for recommending a /64 subnet mask for the Azure virtual network subnets
using IPv6, hosting up to 200 load-balanced virtual machines each:
Azure, by default, allocates a /64 IPv6 address space to each subnet within a virtual network. This is a
deliberate design decision by Microsoft to ensure adequate address space and simplify network management.
While you could technically carve out smaller IPv6 subnets, it's generally not recommended within Azure,
primarily due to this design principle and the way Azure handles IPv6 addressing.
A /64 subnet provides an incredibly large address space (2^64 addresses). This is more than sufficient for
hosting 200 VMs and providing room for growth and internal services within each subnet. Attempting to use a
smaller subnet (like /120) would mean the subnet's prefix may conflict with the /64 subnet that Azure
assigned, thus causing routing issues.
Using a larger subnet (like /48 or /24) is unnecessary and inefficient for this scenario. A /48 is typically
reserved for large organizations or entire sites, while /24 is still too large for a single subnet that only needs
to accommodate a few hundred machines. Allocating more addresses than needed leads to address space
wastage. Also, it's crucial to note that while the question does not explicitly indicate an issue utilizing a larger
subnet, it is best practice to efficiently use IP address space and to design according to Azure best practices.
The implicit requirement is the most efficient address use.
Therefore, the /64 default allocation is the optimal and recommended approach. It provides ample addresses,
aligns with Azure's design principles, and avoids unnecessary address space allocation.
https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/ip-services/ipv6-
overviewhttps://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/virtual-networks-faq - See section on IPv6.

Question: 119 CertyIQ
DRAG DROP
-
You have two on-premises datacenters.
You have an Azure subscription that contains four virtual networks named VNet1, VNet2, VNet3, and VNet4.
You create an Azure virtual WAN named VWAN1. VWAN1 contains a single virtual hub that is connected to both on-
premises datacenters and all the virtual networks in a full mesh topology.
You create a route table named RT1.
You need to configure VWAN1 to meet the following requirements:
•Connectivity between VNet1 and VNet2 and both on-premises datacenters must be allowed.
•Connectivity between VNet3 and VNet4 and both on-premises datacenters must be allowed.
•VNet1 and VNet2 must be isolated from VNet3 and VNet4.
How should you configure routing for VNet1 and VNet2 and for both on-premises datacenters? To answer, drag the
appropriate route tables and route table propagation to the correct requirements. Each route table and route table
propagation may be used once, more than once, or not at all. You may need to drag the split bar between panes or
scroll to view content.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
Answer:
Explanation:
1. VNet1 and VNet2
Associated route table (RT1): These VNets will look at RT1 whenever they want to send traffic. They do not
look at the Default route table.
Propagating to (Default): These VNets "shout" their address prefix into the Default route table. This means
anything associated with the Default table (like your On-premises) will know how to find them.
2. On-premises datacenters
Associated route table (Default): On-premises traffic will look at the Default route table to decide where to go.

Propagating to (RT1 and Default): On-premises "shouts" its address prefix into both tables. This ensures that
resources in both RT1 and Default know how to reach the datacenter.
Question: 120 CertyIQ
You are planning the IP addressing for the subnets in Azure virtual networks.
Which type of resource requires IP addresses in the subnets?
A.Azure Virtual Network NAT
B.service endpoint policies
C.internal load balancers
D.virtual network peering
Answer: C
Explanation:
The correct answer is indeed C, internal load balancers. Here's why:
An internal load balancer (ILB) in Azure requires IP addresses from the subnet where it's deployed because it
must have an IP address within the virtual network's address space. This IP address serves as the internal
endpoint for applications or services behind the load balancer. Clients within the same virtual network can use
this IP address to access the load-balanced resources.
Let's analyze the other options:
A. Azure Virtual Network NAT: While Azure Virtual Network NAT is used to provide outbound internet
connectivity for resources within a virtual network, it does not directly require IP addresses from the subnet
for its own operation. It uses a public IP associated with the NAT gateway. The resources using the NAT
gateway consume IP addresses from the subnet, but the NAT gateway itself does not.
B. Service Endpoint Policies: Service endpoint policies are used to filter egress traffic to Azure services using
service endpoints. They don't require dedicated IP addresses within the subnet for their operation. They work
by restricting the traffic based on the defined rules.
D. Virtual Network Peering: Virtual network peering enables you to seamlessly connect two or more virtual
networks in Azure. Peering itself doesn't consume IP addresses directly within a subnet. Resources in the
peered networks use their own assigned IPs from their respective subnets.
In summary, an internal load balancer needs an internal IP address from a subnet so that clients in the same
virtual network can access the applications or services behind the load balancer.
For further reading, you can refer to the official Microsoft Azure documentation on Internal Load Balancers:
https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/load-balancer/load-balancer-internal. You can also read about Azure
Virtual Network NAT here: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/nat-gateway/nat-overview
and Service Endpoints here: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/virtual-network-service-
endpoints-overview and Virtual Network Peering here: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-
network/virtual-network-peering-overview
Question: 121 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT

-
You have an Azure subscription that contains an Azure key vault named Vault1 and an app registration for an Azure
AD app named App1.
You have a DNS domain named contoso.com that is hosted by a third-party DNS provider.
You plan to deploy App1 by using Azure App Service. App1 will have the following configurations:
•App1 will be hosted across five App Service apps.
•Users will access App1 by using a URL of https://app1.contoso.com.
•The user traffic of App1 will be managed by using Azure Front Door.
•The traffic between Front Door and the App Service apps will be sent by using HTTP.
•App1 will be secured by using an SSL certificate from a third-party certificate authority (CA).
You need to support the Front Door deployment.
Which two DNS records should you create, and to where should you import the SSL certificate for App1? To
answer, select the appropriate options in the answer area.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
Answer:

Explanation:
1. DNS Records: An A record and a CNAME record.
A Record (Address Record):
This maps a domain name (e.g., example.com) to an IP address (IPv4).
Used for direct domain-to-IP mapping.
CNAME Record (Canonical Name Record):
This maps a domain alias to another domain name rather than an IP address.
Commonly used to create subdomains (e.g., www.example.com pointing to example.com).
A records are required when you want a domain to resolve directly to an IP.
CNAME records are useful for pointing a domain to another domain without using an IP.
This is a common setup for web hosting and domain name management.
2. Import the certificate to: Vault1
This likely refers to Azure Key Vault, a cloud service used to store and manage secrets, including SSL/TLS
certificates.
Azure Key Vault allows secure management of cryptographic keys and certificates.
Certificates stored in Key Vault can be used by Azure services, such as App Services and Virtual Machines.
Centralized security and policy enforcement.
Alternative options in the dropdown:
The app registration for App1: Would be used for identity management.

The App Service apps: Would be for direct use in Azure Web Apps.
Storing a certificate in Key Vault provides enhanced security and centralized management.
Certificates can be accessed by multiple services securely.
Useful for automated certificate renewal and policy enforcement.
Question: 122 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains a virtual network named VNet1. VNet1 has a subnet mask of/24.
You plan to implement an Azure application gateway that will have the following configurations:
•Public endpoints: 1
•Private endpoints: 1
•Minimum instances: 1
•Maximum instances: 10
You need to configure the address space for the subnet of the application gateway. The solution must minimize the
number of IP addresses allocated to the application gateway subnet.
What is the minimum number of assignable IP addresses required?
A.1
B.2
C.11
D.12
E.20
Answer: C
Explanation:
The minimum number of assignable IP addresses required for the application gateway subnet is 11.
Here's why:
Azure Application Gateway requires a dedicated subnet. While it might seem like only the configured
instances would need IPs, Azure reserves some addresses within the subnet for its own management and
operational needs.
Specifically, Azure reserves five IP addresses within each subnet for its internal use. This is a standard Azure
networking behavior to ensure proper functionality of the platform.
In this scenario, the application gateway has a minimum of 1 instance. This means we need at least one IP
address for the gateway itself, even when scaled down.
Further, the question states there will be one public endpoint and one private endpoint. Azure Application
Gateway v2 (the version typically deployed now) can handle both public and private endpoints on a single
gateway instance. This doesn't increase the IP address requirements for the gateway's instances themselves,
but is a factor in the overall subnet design.
The maximum number of instances is 10. While this sets the upper limit for scaling, we only need to consider
the minimum required addresses for this particular question.
Therefore, the calculation is as follows:
Azure reserved addresses: 5

Minimum instances: 1 (even if scaled down to 1 instance, the gateway still requires at least one IP)
Total required = 5 (reserved) + 1 (minimum instance) = 6.
However the minimum instances must be greater than 1 which means the subnet must accomodate at least 2
IP addresses,
Revised Total required = 5 (reserved) + 2 (minimum instance) = 7
For the instance to work, it must have at least a public address, and a private address to communicate inside
the VNET. The calculation becomes.Revised Total required = 5 (reserved) + 2 (minimum instance - internal
subnet) + 1 for public = 8
If we have 1 public endpoint, one private endpoint, and a minimum of 1 gateway instance, then we also need
two other IP addresses.
In addition we have to consider the instance size. If the size is small then 2 instances will be required.
So, with 1 public and 1 private, the subnet requires at least 2 IP addresses at all times. The updated calculation
is 5+2 = 7 IP Addresses.
Adding to all these consideration, there must also be at least 4 assignable addresses for the public or private
endpoints. 2 * public and private.
In addition to all this, Azure sometimes requires additional IP addresses for updates.Therefore, the bare
minimum is 5 (reserved) + 2 (min. instances) = 7 addresses + an addition 4 for public/private endpoints = 11 or
more
However, let's break down the official Azure guidance:
Reserved Addresses: Azure reserves the first four and the last IP addresses in each subnet.
Application Gateway v2: Requires a dedicated subnet.
The critical aspect is that Azure's internal use plus the gateway itself need IP addresses.
Final Calculation:
5 (Azure reserved) + 6 (for the public and private endpoints + instances running) = 11.
The best available answer to this question is: C. 11
Supporting Documentation:
Azure Virtual Network FAQ: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/virtual-networks-faq
Azure Application Gateway sizing: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/application-gateway/application-
gateway-autoscaling-zone-redundant#size

You have an Azure subscription that contains the resources shown in the following table.

Case Study
-

case study. Case studies might contain exhibits and other resources that provide more information about the
scenario that is described in the case study. Each question is independent of the other questions in this case study.
At the end of this case study, a review screen will appear. This screen allows you to review your answers and to
make changes before you move to the next section of the exam. After you begin a new section, you cannot return
to this section.
To start the case study
-
To display the first question in this case study, click the Next button. Use the buttons in the left pane to explore
the content of the case study before you answer the questions. Clicking these buttons displays information such
as business requirements, existing environment, and problem statements. When you are ready to answer a
question, click the Question button to return to the question.
Overview
-
Contoso, Ltd. is a consulting company that has a main office in San Francisco and a branch office in Dallas.
Contoso recently purchased an Azure subscription and is performing its first pilot project in Azure.
Existing Environment
-
Azure Network Infrastructure
-
Contoso has an Azure Active Directory (Azure AD) tenant named contoso.com.
The Azure subscription contains the virtual networks shown in the following table.

Vnet1 contains a virtual network gateway named GW1.
Azure Virtual Machines
-
The Azure subscription contains virtual machines that run Windows Server 2019 as shown in the following table.
The NSGs are associated to the network interfaces on the virtual machines. Each NSG has one custom security
rule that allows RDP connections from the internet. The firewall on each virtual machine allows ICMP traffic.
An application security group named ASG1 is associated to the network interface of VM1.
Azure Network Infrastructure Diagram
Azure Private DNS Zones
-
The Azure subscription contains the Azure private DNS zones shown in the following table.

Zone1.contoso.com has the virtual network links shown in the following table.
Other Azure Resources
-
The Azure subscription contains additional resources as shown in the following table.
Requirements
-
Virtual Network Requirements
-
Contoso has the following virtual network requirements:
•Create a virtual network named Vnet6 in West US that will contain the following resources and configurations:
o Two container groups that connect to Vnet6
o Three virtual machines that connect to Vnet6
o Allow VPN connections to be established to Vnet6
o Allow the resources in Vnet6 to access KeyVault1, DB1, and Vnet1 over the Microsoft backbone network.
•The virtual machines in Vnet4 and Vnet5 must be able to communicate over the Microsoft backbone network.
•A virtual machine named VM-Analyze will be deployed to Subnet1. VM-Analyze must inspect the outbound
network traffic from Subnet2 to the internet.
Network Security Requirements
-
Contoso has the following network security requirements:
•Configure Azure Active Directory (Azure AD) authentication for Point-to-Site (P2S) VPN users.
•Enable NSG flow logs for NSG3 and NSG4.
•Create an NSG named NSG10 that will be associated to Vnet1/Subnet1 and will have the custom inbound security
rules shown in the following table.
•Create an NSG named NSG11 that will be associated to Vnet1/Subnet2 and will have the custom outbound security
rules shown in the following table.
Which virtual machines can VM1 and VM4 ping successfully before NSG10 and NSG11 are created? To answer,
select the appropriate options in the answer area.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.

Answer:

Explanation:
VM1: VM2, VM3, and VM4 only.
This selection means that VM1 can communicate with VM2, VM3, and VM4, but not with VM5.
The possible reasons for this connectivity setup could be:
Network Security Groups (NSGs): Configured firewall rules allowing traffic between these VMs while
restricting VM5.
Virtual Network (VNet) Peering: VM5 might be in a different subnet or VNet with no peering enabled.
Subnet Configuration: VM5 could be in a different subnet with restricted routing.
VM4: VM1, VM2, and VM3 only.
This selection means that VM4 can communicate with VM1, VM2, and VM3, but not VM5.
Similar to VM1's case, the restriction on VM5 could be due to:
NSG rules restricting access.
Subnet segmentation where VM5 is placed in an isolated subnet.
Routing configurations preventing VM5 from reaching VM1, VM2, or VM3.
