Question: 132 CertyIQ
You have an on-premises DNS server named Server that hosts a primary DNS zone named fabrikam.com.
You have an Azure subscription that contains the resources shown in the following table.

Users on the on-premises network access resources on all the virtual networks by using a Site-to-Site (S2S) VPN.
You need to deploy an Azure DNS Private Resolver solution that meets the following requirements:
•Resources connected to the virtual networks must be able to resolve DNS names for fabrikam.com.
•Server1 must be able to resolve the DNS names of the resources in contoso.com.
•The solution must minimize costs and administrative effort.
What is the minimum number of resolvers you should deploy?
A.1
B.2
C.3
D.4
Answer: B
Explanation:
B. 2.
Resources connected to the virtual networks must be able to resolve DNS names for fabrikam.com.
This requires a DNS resolver configured to handle queries for fabrikam.com.
Server1 must be able to resolve the DNS names of the resources in contoso.com.
This requires a separate DNS resolver configured to handle queries for contoso.com.
The solution must minimize costs and administrative effort.
While minimizing costs and effort is important, the requirement to resolve DNS names for two different
domains (fabrikam.com and contoso.com) typically necessitates separate resolvers unless conditional
forwarding or a more complex setup is used, which could increase administrative effort.
Azure Dns private resolver is a regional service. "A DNS resolver can only refer to a virtual network that's in
the same region as the DNS resolver"
https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/architecture/networking/architecture/azure-dns-private-resolver
Question: 133 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT
-
You have an Azure subscription that contains a dual-stack virtual network named VNet1. VNet1 has the following IP
address spaces:
•IPv4: 192.168.0.0/24
•IPv6: fd00:db8:deca:deed:/48
You plan to deploy an Azure VPN gateway and multiple virtual machines to VNet1.
You need to configure the subnet masks for VNet1. The solution must meet the following requirements:

•Maximize the number of usable IP addresses.
•Support the deployment of the VPN gateway and the virtual machines.
Which subnet mask should you use for each address space? To answer, select the appropriate options in the
answer area.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
Answer: 1>3
Explanation:
IPv4 :/24
IPv4 addresses use a subnet mask to determine how many bits are allocated to the network and how many are
for host identification.
A /24 subnet mask means that 24 bits are allocated to the network portion, leaving 8 bits for host addresses.
Subnet Mask: 255.255.255.0
Total IPs in the Subnet: 256 (2⁸)
Usable IPs: 254 (since 2 addresses are reserved: network address (first IP) and broadcast address (last IP))
Use Case: /24 is a standard subnet size commonly used in enterprise networks, virtual networks, and cloud
environments because it provides a sufficient number of IPs.
IPv6 : /64
IPv6 does not use traditional subnet masks like IPv4 but instead defines subnets using prefix lengths.
A /64 subnet means that the first 64 bits represent the network prefix, while the remaining 64 bits are used
for host addresses.

Total IPs in the Subnet: 2¹²⁸-⁶⁴ = 2⁶⁴ (18 quintillion IPs!)
Use Case: /64 is the standard subnet size for IPv6 networks, as it supports SLAAC (Stateless Address
Autoconfiguration), making it ideal for most deployments, including cloud and enterprise networks.

using a user defined route (UDR) configuration. The firewall intercepts that traffic inline and passes it to the
destination.
https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/explicit-proxythe KEY element here is the TLS feature that
seems included in the premium only "advanced threat protection
https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/choose-firewall-sku

Question: 136 CertyIQ
DRAG DROP
-
You have an on-premises network.
You have an Azure subscription that contains a virtual network named VNet1. VNet1 is connected to an Azure
Virtual WAN hub named Hub1.
You need to enable connectivity between the on-premises network and VNet1 by using Hub1.
Which three actions should you perform in sequence? To answer, move the appropriate actions from the list of
actions to the answer area and arrange them in the correct order.
Answer:

Explanation:
1. Configure a Site-to-Site (S2S) VPN gateway.A Site-to-Site (S2S) VPN gateway is required to establish a
secure tunnel between an on-premises network and Azure.This gateway acts as a bridge, routing traffic
between on-premises resources and Azure Virtual Networks (VNets).2.Create a VPN site.A VPN site in Azure
represents the on-premises network in the Virtual WAN setup.This involves defining the public IP address of
the on-premises VPN device and specifying routing details.3.Connect the VPN site to Hub1."Hub1" refers to
the Azure Virtual WAN Hub, a centralized networking hub for managing connectivity between multiple
networks (on-premises, branch offices, Azure VNets).Connecting the VPN site to Hub1 establishes the routing
relationship needed for secure communication between on-premises resources and Azure services.
Question: 137 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription. The subscription contains a locally-redundant storage (LRS) account named
storage1 that is deployed to the US East Azure region and has a Microsoft.Storage service endpoint.
You set Redundancy for storage1 to Read-access geo-redundant storage (RA-GRS).
You need to ensure that the contents of storage1 will be accessible by using a service endpoint in a paired region.
The solution must minimize administrative effort.
What should you do first?
A.Create an object replication rule for storage.
B.Delete the existing service endpoint.
C.From storage1, select Secure transfer required.
D.Create a service endpoint policy.
Answer: B
Explanation:
B. Delete the existing service endpoint.
You originally created a service endpoint for Microsoft.Storage when the storage account was LRS (local
region only).
After upgrading to RA-GRS, the storage account now has:
Primary region: East US
Secondary paired region: East US 2 (read-only)

But service endpoints are region-bound at creation time.
Because the endpoint was created when the storage account was LRS, it only supports the primary region.
To allow access to the secondary region (paired region) using service endpoints:
You must remove the old service endpoint and recreate it, so Azure recognizes both regions.
This is the minimum administrative effort and the required step per Microsoft documentation.

Question: 139 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT
-
You have an Azure subscription that contains the resources shown in the following table.
You need to ensure that remote users can establish RDP connections to the virtual machines by using Bastion1.
How should you configure the rules for NSG1 for traffic from the internet to AzureBastionSubnet and for traffic
from AzureBastionSubnet to Subnet1? To answer, select the appropriate options in the answer area.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.

Answer:
Explanation:
1.Enable TCP port 443 inbound.
TCP port 443 (HTTPS) is required for users to connect securely to the Azure Bastion service through the
Azure portal.
Azure Bastion does not require RDP (3389) or SSH (22) to be open from the internet, improving security.
2.Enable TCP port 3389 outbound.
Azure Bastion connects to virtual machines over TCP port 3389 (RDP) for Windows VMs and TCP port 22
(SSH) for Linux VMs.
This outbound connection allows Bastion to communicate with the VMs inside Subnet1 without exposing them
to the public internet.

Question: 140 CertyIQ
DRAG DROP
-
You have the resources shown in the following table.
From the Microsoft Entra admin center, you register the Azure VPN application as an enterprise application.
You need to enable Microsoft Entra authentication for the P2S VPN connections. The solution must meet the
following requirements:
•Ensure that only the members of Group1 can establish VPN connections to VPNGW1.
•Ensure that only the members of Group2 can establish VPN connections to VPNGW2.
In which order should you perform the actions? To answer, move all actions from the list of actions to the answer
area and arrange them in the correct order.
Answer: 1>2>3>4
Explanation:
Step 1: Register Two Apps in Microsoft Entra ID
Register two applications (App1 and App2) in the Microsoft Entra admin center.
Assign Group1 to App1 and Group2 to App2.
In Microsoft Entra ID, applications need to be registered before they can be used for authentication.
Assigning groups ensures that only authorized users can access the specific apps.
Step 2: Add a Scope to App1 and App2
Define API permissions (scopes) for App1 and App2 in Microsoft Entra admin center.
Scopes define what resources an app can access.

This is crucial for controlling permissions and access levels for users.
Step 3: Add a Client App to App1 and App2
Register a client application for App1 and App2 in Microsoft Entra admin center.
A client app (e.g., a web or mobile application) must be associated with the registered API apps (App1 and
App2) to request access tokens.
This step is needed for applications to communicate securely.
Step 4: Configure the Point-to-Site VPN in Azure
In the Azure portal, configure Point-to-Site (P2S) VPN settings for VPNGW1 and VPNGW2.
Point-to-Site VPN allows users to securely connect to Azure Virtual Networks (VNets) from their devices.
VPNGW1 and VPNGW2 are likely VPN Gateways set up for different user groups or applications.
Question: 141 CertyIQ
DRAG DROP
-
You have an on-premises network.
You have an Azure subscription that contains a virtual network named VNet1. VNet1 contains an ExpressRoute
gateway named Gateway1.
You need to implement an ExpressRoute solution from a third-party provider named Fabrikam, Inc. The solution
must ensure that devices on the on-premises network can connect to the Azure resources on VNet1.
Which four actions should you perform in sequence? To answer, move the appropriate actions from the list of
actions to the answer area and arrange them in the correct order.

Answer:
Explanation:
Step 1: Create an ExpressRoute Circuit.
In the Azure portal, create a new ExpressRoute circuit.
An ExpressRoute circuit is required to establish private connectivity between Azure and an on-premises data
center.
This step generates a service key, which is needed by the service provider (in this case, Fabrikam).
Step 2: Send the Service Key to Fabrikam.
Provide the service key (generated in Step 1) to Fabrikam, the service provider.
The service provider (Fabrikam) needs this service key to provision and activate the ExpressRoute circuit.
Without this step, the circuit remains in a "Not Provisioned" state.
Step 3: Configure Azure Private Peering.
Once the circuit is active, configure Azure private peering.
Azure private peering allows private connectivity between on-premises networks and Azure Virtual Networks
(VNets).
This step ensures secure and high-performance communication between environments.
Step 4: Connect Gateway1 to the ExpressRoute Circuit.

Create or connect Gateway1 (a Virtual Network Gateway) to the ExpressRoute circuit.
An ExpressRoute gateway (Gateway1) enables a VNet to communicate with on-premises networks over
ExpressRoute.
Without this step, Azure resources cannot use the ExpressRoute circuit.

Question: 143 CertyIQ
You have the Azure subscriptions shown in the following table.

costs.
What is the minimum number of DDoS Network Protection plans you should deploy?
A.1
B.2
C.3
D.6
Answer: B
Explanation:
B. 2.
Each virtual network contains 20 internet-accessible resources with public IP addresses.

These resources need protection from DDoS attacks.
The solution must minimize costs.
Azure DDoS Network Protection plans are billed per protected public IP address, and the cost can be
optimized by sharing a single DDoS protection plan across multiple resources within the same virtual network.
Azure DDoS Network Protection plans can cover multiple public IP addresses within the same virtual network.
However, if there are multiple virtual networks, each virtual network typically requires its own DDoS
protection plan to ensure comprehensive coverage and isolation.

Question: 145 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains 100 network security groups (NSGs).
You need to ensure that you log the application of specific NSG rules.
Which type of log should you configure?
A.flow log
B.activity log
C.Azure resource log
D.audit log
Answer: C
Explanation:

The correct answer is C. Azure resource log. Here's a detailed justification:
To log the application of specific NSG rules, you need a log that captures details about the operations
performed on the NSG itself. Azure resource logs are designed to provide this level of detail, capturing
operational and diagnostic information for Azure resources, including network security groups. This includes
events related to NSG rule processing.
Azure resource logs provide insights into operations that were performed within an Azure resource. For NSGs,
this can include information about when rules were applied, modified, or deleted.
Flow logs capture information about IP traffic flowing through an NSG, which is useful for network traffic
analysis and security monitoring, but do not log the application of the rules themselves.
Activity logs track subscription-level events, such as creating or deleting an NSG, but they do not provide
granular details about the application of specific rules within the NSG.
Audit logs are generally considered to be part of the Activity Log in Azure and, therefore, do not offer the
granular rule application information required.
Therefore, to audit the successful or unsuccessful application of specific NSG rules to network traffic, Azure
resource logs are the appropriate choice.
Authoritative Links:
Azure Resource Logs: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/azure-monitor/essentials/resource-logs
Network Security Group Logging with Azure Network Watcher: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-
us/azure/network-watcher/network-watcher-logging-overview - While focused on Network Watcher, this
page touches upon NSG logging capabilities.
Question: 146 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT
-
You have an Azure subscription that contains a virtual machine named VM1 and a virtual network named Vnet1.
Vnet1 contains three subnets named Subnet1, Subnet2, and GatewaySubnet. VM1 is connected to Subnet1.
You plan to deploy a new virtual machine named VM2 that will perform network traffic routing and inspection.
You need to ensure that all the traffic from VM1 to the internet will be routed through VM2.
What should you do? To answer, select the appropriate options in the answer area.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
Answer: 1>2

Explanation:
1. Deploying VM2 to: Gateway Subnet.
Gateway Subnet is reserved for VPN Gateways and Azure Virtual Network Gateways, and VMs cannot be
deployed in this subnet.
However, if this is an exception scenario, Azure may allow placing a VM in GatewaySubnet for specific
network services, such as:
Network Virtual Appliances (NVA)
Custom VPN solutions
Firewall appliances
Diagnostics and monitoring VMs (for troubleshooting gateway traffic)
2. Associating the Custom Route Table with: Subnet1.
Custom route tables are used to control network traffic flow.
Associating a custom route table with Subnet1 allows:
Routing traffic through an NVA
Forcing traffic through a VPN Gateway
Directing internet-bound traffic to a firewall or proxy
This is common in hybrid network scenarios where Subnet1 needs custom routing rules.
Question: 147 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT
-
You have an on-premises network and an Azure virtual network named VNet1.
You need to implement Azure Extended Network. The solution must minimize costs.
Which type of virtual machine should you deploy to VNet1, and which tool should you use to configure Azure
Extended Network? To answer, select the appropriate options in the answer area.

NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
Answer: 2>4
Explanation:
1. Virtual Machine :Windows Server 2022 Datacenter: Azure Edition.
Windows Server 2022 Datacenter: Azure Edition is specifically designed for Azure-based workloads and
offers cloud-native capabilities such as:
Hotpatching (install security updates without requiring a reboot).
Azure Automanage for streamlined VM management.
Extended support for Azure hybrid services.
This edition is optimized for running in Azure environments rather than on-premises.
2.Tool : Windows Admin Center.
Windows Admin Center (WAC) is a browser-based management tool used to remotely manage Windows
Servers, including Azure-based servers.
It provides a modern GUI for managing server roles, networking, and security settings without requiring
Remote Desktop.
WAC is useful for hybrid cloud management and integrates natively with Azure services.

Question: 148 CertyIQ
Your company has a remote office that contains a macOS device named Device1. Device1 has an IKEv2 VPN client
installed.
You have an Azure subscription that contains the resources shown in the following table.
You need to ensure that Device1 can access the resources on VNet1 by using the VPN connections of VPNGW1. The
solution must minimize administrative effort.
What should you do first?
A.To VNet1, deploy a virtual machine that contains a RADIUS server.
B.On Device1, install the OpenVPN client.
C.On Device1, add an X.509 certificate.
D.From Devices in the Microsoft Entra admin center, configure the Device settings.
Answer: B
Explanation:
B. On Device1, install the OpenVPN client.
You need to ensure Device1 can access resources on VNet1 using the VPN connections of VPNGW1 while
minimizing administrative effort.
Azure Point-to-Site (P2S) VPN supports three authentication methods:
Azure Certificate Authentication

RADIUS Authentication
Microsoft Entra ID Authentication (Previously Azure AD authentication)
To connect to an Azure VPN Gateway (VPNGW1) from a client device (Device1), the user must install a VPN
client that is compatible with the VPN gateway configuration.
Azure VPN Gateway supports multiple VPN protocols:
OpenVPN (SSL-based, recommended for cross-platform)
IKEv2 (Windows native VPN client)
SSTP (for Windows-only connections)
Among these, OpenVPN is the most flexible and widely used, supporting Windows, macOS, Linux, iOS, and
Android.
Question: 149 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT
-
You have an Azure subscription that contains a virtual network named VNet1. VNet1 uses an IP address space of
192.168.0.0/24.
You plan to deploy Azure virtual machines and Azure Bastion to VNet1.
You need to recommend an IP subnetting configuration for VNet1. The solution must maximize the number of IP
addresses that can be assigned to the virtual machines.
What should you recommend? To answer, select the appropriate options in the answer area.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
Answer: 2>2
Explanation:
/25 and 182
Because the total address space is /24 which gives 265 IPs. This number should be used to address both of
the VM subnets and the Bastion subnet.

By default, Bastion subnets takes /26 which gives 64 IP address.
So the remaining IP addresses from the total address space is: 256-64 = 192 available IP address to be used
for VMs.
But to use this IP range, needs to be divided into to VM Subnets:
/25 (128 - 5 = 123)
/26 (64 - 5 = 59)
Based on the above the total number of the available IP address for VM subnets is: 182
In the question its asking for the "Bit mask for the LARGER virtual machine subnet" which is: /25
Question: 150 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT
-
You have an on-premises network that includes the sites shown in the following table.
Each site is connected to the Internet by a firewall. All sites are connected to an SD-WAN. Each site is configured
to propagate routes by using BGP.
You have an Azure subscription that includes a virtual network named Vnet1 that contains a Virtual Network
Gateway named Gateway1.
You create a local network gateway with the configuration shown in the gateway exhibit (Click the Gateway tab.)

You create a Site-to-Site (S2S) connection with the configuration shown in connection exhibit. (Click the
Connection tab.)

For each of the following statements, select Yes if the statement is true. Otherwise, select No.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.

Answer: No,yes,No.
Explanation:
Users in the Berlin site can connect to resources in Vnet1 via VPN1.
No: This statement is incorrect. Users in the Berlin site cannot connect to resources in Vnet1 via VPN1.
To create a direct Site-to-Site connection to the Berlin site, an additional Local Network Gateway is

necessary to enable users in the Paris site to connect to Vnet1.
Question: 151 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT
-
You have an on-premises server named Server that is assigned a public IP address of 131.107.100.200.
You have an Azure subscription that contains the resources shown in the following table.

storage85347 has the Networking settings configured as shown in the following exhibit.
From the Firewalls and virtual networks tab, you add Subnet1 to storage85347.
For each of the following statements, select Yes if the statement is true. Otherwise, select No.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
Answer:

Explanation:
1. A service endpoint is created on VNet1 : No.Service endpoints allow virtual networks to securely access
Azure services without requiring a public IP address. However, service endpoints are not created
automatically—they must be explicitly enabled on a subnet within a virtual network.Since the question does
not specify that a service endpoint was manually configured on VNet1, this statement is false.2. All the virtual
machines can access storage85347. :NoThe storage account storage85347 is not automatically accessible to
all virtual machines (VMs) in the VNet unless explicit access rules (such as a service endpoint, private
endpoint, or firewall rule) are configured.If no service endpoint, private endpoint, or explicit network access
rule is set, the VMs may not have direct access to the storage account.The default configuration does not
automatically grant access to all VMs.3. An additional route is created automatically in the route table of
Subnet1 : NoIn Azure Virtual Networks, when a subnet is created, Azure provides default system routes (e.g., a
route for the virtual network, internet, etc.).However, an additional route is not automatically created unless a
user manually defines a custom route table and associates it with Subnet1.If no explicit configuration is
mentioned, no extra route is automatically created.
Question: 152 CertyIQ
You have an on-premises datacenter in Seattle.
You have an Azure subscription that contains an Azure Network Watcher resource in the West US 2 Azure region.
You need to document network latency between the on-premises datacenter and the West US 2 region and
between the on-premises datacenter and the East US 2 public Azure region. The solution must minimize
administrative effort.
What should you do first?
A.Run the Get-AzNetworkWatcherConnectionMonitor cmdlet.
B.Run the Get-AzNetworkWatcherReachabilityProvidersList cmdlet.
C.Create a Network Watcher resource in the East US 2 region.
D.Create a Connection Monitor resource in the West US 2 region.
Answer: D
Explanation:
The correct answer is D. Create a Connection Monitor resource in the West US 2 region.
Here's a detailed justification:
The primary goal is to document network latency between the on-premises datacenter and both West US 2
and East US 2 Azure regions with minimal administrative effort. Azure Network Watcher's Connection Monitor

feature is designed precisely for this purpose.
Connection Monitor's Role: Connection Monitor proactively tests the connectivity and latency between
different endpoints. It periodically sends probes (ICMP, TCP, or HTTP) between the source (on-premises
datacenter, represented by an agent installed there) and destination (West US 2 or East US 2 Azure regions).
Why not A (Get-AzNetworkWatcherConnectionMonitor): This cmdlet retrieves information about an existing
connection monitor. You need to create one first.
Why not B (Get-AzNetworkWatcherReachabilityProvidersList): This cmdlet provides a list of network
providers and their routing paths to an Azure region. While useful for general understanding, it doesn't
directly measure latency between the on-premises datacenter and Azure regions in an ongoing and
documented manner.
Why not C (Create a Network Watcher resource in the East US 2 region): Network Watcher is automatically
enabled in each region in your subscription. Creating a new Network Watcher resource is redundant and not
necessary for creating connection monitors in that region. The default, enabled Network Watcher is sufficient.
You still need to create a Connection Monitor resource.
Connection Monitor Configuration: To measure latency, you need a Connection Monitor configured to
monitor the connectivity between the on-premises endpoint and endpoints within the West US 2 and East US
2 regions. This involves configuring the Connection Monitor with source endpoint(s) (representing the on-
premises datacenter) and destination endpoint(s) (representing resources in West US 2 and East US 2, such as
VMs or virtual network gateways).
Single Connection Monitor for Multiple Destinations: A single Connection Monitor in West US 2 can monitor
connectivity to both West US 2 and East US 2 destinations. It doesn't require one Connection Monitor per
Azure region.
Minimal Effort: By using Connection Monitor, you automate the process of measuring and documenting
latency. After initial setup, it continuously monitors the network and provides historical data.
Therefore, the first step is to create a Connection Monitor resource in the West US 2 region. Then, you would
configure it to monitor the connectivity between the on-premises datacenter and both the West US 2 and East
US 2 regions. This leverages Network Watcher's capabilities most efficiently to achieve the stated goal.
Authoritative Links:
Azure Network Watcher: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/network-watcher/network-watcher-
monitoring-overview
Connection Monitor: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/network-watcher/connection-
monitor/connection-monitor-overview
Question: 153 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT
-
You have an Azure subscription that contains the virtual networks shown in the Virtual networks exhibit.

You create an Azure DNS Private Resolver as shown in the DNS private resolvers exhibit.

DNS-resolver-inbound-endpoint-1 is configured as shown in the Inbound endpoints exhibit.

DNS-resolver-rule-1 is configured as shown in the Rules exhibit.
For each of the following statements, select Yes if the statement is true. Otherwise, select No.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
Answer:
Explanation:
VNet1 can be linked to DNS-resolver-ruleset-1.

No
you believe or know that VNet1 cannot be linked to "DNS-resolver-ruleset-1." This could be due to technical
limitations in your system, a lack of configuration options, or a design choice.
VNet2 must be configured to use 10.3.1.4 as the preferred DNS server.
No
Means you believe that VNet2 is not required to use 10.3.1.4 as its preferred DNS server. Perhaps it uses a
different DNS server, obtains DNS server information dynamically, or has no specific requirement for this
particular IP address.
Virtual machines connected to VNet3 can resolve names in the MargiesTravel.com domain.
Yes
you believe or know that the DNS configuration for VNet3 (or the wider network infrastructure it relies on) is
set up correctly to allow name resolution for the "MargiesTravel.com" domain. This could involve VNet3 being
configured to use DNS servers that are authoritative for or can forward queries to the DNS servers
responsible for "MargiesTravel.com."
Question: 154 CertyIQ
Case Study -
This is a case study. Case studies are not timed separately. You can use as much exam time as you would like to
complete each case. However, there may be additional case studies and sections on this exam. You must manage
your time to ensure that you are able to complete all questions included on this exam in the time provided.
To answer the questions included in a case study, you will need to reference information that is provided in the
case study. Case studies might contain exhibits and other resources that provide more information about the
scenario that is described in the case study. Each question is independent of the other questions in this case study.
At the end of this case study, a review screen will appear. This screen allows you to review your answers and to
make changes before you move to the next section of the exam. After you begin a new section, you cannot return
to this section.
To start the case study -
To display the first question in this case study, click the Next button. Use the buttons in the left pane to explore
the content of the case study before you answer the questions. Clicking these buttons displays information such
as business requirements, existing environment, and problem statements. When you are ready to answer a
question, click the Question button to return to the question.
Overview -
Proseware, Inc. is a financial services company that has a main office in New York City and a branch office in San
Francisco.
Existing Environment. Hybrid Environment
Proseware has an on-premises Active Directory Domain Services (AD DS) forest named corp.proseware.com that
syncs with a Microsoft Entra tenant named proseware.com.
Proseware has an Azure subscription that is linked to proseware.com.
Proseware has an internal certification authority (CA).
Existing Environment. Network Infrastructure
The offices contain the resources shown in the following table.

NYCNet connects to Azure by using an ExpressRoute circuit.
SFONet connects to Azure by using a Site-to-Site (S2S) VPN.
Existing Environment. Azure Resources
The Azure subscription contains the virtual networks and subnets shown in the following table.
The subscription contains four virtual machines named VM1, VM2, VM3, and VM4. VM1 and VM2 host an app named
App1.
VM3 and VM4 host a web app named App2 that is accessed by using a FQDN of app2.proseware.com. Users
access app2.proseware.com by using HTTP or HTTPS.
VM1, VM2, and VM4 are connected to SpokeVNet.
The subscription contains Application Gateway resources shown in the following table.

The subscription contains an Azure Front Door Standard profile named FD1. FD1 contains a single origin group that
targets APPGW1 by using the default endpoint name.
HubVNet connects to NYCNet by using an ExpressRoute gateway named ERGW1.
Planned Changes and Requirements. Planned Changes
Proseware plans to implement the following changes:
•Deploy an Azure Private DNS Resolver named PRDNS1 to HubVNet and link PRDNS1 to SpokeVNet.
•Create a DNS forwarding ruleset named DNSRS1 and associate DNSRS1 with PRDNS1.
•Deploy Azure Virtual Network Manager and implement the following rules:
- Allow inbound connections on TCP port 3389 from the on-premises networks to SUBNET-JUMPHOSTS.
- Block inbound connections on TCP port 80 from the internet to SpokeVNet.
•Ensure that Azure Virtual Network Manager rules take precedence over conflicting NSG rules.
•Deploy two network virtual appliances (NVAs) named NVA1 and NVA2 to HubVNet.
•Deploy a gateway load balancer named LBGW1 to HubVNet.
•Configure LBGW1 to inspect traffic on TCP ports 443, 1433, and 1434 from LBS1 by using NVA1 and NVA2.
•Ensure that all the traffic to App2 is processed by using FD1.
Planned Changes and Requirements. Connectivity requirements
Proseware identifies the following connectivity requirements:
•Minimize the complexity of the Azure Virtual Network Manager deployment.
•Route traffic between NYCNet and SFONet via the ExpressRoute circuit and the S2S VPN.
•Ensure that remote users on Windows 11 devices can connect to HubVNet by using a Point-to-Site (P2S) VPN and
their proseware.com credentials.
Planned Changes and Requirements. Security requirements
Proseware identifies the following security requirements:
•Whenever possible, use the internal CA.
•Ensure that all connections routed via APPGW1 use end-to-end encryption.
•Ensure that user connections to Azure-hosted apps use end-to-end encryption.
•Ensure that all inbound internet traffic to app2.proseware.com is routed via FD1.
•Prevent devices that connect to NYCNet from accessing Azure services that use private endpoints.
•Enable the virtual machines that connect to HubVNet and SpokeVNet to access Azure services that use private
endpoints.
Planned Changes and Requirements. General requirements
Proseware identifies the following general requirements:
•Minimize the IP address space required to deploy platform-managed resources to the virtual networks.
•From SpokeVNet, resolve name resolution requests for the azure.proseware.com namespace and the
corp.proseware.com namespace by using PRDNS1.
•Whenever possible, minimize administrative effort.
You need to configure APPGW1 to support end-to-end encryption. The solution must meet the security
requirements.
What should you do?
A.From the SSL settings, upload a TLS client certificate that is issued by the internal root CA and includes the
full certificate chain.
B.From the Backend settings, upload the internal root CA certificate.
C.From the SSL settings, upload a TLS client certificate that is issued by the internal root CA.
D.From the Backend settings, upload a wildcard TLS certificate that has a private key issued by the internal
root CA.
Answer: B

Explanation:
B. From the Backend settings, upload the internal root CA certificate.
To enable end-to-end TLS encryption on Azure Application Gateway, the gateway must be able to trust the
backend server certificates. Since the backend servers use certificates issued by an internal root CA,
Application Gateway must be configured to trust that CA.
Question: 155 CertyIQ
Answer:
You are configuring the DNS forwarding ruleset for DNSRS1.
You need to configure the destination IP address for azure.proseware.com and for corp.proseware.com. The
solution must meet the general requirements.
Which IP addresses should you configure for each namespace? To answer, select the appropriate options in
the answer area.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
Correct Answer:
azure.proseware.com ➔ 168.63.129.16:
This is the Azure-provided DNS IP, automatically available to Azure VMs. It's used for DNS resolution when
VMs are set to use Azure DNS. For any Azure-hosted domain like azure.proseware.com, you rely on this
standard Azure DNS.
corp.proseware.com ➔ First IP address of the inbound endpoint subnet of PRDNS1:
corp.proseware.com is a private domain, so internal Azure workloads need a Private DNS Resolver's inbound
endpoint to resolve it. The inbound endpoint accepts queries from VMs that need private DNS lookups and
forwards them internally.

The subscription contains four virtual machines named VM1, VM2, VM3, and VM4. VM1 and VM2 host an app named
App1.
VM3 and VM4 host a web app named App2 that is accessed by using a FQDN of app2.proseware.com. Users
access app2.proseware.com by using HTTP or HTTPS.
VM1, VM2, and VM4 are connected to SpokeVNet.
The subscription contains Application Gateway resources shown in the following table.
The subscription contains an Azure Front Door Standard profile named FD1. FD1 contains a single origin group that
targets APPGW1 by using the default endpoint name.
HubVNet connects to NYCNet by using an ExpressRoute gateway named ERGW1.
Planned Changes and Requirements. Planned Changes
Proseware plans to implement the following changes:
•Deploy an Azure Private DNS Resolver named PRDNS1 to HubVNet and link PRDNS1 to SpokeVNet.
•Create a DNS forwarding ruleset named DNSRS1 and associate DNSRS1 with PRDNS1.
•Deploy Azure Virtual Network Manager and implement the following rules:
- Allow inbound connections on TCP port 3389 from the on-premises networks to SUBNET-JUMPHOSTS.
- Block inbound connections on TCP port 80 from the internet to SpokeVNet.
•Ensure that Azure Virtual Network Manager rules take precedence over conflicting NSG rules.
•Deploy two network virtual appliances (NVAs) named NVA1 and NVA2 to HubVNet.
•Deploy a gateway load balancer named LBGW1 to HubVNet.
•Configure LBGW1 to inspect traffic on TCP ports 443, 1433, and 1434 from LBS1 by using NVA1 and NVA2.
•Ensure that all the traffic to App2 is processed by using FD1.
Planned Changes and Requirements. Connectivity requirements
Proseware identifies the following connectivity requirements:

•Minimize the complexity of the Azure Virtual Network Manager deployment.
•Route traffic between NYCNet and SFONet via the ExpressRoute circuit and the S2S VPN.
•Ensure that remote users on Windows 11 devices can connect to HubVNet by using a Point-to-Site (P2S) VPN and
their proseware.com credentials.
Planned Changes and Requirements. Security requirements
Proseware identifies the following security requirements:
•Whenever possible, use the internal CA.
•Ensure that all connections routed via APPGW1 use end-to-end encryption.
•Ensure that user connections to Azure-hosted apps use end-to-end encryption.
•Ensure that all inbound internet traffic to app2.proseware.com is routed via FD1.
•Prevent devices that connect to NYCNet from accessing Azure services that use private endpoints.
•Enable the virtual machines that connect to HubVNet and SpokeVNet to access Azure services that use private
endpoints.
Planned Changes and Requirements. General requirements

•Minimize the IP address space required to deploy platform-managed resources to the virtual networks.

corp.proseware.com namespace by using PRDNS1.
•Whenever possible, minimize administrative effort.

SpokeVNet. The solution must meet the general requirements.
What should you identify for each virtual network? To answer, select the appropriate options in the answer area.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
Answer:

Explanation:
HubVNet (Hub Virtual Network):/28.
This is usually the central network in a hub-and-spoke topology.
It typically contains shared services such as firewalls, VPN gateways, or ExpressRoute connections.
The selection /28 means the smallest subnet size (14 usable IPs), which might be used for small-scale
deployments.
SpokeVNet (Spoke Virtual Network): No address Space required.
This is a connected VNet that usually contains application workloads.
It connects to the HubVNet for centralized management or shared resources.
The selection "No address space required" means that this spoke VNet is either:
Not needed in the current deployment, or
Using existing address space from a previously configured setup.
Question: 158 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains a virtual machine named VM1, a virtual network interface card (NIC)
named NIC1, and a Basic SKU public IP address named IP1. NIC1 is attached to VM1. IP1 is associated to NIC1.
You need to upgrade IP1 to the Standard SKU.
What should you do first?

A. Create a new NIC for VMI.
B. Disassociate IP1 from NIC1.
C. Detach NIC1 from VM1.
D. Stop vM1.
Answer: B
Explanation:
The correct first step is to disassociate the Basic SKU public IP address (IP1) from the network interface card
(NIC1). Here's why:
Upgrading a public IP address from Basic to Standard SKU requires the IP address to be disassociated from
all resources first. Azure does not allow a direct in-place upgrade while the IP address is actively associated.
The Basic SKU and Standard SKU of public IP addresses have different capabilities and resource allocation
mechanisms, so a clean dissociation is needed for a seamless upgrade.
Detaching NIC1 from VM1 (option C) or creating a new NIC (option A) are unnecessary steps at this stage.
These actions introduce unnecessary complexity and potential downtime, especially since the objective is
solely to upgrade the IP address. These actions also carry the potential to change the IP address
configuration, which is contrary to the requirements.
Stopping VM1 (option D) might seem logical since changes are being made, but it is not the immediate
prerequisite. You can disassociate the IP address while the VM is running. Stopping the VM will lead to
unnecessary downtime. The key action before any changes to the IP address SKU can be made is to ensure
that it's no longer actively linked to the NIC. After disassociation, the upgrade can proceed, and then the
upgraded IP can be re-associated with the NIC.
Therefore, disassociating the IP from the NIC allows the upgrade process to begin by freeing up the IP
address for the change.
Public IP addresses - Azure | Microsoft Learn: This document details the different SKUs for public IP
addresses and how they are used.
Associate a public IP address to a virtual machine - Azure Virtual Machines | Microsoft Learn: This resource
explains how to manage public IP addresses associated with virtual machines.

Question: 160 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT
-
You have an Azure subscription that contains virtual networks, network security groups (NSGs), load balancer,
virtual machines, and virtual network gateways.
You enable Azure Monitor Network Insights.
You need to perform the following actions:
•Review the NSG flow logs.
•Monitor resource utilization.
•Review the results of IP flow verify testing.
Which Azure Monitor Network Insights feature should you use for each action? To answer, select the appropriate
options in the answer area.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.

Answer:

Explanation:
Review the NSG flow logs: Traffic
“Traffic” (Traffic Analytics / NSG flow logs) is the Network Watcher capability that collects and surfaces NSG
flow logs. It shows per-flow telemetry (src/dest IP, ports, bytes/packets, action allowed/denied, rule that
matched) and can produce aggregated analytics and charts.
Monitor resource utilization: Topology
The Topology tool produces a visual map of network resources and their relationships (VNets, subnets, NICs,
load balancers, gateways, VMs). It’s the quickest way to inspect where resources are deployed, how they
connect, and to visually spot overloaded/incorrect placements or unexpected links.
(Note: for detailed numerics like CPU/memory, throughput metrics, use Azure Monitor/Network Insights.
Topology gives structural/placement visibility and is commonly used to assess how resources are distributed
and where you should monitor utilization more deeply.)
Review the results of IP flow verify testing: Diagnostic Toolkit
IP flow verify (Test-IP-Flow) is part of Network Watcher’s diagnostic toolset. It tests whether traffic is allowed
or denied to/from a VM by evaluating effective NSG rules and returns the exact allow/deny result and the rule
that caused it. The Diagnostic Toolkit is the portal area where you run IP flow verify and similar tests.

Question: 161 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT
-
You have an Azure subscription that contains 20 virtual machines and a virtual network named VNet1.
You plan to provide access to the virtual machines by using Azure Bastion.
You need to configure a subnet for Azure Bastion. The solution must minimize the number of IP addresses required
for the subnet.
How should you configure the subnet? To answer, select the appropriate options in the answer area.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
Answer:

Explanation:
Subnet mask: /26 Name: AzureBastionSubnet 1. Subnet size requirement for Azure Bastion Azure Bastion
requires its own dedicated subnet. The minimum subnet size supported for Azure Bastion is /26 (which
provides 64 IP addresses). Smaller subnets (like /28 or /30) are not supported. Larger subnets (/24) would
work, but the question says: "The solution must minimize the number of IP addresses required for the subnet."
So, the smallest valid choice is /26. 2. Subnet name requirement The subnet must be named exactly
AzureBastionSubnet. If you use any other name (like BastionSubnet, Azure_Bastion_Subnet, etc.), deployment
will fail because Azure checks for this reserved name.

Answer:
Explanation:
IPv4: /24 —
While Azure supports IPv4 subnets from /8 to /29, a /24 is the standard, most common size used for general-

purpose subnets in exam scenarios. Unless the question specifies a requirement for a specialized service (like
Azure Bastion which requires /26), /24 is the logical choice.
IPv6: /64 —
In Azure Virtual Networks, the IPv6 subnet size is not flexible. Every IPv6 subnet must be exactly /64 in size.
This is a strict requirement for Azure's implementation of dual-stack networking.
Question: 163 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT
-
You have two Azure subscriptions named Sub1 and Sub2 that contain the resources shown in the following table.
You need to ensure that App1 can access Resource1 by using the Azure Private Link service.
Which should you deploy to each virtual network to meet the prerequisites of the Private Link service? To answer,
select the appropriate options in the answer area.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
Answer:

Explanation:
Vnet1: A load balancer .
In a Private Link Service scenario, the Service Provider (Vnet1) must host the application behind a Standard
Load Balancer. The Private Link Service is then associated with the frontend IP of this load balancer to
"advertise" the service.
Vnet2: A private endpoint .
The Service Consumer (Vnet2) accesses the service privately by creating a Private Endpoint. This endpoint
acts as a local network interface (NIC) with a private IP address from Vnet2’s address space, mapping it
directly to the service in Vnet1.
Why this is the solution
This specific architecture is used to share services across different virtual networks, subscriptions, or even
Entra ID (Azure AD) tenants without requiring VNet Peering.
Vnet1 (Provider side): Needs a Standard Load Balancer to distribute traffic to the backend pool of VMs or
services.
Vnet2 (Consumer side): Needs a Private Endpoint to receive a private IP that points to the provider's service.
Quick Comparison of the Distractors
Service Endpoint: This is used to secure a specific Azure service (like Storage or SQL) to a subnet. It doesn't
use a Load Balancer in the destination VNet.
Virtual Network Gateway: This is used for VPN or ExpressRoute connections between networks, which is an
"all-or-nothing" routing approach rather than a specific service-level connection.
Would you like to see how Private DNS Zones fit into this specific setup to ensure the consumer can resolve
the service's name?

Question: 164 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT
-
You have two on-premises sites named Site1 and Site2. Each site connects to the internet by using a local firewall
device.
You have an Azure subscription that contains two virtual networks named VNet1 and VNet2. VNet1 and VNet2
reside in the East US Azure region and are connected by using virtual network peering.
You need to configure a Site-to-Site (S2S) VPN solution that meets the following requirements:
•Site1 and Site2 must connect directly to the Azure subscription by using their local firewall device.
•The computers in Site1 and Site2 must be able to connect to the resources on VNet1 and VNet2.
•The solution must include a virtual network gateway that uses three availability zones.
What is the minimum number of virtual network gateways and local network gateways that you should deploy to
Azure? To answer, select the appropriate options in the answer area.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
Answer:

Explanation:
1. Virtual Network Gateways: 1.
The Azure Side: A Virtual Network Gateway (VNG) is the resource that sits inside your Azure VNet (in the
GatewaySubnet).
The Rule: A single Azure Virtual Network supports only one VPN gateway.
Multi-Site Capability: Even if you are connecting to multiple different on-premises locations, you still only use
that one gateway. A single Route-based VPN gateway can support multiple simultaneous Site-to-Site (S2S)
tunnels (this is known as a Multi-Site VPN configuration).
2. Local Network Gateways: 2
The On-Premises Side: A Local Network Gateway (LNG) is a configuration object in Azure that represents your
on-premises environment.
What it stores: It holds the Public IP address of the on-premises VPN device and the Local Address Space (IP
ranges) of that specific site.
The Requirement: Because you have two distinct on-premises networks (Network1 and Network2), they each
have their own unique public IP and their own unique internal IP ranges. Therefore, you must create one Local
Network Gateway for each network so Azure knows where to send traffic for each specific site.

Question: 167 CertyIQ
You are planning the IP addressing for the subnets in Azure virtual networks.
Which type of resource requires IP addresses in the subnets?

A. private endpoints
B. virtual network peering
C. Azure DDoS Protection
D. storage account
Answer: A
Explanation:
Technical Justification for Correct Answer: A (Private Endpoints)
The correct answer is A. Private Endpoints because private endpoints are IP-bound resources that are
provisioned within an Azure Virtual Network (VNet) subnet. Each private endpoint is assigned one or more
private IP addresses from the subnet's IP address space. This integration allows for secure, private access to
Azure services (like Storage, Cosmos DB, etc.) without exposing them to the public internet. The private IP
address ensures the endpoint is reachable from within the VNet, aligning with the requirement for IP
addresses in the subnets.
Why Other Options are Less Suitable:
B. Virtual Network Peering: While Virtual Network Peering allows for communication between subnets in
different VNs, it does not require IP addresses to be allocated specifically for the peering itself within the
subnets. Peering utilizes the existing IP infrastructure of the subnets. Thus, it doesn't directly necessitate new
IP assignments in the subnets for its operation.
C. Azure DDoS Protection: Azure DDoS Protection is a security service that protects against distributed
denial-of-service attacks. It operates at the network traffic level and does not require dedicated IP addresses
within subnets for its functionality. Instead, it protects IP addresses and traffic flows within the VNet.
D. Storage Account: Storage Accounts themselves do not require IP addresses within a VNet's subnets
unless accessed via a Private Endpoint (which brings us back to option A). By default, Storage Accounts are
accessed via their public endpoints. If a Storage Account is accessed via a Private Endpoint, then the Private
Endpoint (not the Storage Account directly) would require an IP address in the subnet.
References
Microsoft Documentation: Private Endpoint for Azure
Microsoft Documentation: Plan and design Azure VNet subnets

Question: 175 CertyIQ
You have an Azure application gateway named AGW1 that has a routing rule named Rule1. Rule 1 directs traffic for
http://www.contoso.com to a backend pool named Pool1. Pool1 targets an Azure virtual machine scale set named
VMSS1.
You deploy another virtual machine scale set named VMSS2.
You need to configure AGW1 to direct all traffic for http://www.adatum.com to VMSS2.
The solution must ensure that requests to http://www.contoso.com continue to be directed to Pool1.
Which three actions should you perform? Each correct answer presents part of the solution.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
A. Add a backend pool.
B. Modify an HTTP setting.
C. Add an HTTP setting.
D. Add a listener.
E. Add a rule.

Answer: ADE
Explanation:
The correct answer is ADE. Here's a detailed justification:
A. Add a backend pool: We need a separate backend pool for VMSS2 because the existing Pool1 is configured
for VMSS1. A backend pool is a collection of backend servers (in this case, VMSS2 instances) that the
application gateway will route traffic to. Each pool represents a distinct target for the application gateway's
routing rules.
D. Add a listener: A listener listens for incoming requests on specific ports, protocols, and hostnames. The
existing listener is configured to listen for requests to http://www.contoso.com. To handle requests to
http://www.adatum.com, we require a new listener configured to listen specifically for traffic destined for that
hostname. Without a new listener, the application gateway wouldn't be able to differentiate between the two
domains.
E. Add a rule: A rule defines how the application gateway routes traffic based on the listener. We need a new
rule that associates the new listener for http://www.adatum.com with the new backend pool (containing
VMSS2). This rule will direct all traffic received by the new listener to the backend pool containing VMSS2.
The original rule for http://www.contoso.com and Pool1 remains unaffected.
Why B and C are incorrect:
B. Modify an HTTP setting: HTTP settings define parameters like port, protocol, and cookie-based affinity for
backend servers. Modifying the existing HTTP setting could potentially disrupt the original configuration for
http://www.contoso.com, which we want to avoid. Instead, we use the existing or create another setting as
needed. HTTP settings are usually reusable between rules and listeners.
C. Add an HTTP setting: A new HTTP Setting is not strictly required. The current HTTP setting associated to
Pool1 can be used if the backend servers in VMSS2 need to be accessed the same way as VMSS1. Creating
new Setting is only required if your backends need different protocol, ports or timeouts.
In summary, creating a new backend pool and adding a new listener and routing rule will enable the
application gateway to route traffic to VMSS2 based on the http://www.adatum.com hostname, while
preserving the existing routing for http://www.contoso.com to VMSS1.
Authoritative Links:
Azure Application Gateway Listeners: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/application-
gateway/configuration-listeners
Azure Application Gateway Backend Pools: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/application-
gateway/configuration-infrastructure#backend-pools
Azure Application Gateway Routing Rules: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/application-
gateway/configuration-http-settings
Question: 176 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT -
You have an Azure Traffic Manager parent profile named TM1. TM1 has two child profiles named TM2 and TM3.
TM1 uses the performance traffic-routing method and has the endpoints shown in the following table.

TM2 uses the weighted traffic-routing method with MinChildEndpoint = 2 and has the endpoints shown in the
following table.
TM3 uses priority traffic-routing method and has the endpoints shown in the following table.
The App2, App4, and App6 endpoints have a degraded monitoring status.
To which endpoint is traffic directed? To answer, select the appropriate options in the answer area.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point
Hot Area:

Answer:

Explanation:
Traffic from West Europe:
Basedd on TM1 table, West Europe will trigger TM2. However, as the MinChildEndpoint is set to 2, and App4 is
degraded (down), the entire TM2 will not be considered available.
This goes back to the origin TM1 that uses performance traffic-routing method, which means the closest
location is App1 and naturally be the next best performance instance.
Hence, Answer = App1
Traffic from West US:
Based on TM1 table, West US will trigger TM3. However, both App2 and App6 were degraded (down), so none
of them can be considered.
This goes back to the original TM1 that uses performance traffic-routing method, from TM1, the other 2 US
locations would be App2 and App3. But App2 we know it's already degraded (unavailable), hence the only
option would be App3.
Answer = App3

Question: 177 CertyIQ
Note: This question is part of a series of questions that present the same scenario. Each question in the series
contains a unique solution that might meet the stated goals. Some question sets might have more than one correct
solution, while others might not have a correct solution.
After you answer a question in this section, you will NOT be able to return to it. As a result, these questions will not
appear in the review screen.
You have an Azure application gateway that has Azure Web Application Firewall (WAF) enabled.

Question: 180 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains an Azure App Service app. The app uses a URL of
https://www.contoso.com.
You need to use a custom domain on Azure Front Door for www.contoso.com. The custom domain must use a
certificate from an allowed certification authority
(CA).
What should you include in the solution?

A.an enterprise application in Azure Active Directory (Azure AD)
B.Active Directory Certificate Services (AD CS)
C.Azure Key Vault
D.Azure Application Gateway
Answer: C
Explanation:
The correct answer is C: Azure Key Vault.
Azure Front Door requires a certificate to secure custom domains over HTTPS. This certificate needs to be
issued by an allowed Certification Authority (CA). Azure Key Vault provides a secure and centralized storage
for managing secrets, keys, and certificates, including SSL/TLS certificates used for HTTPS. Instead of
manually uploading and managing certificates, Azure Front Door can securely access and deploy certificates
directly from Azure Key Vault. This eliminates the need to handle the certificate files manually, simplifying the
process and improving security by enforcing access control and auditing.
Option A, an enterprise application in Azure Active Directory (Azure AD), is used for managing user identities
and access to applications, but not for storing and managing SSL/TLS certificates. Option B, Active Directory
Certificate Services (AD CS), is an on-premises service for issuing and managing certificates, but Azure Front
Door expects the certificate to be stored and managed in a centralized cloud-based service like Key Vault.
Option D, Azure Application Gateway, is a web traffic load balancer and application delivery controller, which
can also manage SSL/TLS certificates. However, the best practice for Azure Front Door is to integrate with
Azure Key Vault.
Key Vault provides role-based access control, so only authorized users or services (like Front Door) can access
the certificates. Also, Key Vault supports automatic certificate renewal, which simplifies the management of
certificate lifecycles and helps prevent service disruptions due to expired certificates. Furthermore, Key Vault
is integrated with Azure Monitor, allowing auditing and logging of certificate access and usage.
References:
Azure Front Door custom domain HTTPS
Azure Key Vault
Question: 181 CertyIQ
You have an Azure application gateway for a web app named App1. The application gateway allows end-to-end
encryption.
You configure the listener for HTTPS by uploading an enterprise-signed certificate.
You need to ensure that the application gateway can provide end-to-end encryption for App1.
What should you do?
A. Increase the Unhealthy threshold setting in the custom probe.
B. Enable the SSL profile to the listener.
C. Set Listener type to Multi site.
D. Upload the public key certificate to the HTTP settings.
Answer: D
Explanation:

The correct answer is D. Upload the public key certificate to the HTTP settings.
Here's why:
To achieve end-to-end encryption with Azure Application Gateway, data must be encrypted from the client to
the Application Gateway and from the Application Gateway to the backend servers hosting App1. The
enterprise-signed certificate uploaded to the HTTPS listener handles the encryption between the client and
the Application Gateway.
However, for the Application Gateway to encrypt the traffic to the backend servers, it needs to trust the
certificates presented by those servers. If the backend servers use self-signed certificates or certificates
signed by a private Certificate Authority (CA), the Application Gateway won't inherently trust them.
The Application Gateway trusts certificates signed by well-known public CAs out of the box. But when you
use an enterprise-signed certificate (likely from your organization's internal CA) on the backend servers, the
Application Gateway requires the public key certificate (the root or intermediate certificate of your internal
CA) to be uploaded to the HTTP settings of the Application Gateway. This tells the Application Gateway that
certificates signed by that CA are trusted. By uploading the public certificate, you are essentially providing
the Application Gateway with the means to validate the backend server's certificate. Without this, the
Application Gateway may consider the connection to the backend servers as insecure and refuse to forward
the traffic, breaking the end-to-end encryption.
Options A, B, and C are not relevant to establishing end-to-end encryption trust. The Unhealthy threshold in a
custom probe relates to the health checking of backend servers, not certificate validation. SSL profiles
manage cipher suites and SSL/TLS versions, and Listener type being set to Multi-site affects how an
Application Gateway routes incoming traffic based on hostnames, neither of which relates to the backend
server's certificate trust.
Therefore, uploading the public key certificate to the HTTP settings establishes the necessary trust for the
Application Gateway to complete the end-to-end encryption process, allowing it to securely forward
encrypted traffic to the backend servers hosting App1.
Authoritative Links:
End-to-end encryption with Application Gateway: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/application-
gateway/end-to-end-ssl-encryption
Configure SSL settings: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/application-gateway/ssl-overview

1) NAT gateway can provide outbound connectivity for virtual machines from other availability zones different

While virtual machines in subnets from different availability zones can all be configured to a single zonal NAT
gateway resource, this configuration doesn't provide the most effective method for ensuring zone-resiliency
against zonal outages.

exhibit 2 it shows that Subnet 1 is associated with that Nat Gateway.

Question: 183 CertyIQ
You have an Azure application gateway named AppGW1 that balances requests to a web app named App1.
You need to modify the server variables in the response header of App1.
What should you configure on AppGW1?

A. HTTP settings
B. rewrites
C. rules
D. listeners
Answer: B
Explanation:
The correct answer is B. rewrites.
Rewrites within Azure Application Gateway allow modification of HTTP request and response headers, URL,
and query string parameters. Modifying the server variables in the response header of App1 directly
addresses the requirement. HTTP settings define the backend protocol, port, and other settings used to
connect to the backend servers. Rules define how traffic is routed to the backend pools based on different
conditions, while listeners determine how Application Gateway listens for incoming traffic, based on
protocols, ports, and hostnames. Neither HTTP settings, rules, nor listeners directly support modifying
response headers in the way rewrites do. Rewrites are specifically designed for manipulating HTTP request
and response elements, making it the appropriate solution.
For example, a rewrite rule can be created to replace the default "Server: Microsoft-IIS/10.0" header with a
custom value for security hardening or information masking. This level of granular control over response
headers is precisely what's needed to change server variables in the App1 response. Therefore, the correct
component to configure on AppGW1 is a rewrite rule.
Further reading can be found here:
Azure Application Gateway Rewrite Overview
Configure HTTP settings in Application Gateway
Application Gateway Listener Configuration
Application Gateway request routing rules
Question: 184 CertyIQ
You have an Azure Virtual Desktop deployment that has 500 session hosts.
All outbound traffic to the internet uses a NAT gateway.
During peak business hours, some users report that they cannot access internet resources. In Azure Monitor, you
discover many failed SNAT connections.
You need to increase the available SNAT connections.
What should you do?
A.Bind the NAT gateway to another subnet.
B.Add a public IP address.
C.Deploy Azure Standard Load Balancer that has outbound rules.
Answer: B
Explanation:
The correct answer is B: Add a public IP address to the NAT gateway. The problem indicates SNAT exhaustion,
meaning the NAT gateway is running out of available ports to translate internal IP addresses to its public IP
for outbound internet access. NAT gateways have a limited number of SNAT ports per public IP address
assigned to them. By default, a NAT gateway provides 64,000 SNAT ports per public IP address. When 500

session hosts share a single public IP, they consume SNAT ports more rapidly, especially during peak hours.
Adding another public IP address to the NAT gateway directly increases the total number of available SNAT
ports. The additional SNAT ports alleviates the exhaustion issue, allowing more concurrent outbound
connections from the session hosts. Each added public IP address provides an additional 64,000 SNAT ports.
This increase in available SNAT ports reduces the likelihood of users experiencing connection failures.
Option A, binding the NAT gateway to another subnet, does not directly address SNAT exhaustion. While it
might indirectly affect traffic patterns, it doesn't increase the number of available SNAT ports. The subnet's
size or configuration does not influence the number of SNAT ports offered by the NAT gateway.
Option C, deploying Azure Standard Load Balancer with outbound rules, introduces additional complexity and
may not be the most efficient solution. While a load balancer can provide outbound NAT, using it solely for
this purpose is often overkill, and managing outbound rules adds overhead. It also requires additional
configuration and might incur higher costs than simply adding a public IP to the existing NAT gateway.
Furthermore, migrating the entire outbound traffic management to a new service is an unnecessary
disruption. It is better to augment the existing infrastructure if possible.
Therefore, adding a public IP address to the NAT gateway is the most straightforward and effective solution
to increase available SNAT connections and resolve the SNAT exhaustion issue.
For further research, refer to the official Microsoft Azure documentation on NAT Gateway:
What is Azure NAT Gateway?
NAT Gateway resource

be used. If the load balancer is of type Public, it needs a public IP address that meets the SKU requirement.

Question: 187 CertyIQ
You are configuring two network virtual appliances (NVAs) in an Azure virtual network. The NVAs will be used to
inspect all the traffic within the virtual network.
You need to provide high availability for the NVAs. The solution must minimize administrative effort.
What should you include in the solution?
A. Azure Standard Load Balancer
B. Azure Application Gateway
C. Azure Traffic Manager
D. Azure Front Door
Answer: A
Explanation:
The correct answer is A, Azure Standard Load Balancer, because it provides the necessary features for high
availability and traffic distribution across the NVAs within the same virtual network.
Here's why:
High Availability: An Azure Standard Load Balancer can distribute traffic to multiple NVAs, ensuring that if
one NVA fails, the others can continue processing traffic. This is crucial for maintaining network inspection
capabilities without interruption.
Traffic Distribution: The Standard Load Balancer can distribute traffic based on source IP, destination IP, and
port (using a hash-based distribution algorithm or custom health probes). This ensures that traffic is evenly
distributed across the healthy NVAs, preventing overload.
Internal Load Balancing: For inspection of traffic within the virtual network, an internal (private) Standard
Load Balancer is appropriate. This allows it to reside within the virtual network itself, inspecting East-West
traffic.
Health Probes: Standard Load Balancers use health probes to monitor the health of the backend NVAs. If an
NVA becomes unhealthy, the load balancer automatically stops sending traffic to it.
Minimizes Administrative Effort: Azure Load Balancers are a managed service, which significantly reduces
the administrative overhead compared to manually managing a load balancing solution. The configuration is
relatively straightforward through the Azure portal or Azure CLI/PowerShell.
Not Application Gateway: Application Gateway is primarily designed for web application traffic
(HTTP/HTTPS) and is not suitable for generic network traffic inspection.
Not Traffic Manager or Front Door: Traffic Manager and Front Door are global load balancers, designed to
distribute traffic across different Azure regions or even to on-premises environments. They are not suitable
for load balancing NVAs within a single virtual network.
In summary, Azure Standard Load Balancer provides the simplest and most effective way to achieve high
availability and traffic distribution for NVAs inspecting traffic within an Azure virtual network, minimizing
administrative effort.
Authoritative Links:
Azure Load Balancer: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/load-balancer/
Azure Load Balancer Standard SKU overview: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/load-balancer/load-
balancer-standard-overview
Question: 188 CertyIQ

You have five virtual machines that run Windows Server. Each virtual machine hosts a different web app.
You plan to use an Azure application gateway to provide access to each web app by using a hostname of
www.contoso.com and a different URL path for each web app, for example: https://www.contoso.com/app1.
You need to control the flow of traffic based on the URL path.
What should you configure?
A. HTTP settings
B. listeners
C. rules
D. rewrites
Answer: C
Explanation:
The correct answer is C. rules.
Here's a detailed justification:
Azure Application Gateway's core functionality revolves around routing traffic based on specific conditions.
When routing traffic based on URL paths (like /app1, /app2), you need to define rules that evaluate the
incoming request's URL and direct it to the appropriate backend pool (where your web app VMs reside). These
rules act as the decision-making engine for the Application Gateway. They analyze the request attributes,
including the URL path, and determine the correct backend pool to forward the request to.
While listeners are required to accept incoming traffic on a specific port and protocol (like HTTPS on port
443) and HTTP settings define the protocol, port, and other settings used when the Application Gateway
communicates with the backend servers, neither handles the URL-based routing logic. HTTP settings define
how the traffic is sent to the backend, but rules decide where to send it, according to the incoming URL path.
Rewrites can modify the request or response, but their primary function isn't the initial routing decision. The
rewrite functionality is generally used after the request has already been routed by a routing rule.
In this scenario, each web app is accessible via a different URL path under the same hostname
(www.contoso.com). To achieve this, you configure rules that examine the URL path (e.g., /app1, /app2, etc.).
Each rule is then associated with a specific backend pool containing the VM that hosts the corresponding web
app.
Therefore, using rules allows you to implement the necessary URL-based routing to direct traffic to the
appropriate backend pool for each web app.
Further reading:
Application Gateway routing rules: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/application-
gateway/configuration-http-settings
How an Application Gateway works: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/application-gateway/how-
application-gateway-works
Question: 189 CertyIQ
You plan to publish a website that will use an FQDN of www.contoso.com. The website will be hosted by using the
Azure App Service apps shown in the following table.

You plan to use Azure Traffic Manager to manage the routing of traffic for www.contoso.com between AS1 and
AS2.
You create a Traffic Manager profile named TMprofile1. TMprofile1 uses the weighted traffic-routing method.
You need to ensure that Traffic Manager routes traffic for www.contoso.com.
Which DNS record should you create?
A. two A records that map www.contoso.com to 131.107.100.1 and 131.107.200.1
B. a CNAME record that maps www.contoso.com to TMprofile1.azurefd.net
C. a CNAME record that maps www.contoso.com to TMprofile1.trafficmanager.net
D. a TXT record that contains a string of as1.contoso.com and as2.contoso.com in the details
Answer: C
Explanation:
To ensure that Traffic Manager routes traffic for www.contoso.com, you should create a CNAME record that
maps www.contoso.com to TMprofile1.trafficmanager.net.
A CNAME (Canonical Name) record is a type of DNS record that maps a hostname to another hostname, rather
than an IP address. When a client sends a request for the hostname specified in the CNAME record, the DNS
server responds with the IP address of the target hostname.
In this case, you can create a CNAME record that maps www.contoso.com to TMprofile1.trafficmanager.net,
which is the hostname of the Traffic Manager profile. This will allow clients to access the website using the
hostname www.contoso.com, while Traffic Manager handles the routing of traffic between AS1 and AS2
based on the configured traffic-routing method.
Therefore, the correct answer is C: a CNAME record that maps www.contoso.com to
TMprofile1.trafficmanager.net.
Reference:
https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/traffic-manager/quickstart-create-traffic-manager-profile
https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/app-service/configure-domain-traffic-manager

Your company has 10 instances of a web service. Each instance is hosted in a different Azure region and is

Answer:

Explanation:
Traffic Manager Algorithm: Multivalue.
Azure Traffic Manager offers different routing methods (algorithms) to distribute traffic across endpoints.
Multivalue is selected when you want to return multiple healthy endpoints in response to a DNS query.
