Question: 195 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains the following resources:
✑ A virtual network named Vnet1
✑ Two subnets named subnet1 and AzureFirewallSubnet
✑ A public Azure Firewall named FW1
✑ A route table named RT1 that is associated to Subnet 1
✑ A rule routing of 0.0.0.0/0 to FW1 in RT1
After deploying 10 servers that run Windows Server to Subnet 1, you discover that none of the virtual machines
were activated.
You need to ensure that the virtual machines can be activated.
What should you do?
A. On FW1, configure a DNAT rule for port 1688.
B. Deploy an application security group that allows outbound traffic to 1688.
C. On FW1, create an outbound network rule that allows traffic to the Azure Key Management Service (KMS).
D. On FW1, create an outbound service tag rule for Azure Cloud.
Answer: C
Explanation:
The correct solution is to create an outbound network rule on FW1 that allows traffic to the Azure Key
Management Service (KMS). Windows virtual machines in Azure require activation against a KMS server.
When VMs in a virtual network cannot directly access the internet, a firewall like Azure Firewall typically
handles outbound connectivity. If the firewall blocks access to the KMS server, the VMs will not activate.
Option A, configuring a DNAT rule for port 1688, is incorrect. DNAT (Destination Network Address Translation)
is used for inbound traffic, allowing external traffic to reach internal resources. KMS activation requires
outbound traffic from the VMs to the KMS server.
Option B, deploying an application security group (ASG), is also incorrect in this scenario. ASGs are used to
group VMs and apply security rules based on application context. While ASGs are valuable for segmenting
traffic, the core issue is that the Azure Firewall is likely blocking the VMs' outbound traffic to the KMS server.
Using ASGs wouldn't directly solve the activation problem. They would need to be combined with an NSG or

Firewall rule, making option C more direct.
Option D, creating an outbound service tag rule for Azure Cloud, is a broader approach but less precise than
targeting the KMS service. While "AzureCloud" includes many Azure services, it's best practice to restrict
firewall rules to the minimum required scope. Furthermore, the AzureCloud service tag is regional, and KMS
might be in a different region requiring a more granular rule for KMS or KMS[region].
Therefore, the most direct and secure solution is to create a specific outbound network rule on FW1 that
allows the VMs to reach the Azure KMS server. By enabling outbound traffic to KMS, the Windows VMs will be
able to activate.
Supporting Links:
Azure Firewall Network Rules: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/rule-processing
Azure Service Tags: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/service-tags-overview
Windows Activation on Azure: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/troubleshoot/azure/virtual-
machines/troubleshoot-activation-problems
Question: 196 CertyIQ
You have an Azure Front Door instance that has a single frontend named Frontend1 and an Azure Web Application
Firewall (WAF) policy named Policy1. Policy1 redirects requests that have a header containing "string1" to
https://www.contoso.com/redirect1. Policy1 is associated to Frontend1.
You need to configure additional redirection settings. Requests to Frontend1 that have a header containing
"string2" must be redirected to https:// www.contoso.com/redirect2.
Which three actions should you perform? Each correct answer presents part of the solution.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
A. Create a custom rule.
B. Create a policy.
C. Create a frontend host.
D. Configure a managed rule.
E. Add a custom rule to Policy1.
F. Create an association.
Answer: AEF
Explanation:
Here's a detailed justification for why the correct answer is AEF:
Understanding the Problem:
The scenario requires adding a new redirection rule to an existing Azure Front Door setup. We already have a
WAF policy (Policy1) associated with a frontend (Frontend1). The goal is to redirect requests based on a
specific header ("string2") to a new URL (https://www.contoso.com/redirect2). Since we need specific header
inspection, a custom rule is needed.
Justification of the Correct Actions:
A. Create a custom rule: Custom rules in WAFs allow you to define specific match conditions (in this case, a
header containing "string2") and actions (redirection). This is essential because the default managed rules
don't offer this level of customization for header-based redirection. Custom rules give you the flexibility to
react to specific patterns in incoming requests. https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/web-application-
firewall/afw-custom-rules

E. Add a custom rule to Policy1: Because Policy1 is already associated to Frontend1, adding a new rule to it
avoids creating and associating a new policy, thereby minimizing changes. By adding the custom rule to the
existing policy, you streamline the configuration. https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/web-application-
firewall/ag/configure-waf-rules
F. Create an association: Because a policy already exists in our situation, there is no need to create a new
association. However, if a new policy needed to be created instead, the association action should be used to
connect the policy to the frontend host, specifying that Policy1 should be applied to Frontend1. This
association directs how traffic to a particular frontend is filtered and managed.
https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/web-application-firewall/afw-front-door-configure
Why Other Options are Incorrect:
B. Create a policy: A policy already exists. Creating a new policy would require reassignment of rules.
C. Create a frontend host: A frontend host already exists. Creating a new one is unnecessary. The redirection
applies to traffic already flowing through Frontend1.
D. Configure a managed rule: Managed rules offer pre-configured protection against common threats but
lack the granularity to specify redirection based on custom headers like "string2". Custom rules are required
for that.
In summary, creating a custom rule to match the specific header and adding it to the existing WAF policy, and
confirming the association are the correct steps to achieve the desired redirection.
Question: 197 CertyIQ
You have 10 Azure App Service instances. Each instance hosts the same web app. Each instance is in a different
Azure region.
You need to configure Azure Traffic Manager to direct users to the instance that has the lowest latency.
Which routing method should you use?
A. geographic
B. weighted
C. priority
D. performance
Answer: D
Explanation:
The correct routing method is D. Performance.
Azure Traffic Manager uses different routing methods to determine which endpoint to direct traffic to. The
goal is to direct users to the instance with the lowest latency. The performance routing method in Traffic
Manager is specifically designed for this purpose. It directs traffic to the endpoint closest to the user based
on network latency measurements. Traffic Manager maintains a latency table that maps IP address ranges to
Azure regions, providing an accurate estimate of round-trip time. When a user makes a request, Traffic
Manager uses the source IP address of the DNS query to determine the best endpoint based on the latency
information.
Geographic routing directs traffic based on geographic location of the user. It's useful for scenarios where
content or service availability is region-specific. Weighted routing distributes traffic to endpoints based on

assigned weights, useful for A/B testing or gradual rollouts. Priority routing directs all traffic to the highest-
priority endpoint available, and failing that to the next highest, which is useful for failover scenarios. While
these options have their place, they are not the best fit when optimizing for lowest latency globally.
Performance routing uses actual network latency data, rather than assumptions based on geography or
manually configured weights, to ensure users are directed to the most responsive instance, irrespective of
geographic proximity alone.
Therefore, the performance routing method in Azure Traffic Manager directly addresses the requirement of
directing users to the App Service instance with the lowest latency by leveraging real-time network
performance data.
Relevant Link:
Traffic Manager routing methods
Question: 198 CertyIQ
Your company has offices in London, Tokyo, and New York.
The company has a web app named App1 that has the Azure Traffic Manager profile shown in the following table.
In Asia, you plan to deploy an additional endpoint that will host an updated version of App1.
You need to route 10 percent of the traffic from the Tokyo office to the new endpoint during testing.
What should you configure in Traffic Manager?
A. two profiles and five endpoints
B. two profiles and four endpoints
C. three profiles and four endpoints
D. one profile and five endpoints
Answer: B
Explanation:
Need two profiles. Add one Child profile using Weighted routing. One additional trial endpoint, to the existing
three, for the Child Profile is needed.
Note 1: Each Traffic Manager profile specifies a single traffic-routing method. However, there are scenarios
that require more sophisticated traffic routing than the routing provided by a single Traffic Manager profile.
You can nest Traffic Manager profiles to combine the benefits of more than one traffic-routing method.
Note 2: Weighted routing: Select Weighted routing when you want to distribute traffic across a set of
endpoints based on their weight. Set the weight the same to distribute evenly across all endpoints.

https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/traffic-manager/traffic-manager-nested-profiles

Question: 200 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT -
You have an Azure subscription. The subscription contains virtual machines that host websites as shown in the
following table.
You have the Azure Traffic Manager profiles shown in the following table.
You have the endpoints shown in the following table.
For each of the following statements, select Yes if the statement is true. Otherwise, select No.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
Hot Area:

Answer:
Explanation:
Box 1: No -
VM1, which is hosting site1.contoso.com, is located in East US. The VM1 endpoint status is degraded. Endpoint
monitoring health checks are failing. The endpoint isn't included in DNS responses and doesn't receive traffic.
When an endpoint has a Degraded status, it's no longer returned in response to DNS queries. Instead, an
alternative endpoint is chosen and returned. The traffic- routing method configured in the profile determines
how the alternative endpoint is chosen.
Priority. Endpoints form a prioritized list. The first available endpoint on the list is always returned. If an
endpoint status is Degraded, then the next available endpoint is returned.
The user will connect to site2.us.contoso.com instead.
Box 2: No -
VM3, which is hosting site2.contoso.com, is located in in East US. The VM3 endpoint status is
CheckingEndpoint. The endpoint is monitored, but the results of the first probe haven't been received yet.
CheckingEndpoint is a temporary state that usually occurs immediately after adding or enabling an endpoint
in the profile. An endpoint in this state is included in DNS responses and can receive traffic.
User will connect to site2.contoso.com, not to site2.uk.contoso.com
Box 3: No -
VM3, which is hosting site2.contoso.com, is located in in East US. The VM1 endpoint status is
CheckingEndpoint, which is OK (see above).
User will connect to site2.contoso.com, not to site2.japan.contoso.com
Reference:
https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/traffic-manager/traffic-manager-monitoring

Question: 201 CertyIQ
You have an Azure application gateway configured for a single website that is available at
https://www.contoso.com.
The application gateway contains one backend pool and one rule. The backend pool contains two backend servers.
Each backend server has an additional website that is available on port 8080.
You need to ensure that if port 8080 is unavailable on a backend server, all the traffic for https://www.contoso.com
is redirected to the other backend server.
What should you do?
A. Create a health probe
B. Add a new rule
C. Change the port on the listener
D. Add a new listener
Answer: A
Explanation:
The correct answer is A: Create a health probe.
Health probes are essential for application gateways because they monitor the health of the backend servers.
They periodically send requests to the backend servers to determine if they are responsive and healthy. In this
scenario, creating a health probe that specifically checks the availability of port 8080 on the backend servers
allows the application gateway to detect when port 8080 is unavailable on one of the servers.
When the health probe determines that a backend server is unhealthy (i.e., port 8080 is unavailable), the
application gateway automatically stops sending traffic to that server and redirects all traffic to the
remaining healthy backend server. This ensures high availability and prevents users from experiencing
downtime due to an unavailable backend server. The application gateway does this based on the 'backend
pools', which define where the application gateway redirects traffic based on rules (listeners).
Adding a new rule (B) is not necessary because the existing rule already directs traffic to the backend pool.
Changing the port on the listener (C) would change the port that the application gateway listens on, which is
not what we want. Adding a new listener (D) is also not needed because the existing listener is already
configured for https://www.contoso.com. Only health probe can check port 8080 of backends
Here are some authoritative links for further research:
Azure Application Gateway Health Probes: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/application-
gateway/application-gateway-probe-overview
Backend health monitoring in Azure Application Gateway: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-
us/azure/application-gateway/application-gateway-backend-health
Question: 202 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains the following resources:
• A virtual network named Vnet1
• Two subnets named subnet1 and AzureFirewallSubnet
• A public Azure Firewall named FW1
• A route table named RT1 that is associated to Subnet1
• A rule routing of 0.0.0.0/0 to FW1 in RT1
After deploying 10 servers that run Windows Server to Subnet1, you discover that none of the virtual machines
were activated.
You need to ensure that the virtual machines can be activated.

What should you do?
A. On FW1, create an outbound service tag rule for AzureCloud.
B. Add an internet route to RT1 for the Azure Key Management Service (KMS).
C. On FW1, configure a DNAT rule for port 1688.
D. Deploy an Azure Standard Load Balancer that has an outbound NAT rule.
Answer: A
Explanation:
A. On FW1, create an outbound service tag rule for AzureCloud.
Windows Server VMs activate through Azure Key Management Service (KMS).
KMS is reachable only through AzureCloud service tag (NOT Internet).
Because your route table directs all outbound traffic (0.0.0.0/0) to the firewall (FW1), the firewall must
explicitly allow outbound access to AzureCloud.
So, you must add an outbound rule on Azure Firewall:
Source: Subnet1
Destination: AzureCloud service tag
Port: TCP 1688 (included in AzureCloud traffic)
Action: Allow
This enables VM activation.

Question: 204 CertyIQ
You have an internal Basic Azure Load Balancer named LB1 that has two frontend IP addresses. The backend pool
of LB1 contains two Azure virtual machines named VM1 and VM2.
You need to configure the rules on LB1 as shown in the following table.
What should you do for each rule?
A. Enable Floating IP.
B. Disable Floating IP.

C. Set Session persistence to Enabled.
D. Set Session persistence to Disabled.
Answer: A
Explanation:
A. Enable Floating IP .
When Floating IP is enabled, the load balancer does not change the destination IP address of the incoming
traffic, ensuring that the backend VM processes the request as if it was sent directly to it.
This is critical for NAT rules and DSR (Direct Server Return) scenarios.

Question: 205 CertyIQ
Your company has 40 branch offices that are linked by using a Software-Defined Wide Area Network (SD-WAN).
The SD-WAN uses BGP.
You have an Azure subscription that contains 20 virtual networks configured as a hub and spoke topology. The
topology contains a hub virtual network named Vnet1.
The virtual networks connect to the SD-WAN by using a network virtual appliance (NVA) in Vnet1.
You need to ensure that BGP route advertisements will propagate between the virtual networks and the SD-WAN.
The solution must minimize administrative effort.
What should you implement?
A. An Azure VPN Gateway that has BGP enabled
B. a NAT gateway
C. Azure Traffic Manager
D. Azure Route Server
Answer: D
Explanation:
The correct answer is D. Azure Route Server. Here's why:
The primary requirement is to propagate BGP routes between the Azure virtual networks (hub and spoke) and
the SD-WAN. The hub-and-spoke architecture relies on Vnet1 for connectivity to the SD-WAN via an NVA.
Azure Route Server simplifies dynamic routing between your network virtual appliance (NVA) and Azure
Virtual Network. It allows direct exchange of routing information using BGP between the NVA and the Azure
Software Defined Networking (SDN) stack without needing to manually configure route tables or user-defined
routes (UDRs). This significantly reduces administrative overhead. Route Server automatically learns routes
from your NVA and programs them on the virtual network interfaces.
Azure VPN Gateway with BGP could establish a BGP connection with the SD-WAN. However, it doesn't
inherently propagate routes between the spoke virtual networks and the SD-WAN through the existing NVA.
Implementing multiple VPN Gateways or complex route configurations would increase complexity.
NAT Gateway is used for outbound connectivity from resources inside a virtual network to the internet or

other networks. It doesn't participate in BGP route propagation.
Azure Traffic Manager is a DNS-based traffic load balancer and is not related to BGP routing.
Therefore, Route Server directly addresses the need for simplified BGP route exchange in the existing hub-
and-spoke topology with an NVA, minimizing administrative effort compared to other options. It provides the
dynamic route propagation functionality required without needing to redesign the network. Route server
enables the NVA to advertise its routes (received from the SD-WAN) to Azure, and vice-versa, ensuring
seamless routing between the virtual networks and the SD-WAN via BGP.
Authoritative Links:
Azure Route Server Documentation
About Azure Route Server

Question: 207 CertyIQ
DRAG DROP
-
Your company, named Contoso, Ltd., has an Azure subscription that contains the resources shown in the following
table.

You plan to deploy Azure Front Door. The solution must meet the following requirements:
• Requests to a URL of https://contoso.azurefd.net/uk must be routed to App1uk.
• Requests to a URL of https://contoso.azurefd.net/us must be routed to App1us.
• Requests to a URL of https://contoso.azurefd.net/images must be routed to the storage account closest to the
user.
What is the minimum number of backend pools and routing rules you should create? To answer, drag the
appropriate number to the correct components. Each number may be used once, more than once, or not at all. You
may need to drag the split bar between panes or scroll to view content.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
Answer:
Explanation:
3 Backend Pools | 3 Rules

This is a Classic Front Door question. The first reference link provides an overview of classic routing. The
questions shows we have a single frontend (contoso.azurefd.net) and there are three paths - /uk, /us, and
/images.
The second link shows the three paths would each be a separate rule.
Regarding the number of backend pools, the question states, "...must be routed to [App1uk or App1us]" for the
two App Services. The third link does not indicate there is a way to route traffic to a specific app service based
on location. However, if we put each app service in its own backend pool, we can have the path rule route to
the correct App Service everytime. The Latency routing logic is fine for storage accounts, but not the App
Services based on the question requirements.

Question: 208 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT
-
You have an Azure subscription that contains the resource groups shown in the following table.
You have the virtual networks shown in the following table.
Vnet1 contains two virtual machines named VM1 and VM2. Vnet2 contains two virtual machines named VM3 and
VM4.
You have the network security groups (NSGs) shown in the following table that include only default rules.
You have the Azure load balancers shown in the following table.

For each of the following statements, select Yes if the statement is true. Otherwise, select No.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
Answer:
Explanation:
VM2 can be added to the backend pool of Lb2.
No
A VM can only be added to the backend pool if it is in the same virtual network (VNet) and meets the Load
Balancer SKU requirements.

If VM2 does not meet these criteria, it cannot be added to the backend pool.
The answer "No" means VM2 does not meet the required conditions to be part of Lb2's backend pool.
VM4 can access VM3 via port 1433 by using the frontend address of Lb2.
Yes
Port 1433 is used for SQL Server access.
If VM4 is trying to access VM3 via Lb2 and:
Lb2 is configured to forward SQL traffic (port 1433) to VM3
Load Balancing rules exist for this traffic
NSGs and firewall rules allow the connection

No

Lb1 might be an internal load balancer (not public).
Port 80 rule might not be configured.
NSG or firewall rules might be blocking traffic.

Answer: C

Listener:
A listener is required to handle incoming requests for app2.contoso.com.
The listener will be configured for HTTPS (443) and associated with the specific hostname
(app2.contoso.com).
Backend Pool:

It will contain App2 as the backend target (could be an Azure Web App, VM, or another backend service).
Routing Rule:
A routing rule is necessary to map the listener to the backend pool.
This ensures that requests received by the listener for app2.contoso.com are properly forwarded to App2.

Question: 211 CertyIQ
You have two Azure virtual networks in the East US Azure region as shown in the following table.
The virtual networks are peered to one another. Each virtual network contains four subnets.
You plan to deploy a virtual machine named VM1 that will inspect and route traffic between all the subnets on both
the virtual networks.
What is the minimum number of IP addresses that you must assign to VM1?
A.1
B.2
C.4
D.8
Answer: A
Explanation:
https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/firewall-faq#can-azure-firewall-forward-and-filter-network-
traffic-between-subnets-in-the-same-virtual-network-or-peered-virtual-networksCan Azure Firewall forward
and filter network traffic between subnets in the same virtual network or peered virtual networks?Yes.
However, configuring the UDRs to redirect traffic between subnets in the same VNET requires additional
attention. While using the VNET address range as a target prefix for the UDR is sufficient, this also routes all
traffic from one machine to another machine in the same subnet through the Azure Firewall instance. To avoid
this, include a route for the subnet in the UDR with a next hop type of VNET. Managing these routes might be
cumbersome and prone to error. The recommended method for internal network segmentation is to use
Network Security Groups, which don't require UDRs.
Question: 212 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains the following resources:
• A virtual network named Vnet1
• Two subnets named subnet1 and AzureFirewallSubnet
• A public Azure Firewall named FW1
• A route table named RT1 that is associated to Subnet1
• A rule routing of 0.0.0.0/0 to FW1 in RT1
After deploying 10 servers that run Windows Server to Subnet1, you discover that none of the virtual machines
were activated.
You need to ensure that the virtual machines can be activated.
What should you do?
A. On FW1, configure a DNAT rule for port 1688
B. Deploy a NAT gateway.
C. Add an internet route to RT1 for the Azure Key Management Service (KMS).
D. To Subnet1, associate a network security group (NSG) that allows outbound access to port 1688.
Answer: C

Explanation:
The issue is that VMs in Subnet1 can't activate their Windows licenses, meaning they can't reach the Azure
Key Management Service (KMS) for activation. RT1 routes all internet-bound traffic (0.0.0.0/0) through FW1.
By default, Azure Firewall blocks all outbound traffic unless explicitly allowed through firewall rules. While
option A suggests a DNAT rule, KMS activation requires outbound connectivity, not inbound. Options B and D
would not solve the KMS connectivity issue directly.
Therefore, the correct solution is to create a route within RT1 that directs traffic destined for the Azure KMS
service directly to the internet, bypassing the firewall. This bypass is necessary because the firewall is likely
blocking the KMS traffic. The KMS service is hosted by Microsoft Azure and needs to be accessible on port
1688 for activation. By creating a route specifically for the KMS service, VMs in Subnet1 can communicate
with the KMS servers and activate their licenses. This ensures that the VMs can access the internet service
required for Windows activation without routing all internet traffic through the firewall for activation
purposes, which is more secure and efficient. Adding a specific route avoids modifying firewall rules, keeping
the security posture intact while resolving the activation issue.
Further Reading:
Azure Key Management Service (KMS) activation: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows-server/get-
started/kms-client-activation-keys
Azure Virtual Network traffic routing: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/virtual-
networks-udr-overview
Azure Firewall: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/overview
Question: 213 CertyIQ
You have an on-premises network.
You have an Azure subscription that includes a virtual network named VNet1 and a private Azure Kubernetes
Service (AKS) cluster named AKS1. VNet1 is connected to your on-premises environment via an Azure
ExpressRoute circuit. AKS1 is connected to VNet1.
You need to implement an off-cluster ingress controller for AKS1. The solution must provide connectivity from the
on-premises environment to containerized workloads hosted on AKS1.
Which Azure service should you use?
A. Azure Application Gateway
B. Azure Front Door
C. Azure Traffic Manager
D. Azure Load Balancer
Answer: A
Explanation:
Here's a detailed justification for choosing Azure Application Gateway in this scenario:
The core requirement is to expose containerized workloads in AKS1 to the on-premises network, which is
connected to Azure via ExpressRoute. An off-cluster ingress controller manages external access to the
services within the Kubernetes cluster.
Azure Application Gateway's Ingress Controller (AGIC) directly integrates with AKS. This allows the
Application Gateway to automatically configure itself based on Kubernetes Ingress resources. This
automation simplifies management and ensures consistent routing.
On-premises connectivity: Application Gateway is deployed within VNet1, which is connected to the on-

premises network through ExpressRoute. This allows the Application Gateway to be reachable from the on-
premises environment. The Application Gateway's front-end IP address (either public or private) can be
advertised over the ExpressRoute connection to allow on-premise clients to reach the AKS services.
Layer 7 Routing: Application Gateway operates at Layer 7 (the application layer), allowing it to make routing
decisions based on HTTP headers, URLs, and other application-level information. This is crucial for complex
routing scenarios often found in microservices architectures within Kubernetes.
SSL Termination: Application Gateway can handle SSL termination, offloading the processing burden from
the AKS cluster itself. This improves the performance of the containerized applications.
Web Application Firewall (WAF): Application Gateway provides an integrated WAF, offering protection
against common web exploits. This enhances the security of the exposed applications.
Alternatives' Limitations: Azure Front Door is a global service and while it can route traffic to an Application
Gateway, it doesn't offer the AKS integration or direct on-premises connectivity required. Azure Traffic
Manager is a DNS-based load balancer, less suitable for the fine-grained routing needs of an ingress
controller. Azure Load Balancer, while capable of load balancing, primarily operates at Layer 4 and lacks the
application-level routing capabilities necessary for an effective ingress controller. It also doesn't have the
direct AKS integration that Application Gateway offers.
Therefore, Azure Application Gateway provides the best combination of AKS integration, on-premises
connectivity via ExpressRoute, Layer 7 routing capabilities, and security features needed to implement an off-
cluster ingress controller in this specific scenario.
Supporting Documentation:
Azure Application Gateway Ingress Controller for AKS: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/application-
gateway/ingress-controller-overview
Application Gateway with Private AKS Cluster: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/application-
gateway/tutorial-ingress-controller-private-aks

Question: 218 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains the following resources:
•A virtual network named Vnet1
•Two subnets named subnet1 and AzureFirewallSubnet

•A public Azure Firewall named FW1
•A route table named RT1 that is associated to Subnet1
•A rule routing of 0.0.0.0/0 to FW1 in RT1
After deploying 10 servers that run Windows Server to Subnet1, you discover that none of the virtual machines
were activated.
You need to ensure that the virtual machines can be activated.
What should you do?
A.On FW1, configure a DNAT rule for port 1688.
B.On FW1, create an outbound network rule that allows traffic to the Azure Key Management Service (KMS).
C.Deploy an application security group that allows outbound traffic to 1688.
D.Deploy an Azure Standard Load Balancer that has an outbound NAT rule.
Answer: B
Explanation:
The problem states that VMs in Subnet1 are not activating. Since all traffic from Subnet1 is routed through
Azure Firewall (FW1) due to the 0.0.0.0/0 route in RT1, the firewall is blocking the activation traffic. Windows
Server activation requires communication with the Azure Key Management Service (KMS) on TCP port 1688.
Option B, creating an outbound network rule on FW1 to allow traffic to the Azure KMS, directly addresses the
problem. This allows the VMs to reach the KMS servers for activation while maintaining the overall security
posture enforced by the firewall. Azure VMs rely on KMS activation, particularly when images are provisioned
from the Azure Marketplace.
Option A, configuring a DNAT rule for port 1688, is incorrect. DNAT (Destination Network Address Translation)
is for inbound traffic, not outbound traffic needed for activation.
Option C, deploying an application security group, is also incorrect. Application Security Groups (ASGs) are
used to group VMs and apply network security rules. While ASGs can control outbound traffic, this would add
an unnecessary layer of complexity when a simple firewall rule change on FW1 can fix the problem. This
approach also assumes an existing Network Security Group (NSG) where the ASG is associated, adding to the
resources needed.
Option D, deploying an Azure Standard Load Balancer with outbound NAT, is incorrect and overly complex.
While an outbound NAT rule on a Load Balancer could allow VMs to access the internet, including KMS, it is
unnecessary. The existing Azure Firewall is already designed to control outbound traffic. Using a load
balancer solely for outbound NAT adds cost and complexity that is not required. The existing infrastructure
(FW1) provides the needed functionality with a simple rule addition.
Therefore, the most efficient and correct solution is to create an outbound network rule on FW1 to allow
traffic to the Azure Key Management Service (KMS).
Reference:
Azure Firewall network rules:
Azure Key Management Services (KMS) Activation
Question: 219 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains a virtual network named VNet1. VNet1 contains a subnet named
Subnet1.
You deploy an instance of Azure Application Gateway v2 named AppGw1 to Subnet1. You create a network security
group (NSG) named NSG1 and link NSG1 to Subnet1.

You need to ensure that AppGw1 will only load balance traffic that originates from VNet1. The solution must
minimize the impact on the functionality of AppGw1.
What should you add to NSG1?
A.an outbound rule that has a priority of 4096 and blocks all internet traffic
B.an inbound rule that has a priority of 4096 and blocks all internet traffic
C.an inbound rule that has a priority of 100 and blocks all internet traffic
D.an outbound rule that has a priority 100 and blocks all internet traffic
Answer: B
Explanation:
The correct answer is B. an inbound rule that has a priority of 4096 and blocks all internet traffic.
Here's a detailed justification:
The goal is to ensure Application Gateway (AppGw1) only load balances traffic originating from VNet1. We
need to restrict external access into the subnet where the Application Gateway resides. Network Security
Groups (NSGs) control inbound and outbound traffic at the subnet or network interface level. Since we want
to control traffic entering the subnet containing AppGw1, we need to configure an inbound rule.
The requirement is to allow traffic from VNet1, while blocking all other traffic (specifically, internet traffic).
NSGs process rules based on priority, with lower numbers representing higher priority. Azure NSGs have
default rules. Default rules allow inbound traffic within the VNet. To achieve the desired restriction without
disrupting internal VNet communication, we must create an inbound rule that specifically blocks external
(internet) traffic but doesn't interfere with the default VNet rule. A priority of 4096 is used because it is the
lowest user definable priority. User defined rules are evaluated before default rules. A priority of 100 is more
likely to block other traffic than just the internet traffic. By placing a lower priority rule to block internet
traffic, the internal VNet traffic will continue to work because of the default VNet inbound rule.
Therefore, adding an inbound rule with a priority of 4096 that blocks all internet traffic effectively restricts
access to AppGw1 to only traffic originating from within VNet1, satisfying the requirement and minimizing
impact to AppGw1's normal operation within the VNet.
Azure Network Security Groups (NSGs): https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/network-
security-groups-overview
Application Gateway Ingress Controller (AGIC): https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/application-
gateway/ingress-controller-overview (While not directly related to NSG configuration, understanding AGIC
provides context to Application Gateway usage in Azure)
Question: 220 CertyIQ
You plan to implement an Azure virtual network that will contain 10 virtual subnets. The subnets will use IPv6
addresses. Each subnet will host up to 200 load-balanced virtual machines.
You need to recommend a load balancing solution for the virtual network. The solution must meet the following
requirements:
•The virtual machines and the load balancer must be accessible only from the virtual network.
•Costs must be minimized.
What should you include in the recommendation?
A.Basic Azure Load Balancer
B.Azure Application Gateway v1

C.Azure Standard Load Balancer
D.Azure Application Gateway v2
Answer: C
Explanation:
Here's a detailed justification for why Azure Standard Load Balancer is the correct choice, along with
explanations of why the other options are less suitable:
The requirement to use IPv6 addresses immediately eliminates Basic Load Balancer (Option A). Basic Load
Balancer does not support IPv6. It is also generally a lower-tier, feature-limited service compared to Standard
Load Balancer.
Azure Application Gateway (Options B and D) is primarily a web application firewall and load balancer
intended for HTTP/HTTPS traffic. While it can load balance internal applications, it's not the most cost-
effective solution for simply balancing traffic between virtual machines within a virtual network. It introduces
complexity and features that aren't necessary for the given scenario, namely web application firewall rules
and HTTP layer manipulation. Application Gateway is better suited for publicly facing web applications
requiring security and advanced traffic routing based on HTTP headers. Furthermore, Application Gateway v1
is outdated, and v2 is more expensive than standard load balancer.
Azure Standard Load Balancer (Option C) does support IPv6. It's an internal load balancer meaning it's
accessible only from within the virtual network, meeting the second requirement. Standard Load Balancer is a
general-purpose load balancer designed for TCP and UDP traffic, making it suitable for balancing traffic
across the virtual machines within the subnets. For scenarios with 200 VMs per subnet, the Standard Load
Balancer provides a better scaling and feature set compared to Basic. Standard Load Balancer is typically
more cost-effective than Application Gateway when you don't need the web application firewall (WAF) or
advanced HTTP routing capabilities. Therefore, it fulfills all requirements: IPv6 support, internal accessibility,
and cost minimization.
In summary, Standard Load Balancer is the best option because it meets all the requirements (IPv6, internal
access, and minimized cost) without unnecessary features or limitations present in the other options.
Authoritative Links:
Azure Load Balancer: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/load-balancer/
Azure Load Balancer SKUs: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/load-balancer/skus
Azure Application Gateway: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/application-gateway/
Question: 221 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains the following resources:
•A virtual network named Vnet1
•Two subnets named subnet1 and AzureFirewallSubnet
•A public Azure Firewall named FW1
•A route table named RT1 that is associated to Subnet1
•A rule routing of 0.0.0.0/0 to FW1 in RT1
After deploying 10 servers that run Windows Server to Subnet1, you discover that none of the virtual machines
were activated.
You need to ensure that the virtual machines can be activated.
What should you do?
A.On FW1, configure a DNAT rule for port 1688.
B.Deploy an application security group that allows outbound traffic to 1688.

C.Add an internet route to RT1 for the Azure Key Management Service (KMS).
D.Deploy an Azure Standard Load Balancer that has an outbound NAT rule.
Answer: C
Explanation:
The issue is that VMs in Subnet1 cannot activate Windows due to network restrictions imposed by Azure
Firewall. The VMs need to reach the Azure Key Management Service (KMS) servers for activation. Because the
default route (0.0.0.0/0) in RT1 points to FW1, all outbound internet traffic, including KMS activation requests,
is being inspected and possibly blocked by the firewall.
Option C, "Add an internet route to RT1 for the Azure Key Management Service (KMS)," is the correct solution.
Instead of directing all traffic to the firewall, a specific route for the KMS servers needs to be created. This
route will allow VMs to reach the KMS servers directly for activation purposes bypassing the firewall for that
specific traffic. Azure KMS servers utilize specific IP addresses which can be found within the official
documentation. This requires identifying the KMS IP addresses and then creating a route for those addresses
with a next hop type of "Internet".
Option A is incorrect because DNAT (Destination NAT) is used for inbound traffic, while activation requires
outbound connectivity to the KMS server. Option B is incorrect because application security groups (ASGs)
control network traffic based on applications, not to define explicit routes to the internet. Also, an ASG on its
own won't establish routes to the internet. Option D, using a Load Balancer with outbound NAT, would enable
internet access but isn't necessary and adds unnecessary complexity, especially considering the problem is
specific to KMS traffic. A direct route is more efficient.
Therefore, creating a specific route in RT1 for the KMS servers allows Windows activation to succeed without
unnecessarily routing all internet traffic through the firewall.
Relevant links:
Activate Windows VMs on Azure: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/troubleshoot/azure/virtual-
machines/troubleshoot-activation-problems
Azure Route Tables: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/manage-route-table
How to activate Windows Server using Azure KMS: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-
us/answers/questions/102460/how-to-activate-windows-server-using-azure-kms

Question: 224 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains the following resources:
•A virtual network named Vnet1
•Two subnets named subnet1 and AzureFirewallSubnet
•A public Azure Firewall named FW1
•A route table named RT1 that is associated to Subnet1
•A rule routing of 0.0.0.0/0 to FW1 in RT1
After deploying 10 servers that run Windows Server to Subnet1, you discover that none of the virtual machines
were activated.
You need to ensure that the virtual machines can be activated.
What should you do?

A.On FW1, create an outbound service tag rule for AzureCloud.
B.Deploy an Azure Standard Load Balancer that has an outbound NAT rule.
C.On FW1, create an outbound network rule that allows traffic to the Azure Key Management Service (KMS).
D.To Subnet1, associate a network security group (NSG) that allows outbound access to port 1688.
Answer: C
Explanation:
The core issue is that the Windows Server virtual machines in Subnet1 cannot activate. Given that all outbound
traffic from Subnet1 is routed through Azure Firewall (FW1) via RT1, the firewall is likely blocking the
activation traffic. Windows activation requires communication with the Key Management Service (KMS)
servers.
Option A (AzureCloud service tag): While the AzureCloud service tag encompasses a broad range of Azure
services, it may not specifically include the KMS servers necessary for activation. Also, using overly broad
service tags can create unnecessary exposure and security risks.
Option B (Azure Standard Load Balancer with outbound NAT): Introducing a load balancer solely for outbound
NAT is an overkill solution. The existing Azure Firewall is perfectly capable of handling outbound traffic with
proper rules. It adds unnecessary complexity and cost.
Option C (Outbound network rule for Azure KMS on FW1): This is the correct approach. Creating a specific
outbound network rule on Azure Firewall to allow traffic destined for the Azure Key Management Service
(KMS) resolves the activation issue directly. This rule allows the VMs to communicate with KMS servers,
enabling activation without compromising overall security posture. This offers granular control, allowing KMS
traffic while still maintaining other security policies.
Option D (NSG for outbound access to port 1688): This is incorrect because the existing routing configuration
forces all outbound traffic through the Azure Firewall. An NSG on Subnet1 would be bypassed as the traffic is
routed through the firewall. The firewall, not the NSG, is the point where access control must be configured.
Therefore, the optimal solution is to create a specific outbound network rule on the Azure Firewall (FW1) that
allows traffic to the Azure Key Management Service (KMS). This enables Windows activation while
maintaining the desired security posture.
Supporting Documentation:
Azure Firewall network rules: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/rule-processing
Key Management Services (KMS) client activation: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows-server/get-
started/kms-client-activation-keys
Question: 225 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains the following resources:
•A virtual network named Vnet1
•Two subnets named subnet1 and AzureFirewallSubnet
•A public Azure Firewall named FW1
•A route table named RT1 that is associated to Subnet1
•A rule routing of 0.0.0.0/0 to FW1 in RT1
After deploying 10 servers that run Windows Server to Subnet1, you discover that none of the virtual machine
operating systems were activated.
You need to ensure that the virtual machines can be activated.
What should you do?

A.To Subnet1, associate a network security group (NSG) that allows outbound access to port 1688.
B.On FW1, create an outbound network rule that allows traffic to the Azure Key Management Service (KMS).
C.Deploy a NAT gateway.
D.Deploy an application security group that allows outbound traffic to 1688.
Answer: B
Explanation:
The correct answer is B. To activate Windows Server virtual machines in Azure, the VMs need to connect to
the Azure Key Management Service (KMS) for licensing. With the current configuration, all outbound traffic
from Subnet1 (where the VMs reside) is routed through the Azure Firewall (FW1) because of the 0.0.0.0/0 route
in RT1. This means the VMs cannot directly reach the KMS server.
Option A is incorrect because associating an NSG would not enable activation if FW1 is blocking traffic. Option
C, deploying a NAT gateway, would provide outbound internet access but does not guarantee the VMs can
reach the KMS. Although Option D introduces application security groups, this alone doesn't facilitate KMS
activation in the presence of the routing rules.
The VMs' activation failures signify the Azure Firewall is blocking the KMS traffic. To resolve this, we must
create a network rule on FW1 that explicitly allows outbound traffic to the Azure KMS servers on port 1688.
This ensures the VMs can reach the KMS and successfully activate their Windows Server licenses.
The outbound rule should specify the KMS FQDN or IP address range and port 1688, using the TCP protocol.
By allowing this traffic through the firewall, the activation process can complete successfully. This approach
integrates with the existing infrastructure, leveraging the firewall for security while enabling a necessary
service function.Here are authoritative links:
Azure Key Management Services (KMS) client setup and discovery
Azure Firewall Network Rules

The solution must minimize administrative effort and costs.
What should you identify? To answer, drag the appropriate options to the correct targets. Each option may be used
once, more than once, or not at all. You may need to drag the split bar between panes or scroll to view content.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.

Answer:
Explanation:
1.SKU: Premium.

geofencing, and rate limiting.
The Premium SKU includes managed bot protection rules.

Common Header : X-Forwarded-For.

Question: 228 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains the following resources:
•A virtual network named Vnet1
•Two subnets named subnet1 and AzureFirewallSubnet
•A public Azure Firewall named FW1
•A route table named RT1 that is associated to Subnet1
•A rule routing of 0.0.0.0/0 to FW1 in RT1
After deploying 10 servers that run Windows Server to Subnet1, you discover that none of the virtual machine
operating systems were activated.
You need to ensure that the virtual machines can be activated.
What should you do?
A.On FW1, create an outbound service tag rule for AzureCloud.
B.On FW1, create an outbound network rule that allows traffic to the Azure Key Management Service (KMS).
C.To Subnet1, associate a network security group (NSG) that allows outbound access to port 1688.
D.Deploy an application security group that allows outbound traffic to 1688.
Answer: B
Explanation:
The problem describes a scenario where VMs in an Azure subnet behind an Azure Firewall can't activate their
Windows Server licenses. Windows activation relies on the Key Management Service (KMS), a Microsoft
service for activating software. Because the VMs cannot activate, traffic to the KMS server is being blocked.
Option B is correct because it suggests creating an outbound network rule on the Azure Firewall (FW1) to
allow traffic to the Azure KMS servers. The firewall is explicitly blocking all outbound traffic by default
because of the default route (0.0.0.0/0) pointing to it. KMS uses specific ports (typically TCP port 1688) to
communicate. Therefore, creating a rule on the firewall to allow outbound traffic to the Azure KMS servers on
port 1688 would solve the activation issue.
Option A is incorrect. While the AzureCloud service tag includes various Microsoft services, it is a broader tag
and may not guarantee access specifically to the KMS servers, and is not intended for KMS activation
explicitly. Using the specific network rule targeting KMS offers more granular control and ensures only the
necessary traffic is allowed.

Option C is incorrect. NSGs are used to filter traffic at the subnet or network interface level, but the traffic is
already being routed through the Azure Firewall, which acts as a centralized point of control. Adding an NSG
allowing outbound access to port 1688 on Subnet1 after the route table directs traffic to the firewall will not
solve the issue, as the firewall is still blocking the traffic unless explicitly allowed through its rules.
Option D is incorrect. Application Security Groups (ASGs) are used to group VMs based on application needs
and apply network security policies, but they don't inherently allow outbound traffic. While an ASG could be
used in conjunction with an NSG rule, it doesn't address the core problem of the Azure Firewall blocking
traffic to the KMS server. The traffic goes through the firewall regardless of ASG membership.
Therefore, the only solution that directly addresses the problem of blocked traffic to the KMS servers is to
create an outbound network rule on the Azure Firewall that explicitly allows traffic to the KMS servers.
Further Research:
Azure Firewall Network Rules: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/rule-processing
Key Management Services (KMS) activation planning: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows-
server/get-started/kms-client-activation-planning
Azure Service Tags: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/service-tags-overview

-
You have two servers that are each hosted by a separate service provider in New York and Germany. The server
hosted in New York is accessible by using a host name of ny.contoso.com. The server hosted in Germany is
accessible by using a host name of de.contoso.com.
You need to provide a single host name to access both servers. The solution must ensure that traffic originating
from Germany is routed to de.contoso.com. All other traffic must be routed to ny.contoso.com.
To complete this task, sign in to the Azure portal.
Answer:

Explanation:
Step 1: Sign in to Azure
Log in to the Azure portal with your account.
Step 2: Navigate to the Application Gateway
In the search bar, type Application Gateway.
Select your Application Gateway instance.
Step 3: Select Rewrites
In the left panel, select Rewrites.
Click + Create to add a new rewrite set.
Step 4: Provide a Name for the Rewrite Set
Enter a meaningful name for the rewrite set.
Step 5: Associate the Rewrite Set with a Routing Rule
Assign the rewrite set to an existing routing rule.
Click Next to proceed.
Step 6: Add a Rewrite Rule
Click + Add rewrite rule to create a new rule.
Step 7: Define Conditions for the Rewrite Rule (Optional)
Click + Add condition (if you want the rule to apply conditionally).
Example: Apply only when the Location header contains acmewebsite.net.

Step 8: Choose the Type of Variable to Modify
Select HTTP Header as the variable type.
For Request Headers, choose Common Header → Location.
Apply a regular expression to match a specific pattern (e.g., acmewebsite.net).
Step 9: Define the Action for Header Modification
Select Rewrite header.
Choose the Response header → Location.
Replace acmewebsite.net with contoso.com.
Step 10: Save and Apply the Rewrite Set
Click Create to finalize the rewrite set.
The newly created rewrite rule should now be listed under Rewrites.
Step 11: Verify the Rewrite Set
Ensure that the rewrite set appears in Application Gateway with a status of active.

Question: 233 CertyIQ
You have an Azure Front Door instance named FD that contains an origin group named OG1.
You need to configure a health probe for OG1. The solution must minimize the amount of traffic generated by the
health probe.
Which HTTP method should you use?
A.CONNECT

B.HEAD
C.TRACE
D.GET
Answer: B
Explanation:
The correct HTTP method for health probes in Azure Front Door, to minimize traffic, is HEAD.
Health probes are used by Azure Front Door to determine the availability and health of backend origins in an
origin group. The goal is to send the minimum amount of data possible to avoid overburdening the origin
servers.
The HEAD method requests the headers that would be returned if the HEAD request was replaced with a GET
request. This allows Front Door to check the server's status and headers without transferring the entire
resource body.
A GET request, in contrast, retrieves the entire resource, which generates significantly more traffic and
consumes more bandwidth. CONNECT and TRACE are not suitable for health probes. CONNECT establishes a
tunnel to the server, and TRACE is a diagnostic method that can potentially expose sensitive information.
HEAD specifically retrieves just header information, which fulfills the requirement of checking backend
health with minimal overhead. By using HEAD, you can quickly determine if the origin is responding correctly
without incurring the performance penalty of downloading the full content. This conserves bandwidth,
reduces load on the origin servers, and allows Front Door to efficiently manage traffic based on accurate
health information. It improves overall performance and scalability by minimizing the resources needed for
health monitoring. Therefore, HEAD is the ideal choice for health probes when the objective is to reduce
traffic.
Further reading can be found in the official Microsoft Azure documentation:
Azure Front Door health probes
HTTP methods

Question: 239 CertyIQ
You have two Azure virtual networks named VNet1 and VNet2 that are peered with each other. VNet1 hosts 10
virtual machines that contain web servers. VNet2 hosts five virtual machines that contain database servers.
You need to configure a security solution that meets the following requirements:
•Ensures that the database servers can accept connections only from the web servers
•Ensures that the web servers can initiate connections only to the database servers
•Ensures that all network security groups (NSGs) are associated only with subnets
•Use application security groups to implement the solution
What is the minimum number of application security groups required?
A.1
B.2
C.4
D.8
Answer: B
Explanation:
The correct answer is B (2). Here's why:
To achieve the required security with Application Security Groups (ASGs) associated with subnets, we need to
define groups representing the web servers and database servers. NSG rules can then reference these ASGs
to control traffic flow.
We require one ASG to represent the web servers in VNet1 and another ASG to represent the database
servers in VNet2. We associate the VMs with the corresponding ASGs.
For database servers accepting only web server connections, an NSG rule on the subnet containing database
servers would allow inbound traffic from the web servers ASG and deny all other inbound traffic.
For web servers initiating connections only to database servers, an NSG rule on the subnet containing the web
servers would allow outbound traffic to the database servers ASG and deny all other outbound traffic.
Therefore, only two ASGs are needed: one for the web servers and one for the database servers. The NSG
rules associated with the respective subnets enforce the directional traffic flow between the groups.
Using fewer ASGs would make enforcing the distinct security requirements (web servers only connecting to
databases, and databases only accepting connections from web servers) impossible without compromising
security. Using more than two would be unnecessary complexity.
Further research:

Application Security Groups
Network Security Groups

Question: 245 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT -
Case Study -
This is a case study. Case studies are not timed separately. You can use as much exam time as you would like to
complete each case. However, there may be additional case studies and sections on this exam. You must manage
your time to ensure that you are able to complete all questions included on this exam in the time provided.
To answer the questions included in a case study, you will need to reference information that is provided in the
case study. Case studies might contain exhibits and other resources that provide more information about the
scenario that is described in the case study. Each question is independent of the other questions in this case study.
At the end of this case study, a review screen will appear. This screen allows you to review your answers and to
make changes before you move to the next section of the exam. After you begin a new section, you cannot return
to this section.
To start the case study -
To display the first question in this case study, click the Next button. Use the buttons in the left pane to explore
the content of the case study before you answer the questions. Clicking these buttons displays information such
as business requirements, existing environment, and problem statements. When you are ready to answer a
question, click the Question button to return to the question.
Overview -
Proseware, Inc. is a financial services company that has a main office in New York City and a branch office in San
Francisco.
Existing Environment. Hybrid Environment
Proseware has an on-premises Active Directory Domain Services (AD DS) forest named corp.proseware.com that
syncs with a Microsoft Entra tenant named proseware.com.
Proseware has an Azure subscription that is linked to proseware.com.
Proseware has an internal certification authority (CA).
Existing Environment. Network Infrastructure
The offices contain the resources shown in the following table.
NYCNet connects to Azure by using an ExpressRoute circuit.
SFONet connects to Azure by using a Site-to-Site (S2S) VPN.
Existing Environment. Azure Resources
The Azure subscription contains the virtual networks and subnets shown in the following table.

The subscription contains four virtual machines named VM1, VM2, VM3, and VM4. VM1 and VM2 host an app named
App1.
VM3 and VM4 host a web app named App2 that is accessed by using a FQDN of app2.proseware.com. Users
access app2.proseware.com by using HTTP or HTTPS.
VM1, VM2, and VM4 are connected to SpokeVNet.
The subscription contains Application Gateway resources shown in the following table.
The subscription contains an Azure Front Door Standard profile named FD1. FD1 contains a single origin group that
targets APPGW1 by using the default endpoint name.
HubVNet connects to NYCNet by using an ExpressRoute gateway named ERGW1.
Planned Changes and Requirements. Planned Changes
Proseware plans to implement the following changes:
•Deploy an Azure Private DNS Resolver named PRDNS1 to HubVNet and link PRDNS1 to SpokeVNet.
•Create a DNS forwarding ruleset named DNSRS1 and associate DNSRS1 with PRDNS1.
•Deploy Azure Virtual Network Manager and implement the following rules:
- Allow inbound connections on TCP port 3389 from the on-premises networks to SUBNET-JUMPHOSTS.
- Block inbound connections on TCP port 80 from the internet to SpokeVNet.
•Ensure that Azure Virtual Network Manager rules take precedence over conflicting NSG rules.
•Deploy two network virtual appliances (NVAs) named NVA1 and NVA2 to HubVNet.
•Deploy a gateway load balancer named LBGW1 to HubVNet.
•Configure LBGW1 to inspect traffic on TCP ports 443, 1433, and 1434 from LBS1 by using NVA1 and NVA2.
•Ensure that all the traffic to App2 is processed by using FD1.
Planned Changes and Requirements. Connectivity requirements
Proseware identifies the following connectivity requirements:

•Minimize the complexity of the Azure Virtual Network Manager deployment.
•Route traffic between NYCNet and SFONet via the ExpressRoute circuit and the S2S VPN.
•Ensure that remote users on Windows 11 devices can connect to HubVNet by using a Point-to-Site (P2S) VPN and
their proseware.com credentials.
Planned Changes and Requirements. Security requirements
Proseware identifies the following security requirements:
•Whenever possible, use the internal CA.
•Ensure that all connections routed via APPGW1 use end-to-end encryption.
•Ensure that user connections to Azure-hosted apps use end-to-end encryption.
•Ensure that all inbound internet traffic to app2.proseware.com is routed via FD1.
•Prevent devices that connect to NYCNet from accessing Azure services that use private endpoints.
•Enable the virtual machines that connect to HubVNet and SpokeVNet to access Azure services that use private
endpoints.
Planned Changes and Requirements. General requirements
Proseware identifies the following general requirements:
•Minimize the IP address space required to deploy platform-managed resources to the virtual networks.
•From SpokeVNet, resolve name resolution requests for the azure.proseware.com namespace and the
corp.proseware.com namespace by using PRDNS1.
•Whenever possible, minimize administrative effort.
You need to plan the deployment of LBGW1. The solution must support the planned changes.
What should you include in the solution? To answer, select the appropriate options in the answer area.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
Answer:
Explanation:
Minimum required number of load balancing rules:
1
An Azure Load Balancer distributes incoming network traffic among multiple healthy virtual machines or

instances. A "load balancing rule" defines how specific inbound traffic (based on protocol, frontend IP, port) is
distributed to a backend pool.
If you have a single service that needs to be load-balanced (e.g., HTTP traffic on port 80), you would typically
need at least 1 load balancing rule.
Configure LBGW1 to reference LBS1 by modifying the:
Backend pools.
This question implies a scenario where a Load Balancer (LBGW1 - likely an Azure Application Gateway or a
different Load Balancer type) needs to send traffic to another Load Balancer (LBS1 - likely an Azure Standard
Load Balancer). This is a common pattern in complex architectures, often involving a Web Application Firewall
(WAF) or advanced routing at the first layer, then distributing to backend services via a second load balancer.
Question: 246 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT
-
You have an Azure subscription that contains an Azure application gateway named AGW1.
You need to create a rewrite rule on AGW1 that will convert web requests as follows:
•Input: https://www.contoso.com/fashion/shirts
•Output: https://www.contoso.com/buy.aspx?category=fashion&product=shirts
The rewrite rule will have the following settings:
•Type of variable to check: Server variable
•Case-sensitive: No
•Operator: equal (=)
To what should you set Server variable and Pattern to match? To answer, select the appropriate options in the
answer area.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
Answer:

Explanation:
1. Server variable: uri_path
✅ Correct Choice: uri_path
Explanation: In Azure Application Gateway, you have several options to evaluate the incoming URL:
query_string: Includes only the part after the ? (e.g., id=123).
request_uri: Includes the entire URI (path + query string).
uri_path: Includes only the path portion (e.g., /images/logo.png).
Why it's used here: Most URL rewrite scenarios for this exam involve restructuring the folder path itself. Using
uri_path allows you to isolate the folders from any extra query parameters, making your regex pattern much
cleaner.
2. Pattern to match: /(.+)/(.+)
❌ Your Choice: /.*/.* (Incorrect)
✅ Correct Choice: /(.+)/(.+)
Explanation: To rewrite a URL effectively, you usually need to "capture" parts of the original path so you can
reuse them in the new path.
Capturing Groups ( ): In Regular Expressions (Regex), parentheses are used to create capturing groups. These
groups are saved as variables (like var_uri_path_1 and var_uri_path_2 ).
The Wildcard .+: This matches one or more characters.
Why your choice was incorrect: The pattern /.*/.* (or /.*/*) might match the path, but it doesn't capture it.
Without the parentheses (), the Application Gateway has no way to remember what was in those spots, so you
wouldn't be able to move those values into a new URL structure.

Question: 247 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription.
You need to deploy an App Service web app named App1 that will use an Azure application gateway. The estimated
usage for App1 is 250,000 persistent connections.
What is the minimum number of app instances you should configure?
A. 1
B. 10
C. 100
D. 1000
Answer: B
Explanation:
Technical Justification for Correct Answer B (10 app instances)
To determine the minimum number of app instances required for an App Service web app (App1) that will
utilize an Azure Application Gateway, handling an estimated 250,000 persistent connections, we must
consider the limitations and best practices of both Azure App Service and Application Gateway.
Why B (10) is the Correct Answer:
Azure Application Gateway Consideration: While the Application Gateway can handle a large number of
connections (theoretically up to 650,000 new connections per second for v2 SKUs), its primary role here is to
frontend and distribute traffic to the App Service. The critical factor is the backend (App Service) capacity.
Azure App Service (Web App) Limitations and Best Practices:
Persistent Connections Limit: Each App Service instance (or "instance" in the context of App Service plans)
can theoretically handle up to 25,000 persistent connections, based on Microsoft's general guidance for
scaling considerations. This is not a hard limit but a guideline for planning.
Calculation for App1:
Estimated Usage: 250,000 persistent connections
Connections per Instance (Guideline): 25,000
Minimum Instances Required: 250,000 / 25,000 = 10
Why Other Options are Less Suitable:
A (1): Completely insufficient given the estimated 250,000 persistent connections, far exceeding the guideline
of 25,000 connections per instance.
C (100): While this would provide a significant buffer, it is far beyond the minimum required based on the
provided estimate, leading to unnecessary costs without a justified scalability need.
D (1000): Excessively high, with no scalability justification provided in the scenario to warrant such a large
number of instances, resulting in substantial unnecessary expenditure.
Conclusion:Given the estimated usage and leveraging the guideline of 25,000 persistent connections per App
Service instance, 10 instances (Option B) are the minimum required to support App1's anticipated load
efficiently and cost-effectively.
References

1. Azure App Service Scaling Guidance - https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/app-service/manage-
scale
2. Azure Application Gateway Capabilities - https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/application-
gateway/overview
Question: 248 CertyIQ
You plan to implement an Azure virtual network that will contain 10 virtual subnets. The subnets will use IPv6
addresses. Each subnet will host up to 200 load-balanced virtual machines.
You need to recommend a load balancing solution for the virtual network. The solution must meet the following
requirements:
•The virtual machines and the load balancer must be accessible only from the virtual network.
•Costs must be minimized.
What should you include in the recommendation?
A. Azure Application Gateway v2
B. Azure Standard Front Door
C. Azure Standard Load Balancer
D. Azure Application Gateway v1
Answer: C
Explanation:
Technical Justification for Recommending Azure Standard Load Balancer (Option C)
Why Option C (Azure Standard Load Balancer) is the Best Choice:
Requirement: Virtual Machines and Load Balancer Accessible Only from the Virtual Network: Azure
Standard Load Balancer can be configured in an internal load balancing mode, ensuring that the load
balancer's frontend IP is only reachable from within the virtual network, fulfilling this requirement.
Requirement: Costs Minimized: Among the options provided, Azure Standard Load Balancer is the most cost-
effective solution for load balancing needs that do not require advanced web application firewall (WAF)
capabilities, URL-based routing, or global traffic distribution, which are not mentioned as requirements.
IPv6 Support: Azure Standard Load Balancer supports IPv6 addresses for the virtual machines and can
handle IPv6 traffic, aligning with the plan for IPv6-based subnets.
Scalability for 200 Load-Balanced Virtual Machines per Subnet: The Standard Load Balancer is capable of
handling a large number of backend instances (virtual machines in this case) across multiple subnets, making
it suitable for the planned deployment of up to 200 virtual machines per subnet.
Why Other Options are Less Suitable:
Option A (Azure Application Gateway v2): While it supports IPv6 and can be used for internal load balancing,
it is more costly than necessary for the given requirements since it includes advanced WAF capabilities and
more complex routing features not specified as needed.
Option B (Azure Standard Front Door): Designed for global traffic distribution and edge caching, it does not
fit the requirement of being accessible only from the virtual network. It also incurs higher costs due to its
global nature and features not required by the scenario.
Option D (Azure Application Gateway v1): Similar to v2, it has more features than needed (though less than
v2), increasing cost. Additionally, v1 might have limitations or be deprecated in favor of v2 for new
deployments, making it less ideal for future-proofing.
Recommendation:Include Azure Standard Load Balancer in the recommendation for the load balancing

solution in the Azure virtual network.
References:
Microsoft Azure Documentation: Azure Load Balancer
Microsoft Azure Pricing: Load Balancer
Question: 249 CertyIQ
You have an Azure virtual machine named VM1.
You need to capture all the network traffic of VM1 by using Azure Network Watcher.
To which locations can the capture be written?
A. blob storage only
B. blob storage, a file path on VM1, and a premium storage account
C. a file path on VM1 only
D. blob storage and a file path on VM1 only
E. blob storage and a premium storage account only
F. a premium storage account only
Answer: D
Explanation:
The correct answer is D: blob storage and a file path on VM1 only. This stems from how Azure Network
Watcher's packet capture feature operates.
Azure Network Watcher allows you to create packet captures to analyze network traffic to and from virtual
machines. These captures can be stored for later analysis, helping in troubleshooting network issues, security
investigations, or performance monitoring. The capture itself is generally stored as a .cap or .pcap file, a
standard format for network packet data.
The destinations available for writing the packet capture data are limited to ensure data integrity and
accessibility within the Azure environment. Blob storage is a suitable destination because it offers durable
and scalable storage for large files like packet captures. It also integrates well with other Azure services for
analysis.
Writing directly to a file path on the VM (VM1 in this case) is also a viable option, especially for smaller
captures or immediate analysis. However, this option consumes storage resources on the VM itself and may
not be suitable for long-term storage or large captures. It also introduces a single point of failure if the VM
experiences issues.
Premium storage accounts, while offering high performance, are not directly supported as a destination for
Network Watcher packet captures. Network Watcher requires a standard blob storage account for capture
files. While you might potentially move data from a standard storage account to a premium one after the
capture, the initial capture cannot be written directly to premium storage. The choice of using standard blob
storage aims to provide cost-effectiveness for storing the capture data.
Therefore, the supported locations for capturing the network traffic of VM1 using Azure Network Watcher are
standard blob storage and a file path on VM1. The option to write to a premium storage account is not a direct
feature offered by Azure Network Watcher.
Refer to the official Microsoft Azure documentation for further details:

Azure Network Watcher packet capture
Question: 250 CertyIQ
You have an Azure virtual network that contains the subnets shown in the following table.
You deploy an Azure firewall to AzureFirewallSubnet. You route all traffic from Subnet2 through the firewall.
You need to ensure that all the hosts on Subnet2 can access an external site located at https://*.contoso.com.
What should you do?
A. In a firewall policy, create a DNAT rule.
B. Create a network security group (NSG) and associate the NSG to Subnet2.
C. In a firewall policy, create a network rule.
D. In a firewall policy, create an application rule.
Answer: D
Explanation:
D. In a firewall policy, create an application rule.
Since you have deployed an Azure Firewall and configured Subnet2 to route all traffic through it, you need to
allow traffic specifically to https://*.contoso.com.
Azure Firewall supports two types of rules for controlling outbound traffic:
Network Rules (Layer 3 & 4) – Control IP-based traffic (IP addresses, ports, protocols).
Application Rules (Layer 7) – Control access to FQDNs (Fully Qualified Domain Names) for web traffic
(HTTP/HTTPS).
