https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/tutorial-firewall-deploy-portal
Question: 251 CertyIQ
You have an Azure Web Application Firewall (WAF) policy in prevention mode that is associated to an Azure Front
Door instance.
You need to configure the policy to meet the following requirements:
✑ Log all connections from Australia.
✑ Deny all connections from New Zealand.
✑ Deny all further connections from a network of 131.107.100.0/24 if there are more than 100 connections during
one minute.
What is the minimum number of objects you should create?
A. three custom rules that each has one condition
B. one custom rule that has three conditions

C. one custom rule that has one condition
D. one rule that has two conditions and another rule that has one condition
Answer: A
Explanation:
The correct answer is A: three custom rules that each has one condition. Here's why:
Each requirement necessitates a distinct action (logging, denying, rate limiting) based on different conditions.
Azure WAF policies are most effectively managed by isolating these distinct requirements into separate rules
for clarity and maintainability.
1. Log Connections from Australia: This requires a custom rule with a geo-location condition set to
Australia and an action to log the request. This can be achieved using the match conditions where you
specify the geo location.
2. Deny Connections from New Zealand: Similar to the first requirement, this needs a separate custom
rule with a geo-location condition set to New Zealand and an action to deny the request. Again using
match conditions.
3. Rate Limiting for 131.107.100.0/24: This involves a rate limiting rule. This also needs a different
custom rule that monitors requests from the IP address range 131.107.100.0/24, and applies the rate
limit using action type Block. If the number of requests exceeds 100 in one minute, further
connections from that IP range are blocked.Combining these disparate requirements into a single
complex rule would reduce readability and make troubleshooting more challenging. Creating
multiple conditions within the same rule would force the same action on all conditions, which doesn't
satisfy the individual logging, denying, and rate-limiting requirements.
Therefore, the most efficient and manageable approach is to create three separate custom rules, each
handling a specific requirement with its own condition. This adheres to the principle of separation of concerns,
facilitating easier modification, troubleshooting, and overall policy management.
Here are some relevant links for further research:
Azure Web Application Firewall custom rules
Azure Web Application Firewall rate limiting
Azure Front Door WAF policy settings
Question: 252 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains multiple virtual machines in the West US Azure region.
You need to use Traffic Analytics.
Which two resources should you create? Each correct answer presents part of the solution. (Choose two.)
NOTE: Each correct answer selection is worth one point.
A. an Azure Monitor workbook
B. a Log Analytics workspace
C. a storage account
D. an Azure Sentinel workspace
E. an Azure Monitor data collection rule

Answer: BC
Explanation:
The correct answer is B and C: a Log Analytics workspace and a storage account. Here's why:
Traffic Analytics is a cloud-based solution that provides visibility into user and application activity on your
cloud networks. It analyzes network traffic flow logs to provide insights into traffic patterns across Azure
regions and subscriptions. To achieve this, it relies on two key resources:
1. Log Analytics Workspace: Traffic Analytics uses a Log Analytics workspace to store and process the
network traffic flow logs. The data collected from Network Security Groups (NSGs) or virtual network
flow logs is ingested into this workspace, where it's then analyzed to generate reports and
visualizations. The Log Analytics workspace acts as the central repository for the data used by Traffic
Analytics.
2. Storage Account: While Log Analytics Workspace is the primary repository for storing processed
traffic analytics data, before the data lands in the workspace, the raw network flow logs, specifically
Network Security Group (NSG) flow logs, are stored in an Azure Storage Account. Traffic Analytics
requires access to these raw flow logs to perform its analysis. The NSG flow logs are written to the
storage account, and Traffic Analytics then reads these logs to extract relevant information. This
storage account needs to be accessible by the Traffic Analytics solution.
Option A is incorrect because Azure Monitor workbooks are used for creating interactive visual reports but
they don't store the necessary raw or processed flow log data needed for traffic analysis. Option D is
incorrect; Azure Sentinel is a SIEM/SOAR solution, not directly involved in storing or processing network flow
logs for Traffic Analytics in the same way as Log Analytics workspaces. Option E is incorrect because while
data collection rules define the data sources and destinations for Azure Monitor, they are not sufficient on
their own. You still need a Log Analytics workspace to store and process the data.
Therefore, a Log Analytics workspace to store and process the data, coupled with a storage account for raw
NSG flow log storage, are essential components for implementing Traffic Analytics.
Here are authoritative links for further research:
Traffic Analytics overview: Provides a general overview of Traffic Analytics.
Configure Traffic Analytics: Details the steps to configure Traffic Analytics.
NSG flow logs: Describes NSG flow logs and how they are stored in a storage account.
Log Analytics Workspace overview: Explains the function and purpose of a Log Analytics Workspace.

Question: 255 CertyIQ
You have a hybrid environment that uses ExpressRoute to connect an on-premises network and Azure.
You need to log the uptime and the latency of the connection periodically by using an Azure virtual machine and an
on-premises virtual machine.
What should you use?
A. Azure Monitor
B. IP flow verify
C. Connection Monitor
D. Azure Internet Analyzer
Answer: C
Explanation:
The correct answer is C. Connection Monitor. Here's why:
Connection Monitor is specifically designed for monitoring the connectivity between endpoints in a hybrid
environment like the one described. It allows you to define test groups with specific source and destination
endpoints (in this case, the Azure VM and the on-premises VM). You can configure tests to run periodically and
measure metrics like packet loss, latency (round-trip time), and hop count. Connection Monitor then visually
represents these metrics, helping you track the uptime and performance of your ExpressRoute connection. It

provides detailed reports and allows for alerting based on predefined thresholds.
Azure Monitor (A) is a broader monitoring service encompassing a wider range of resources, including VMs,
applications, and logs. While it can collect metrics related to network traffic, it isn't specifically tailored for
monitoring end-to-end connection performance between specific endpoints across hybrid environments. It
requires more complex configurations than Connection Monitor to achieve the desired monitoring for the
specific problem.
IP flow verify (B) is a network diagnostic tool that helps identify if traffic is blocked by network security
groups (NSGs) or user-defined routes. While helpful for troubleshooting connectivity issues, it doesn't
continuously monitor uptime and latency over time. It is more of a point-in-time diagnostic tool.
Azure Internet Analyzer (D) focuses on understanding how your users experience your internet applications
from different locations around the world. It is meant for analyzing the performance of your public-facing
applications and CDN choices. It is not designed to monitor the connectivity between on-premises and Azure
resources.
Therefore, Connection Monitor is the most suitable solution for periodically logging the uptime and latency of
the ExpressRoute connection between an Azure VM and an on-premises VM, as it's built for this type of
network performance monitoring.
Authoritative Links:
Azure Connection Monitor Documentation: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/network-
watcher/connection-monitor
Azure Monitor Overview: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/azure-monitor/overview
IP Flow Verify: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/network-watcher/network-watcher-ip-flow-verify-
overview
Azure Internet Analyzer: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/internet-analyzer/internet-analyzer-
overview
Question: 256 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains the following resources:
✑ A virtual network named Vnet1
✑ Two subnets named subnet1 and AzureFirewallSubnet
✑ A public Azure Firewall named FW1
✑ A route table named RT1 that is associated to Subnet1
✑ A rule routing of 0.0.0.0/0 to FW1 in RT1
After deploying 10 servers that run Windows Server to Subnet1, you discover that none of the virtual machines
were activated.
You need to ensure that the virtual machines can be activated.
What should you do?
A. On FW1, create an outbound service tag rule for AzureCloud.
B. On FW1, create an outbound network rule that allows traffic to the Azure Key Management Service (KMS).
C. Deploy a NAT gateway.
D. To Subnet1, associate a network security group (NSG) that allows outbound access to port 1688.
Answer: B
Explanation:
The correct answer is B. Here's why:

Windows Server activation typically relies on the Key Management Service (KMS). For virtual machines in
Azure to activate, they need to be able to communicate with Microsoft's KMS servers. Since all outbound
traffic from Subnet1 is routed to the Azure Firewall (FW1) via RT1 (0.0.0.0/0 -> FW1), the firewall needs to
explicitly allow this KMS traffic.
Option A, creating an outbound service tag rule for AzureCloud, might seem relevant but is generally too
broad. It would allow access to all Azure services, which isn't necessary or secure just for activation. It's better
to specifically allow KMS traffic.
Option C, deploying a NAT gateway, would provide outbound internet access for VMs in Subnet1. However, it
doesn't inherently guarantee activation if the firewall is blocking KMS. The firewall still needs to be
configured to allow the KMS traffic even with a NAT gateway. NAT gateway provides SNAT and egress, it
does not allow the communication if firewall rules are restrictive.
Option D, associating a network security group (NSG) that allows outbound access to port 1688, is not the
correct approach because the traffic is already routed via RT1 to FW1. NSGs act as the first layer of defense
within the subnet, but in this case, the route table directing traffic to the firewall supersedes the NSG for
internet bound traffic. NSGs are helpful, but the firewall is the primary control point in this scenario. Also,
NSGs are stateful, so outbound allow also allows return traffic.
Therefore, the most precise and secure solution is to create an outbound network rule on the Azure Firewall
(FW1) specifically allowing traffic to the Azure Key Management Service (KMS). This ensures that only KMS-
related traffic is allowed, minimizing the attack surface. This rule will permit the VMs to contact KMS for
activation without unnecessarily opening up broader access to AzureCloud.
Further research:
Azure Firewall Network Rules: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/network-rules
Azure Key Management Service (KMS) Activation: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows-server/get-
started/kms-client-activation-keys
Azure Service Tags: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/service-tags-overview

Question: 258 CertyIQ
You have an Azure virtual network that contains a subnet named Subnet1. Subnet1 is associated to a network
security group (NSG) named NSG1. NSG1 blocks all outbound traffic that is not allowed explicitly.
Subnet1 contains virtual machines that must communicate with the Azure Cosmos DB service.
You need to create an outbound security rule in NSG1 to enable the virtual machines to connect to Azure Cosmos
DB.
What should you include in the solution?
A. a service tag
B. a service endpoint policy
C. a subnet delegation
D. an application security group
Answer: A
Explanation:
The correct answer is A. a service tag. Here's a detailed justification:
Service tags represent a group of IP address prefixes for a given Azure service. Using service tags simplifies
NSG rule creation and maintenance. Instead of manually finding and updating the IP addresses used by Azure
Cosmos DB, you can specify the "CosmosDB" service tag as the destination in an outbound NSG rule. Azure
automatically manages the IP addresses associated with the "CosmosDB" tag, ensuring your virtual machines
can always reach the service without manual intervention when Cosmos DB IP ranges change.
Service endpoint policies are designed to filter outbound traffic to specific Azure Storage accounts. While
related, they are not the appropriate solution for Cosmos DB. Subnet delegation allows you to assign a
specific Azure service to a subnet, allowing that service to create its own network interfaces and resources
within that subnet. This is irrelevant to the requirement of allowing outbound traffic to Cosmos DB.
Application security groups (ASGs) are used to group virtual machines with similar security requirements,
enabling you to apply NSG rules to these groups rather than individual VMs or subnets. While ASGs can
enhance NSG management, they don't directly address the need to allow communication to Cosmos DB. Using
the "CosmosDB" service tag directly in the NSG rule allows for a streamlined and centrally managed
approach to allow outbound traffic. This removes the burden of manually managing all the IP addresses used
by Cosmos DB.
Therefore, selecting a service tag like "CosmosDB" is the best solution because it simplifies configuration and
allows Azure to manage the IP addresses of the Cosmos DB service, ensuring reliable connectivity.
Authoritative links:
Azure service tags overview
Network security groups
Azure Cosmos DB network security

Question: 259 CertyIQ
Your company has offices in Montreal, Seattle, and Paris. The outbound traffic from each office originates from a
specific public IP address.
You create an Azure Front Door instance named FD1 that has Azure Web Application Firewall (WAF) enabled. You
configure a WAF policy named Policy1 that has a rule named Rule1. Rule1 applies a rate limit of 100 requests for
traffic that originates from the office in Montreal.
You need to apply a rate limit of 100 requests for traffic that originates from each office.
What should you do?
A. Modify the rate limit threshold of Rule1.
B. Create two additional associations.
C. Modify the conditions of Rule1.
D. Modify the rule type of Rule1.
Answer: C
Explanation:
The correct answer is C. Modify the conditions of Rule1.
Here's a detailed justification:
The goal is to apply a rate limit of 100 requests specifically for traffic originating from each of the three
offices (Montreal, Seattle, and Paris). Currently, Rule1 is only configured to apply the rate limit to traffic
originating from Montreal. Simply changing the rate limit threshold (Option A) won't solve the problem
because it would apply the same rate limit across all traffic, regardless of origin. Creating two additional
associations (Option B) is not relevant in the context of rate limiting rules within WAF policies. Changing the
rule type (Option D) is also not the correct approach as the existing rate limit rule type is appropriate.
The key is to modify the conditions of Rule1 (Option C). The conditions define when the rule should be
triggered. Currently, it's likely that Rule1 has a condition that specifies "source IP address equals [Montreal's
public IP address]". To achieve the desired outcome, you must add conditions to Rule1 for the public IP
addresses of the Seattle and Paris offices as well. These conditions can be combined using a logical OR
operator, meaning the rate limit will be applied if the request originates from Montreal OR Seattle OR Paris.
Essentially, you are expanding the scope of Rule1 to include all three offices.
By modifying the conditions, the single rate limit rule (Rule1) effectively applies to traffic from all three
specified locations, meeting the requirement without creating redundant rules. This is the most efficient and
straightforward approach.
Here are some authoritative links for further research on Azure Front Door and WAF policies:
Azure Front Door WAF: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/web-application-firewall/afw-overview
WAF Rule Conditions: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/web-application-firewall/afw-custom-rules
Azure Front Door documentation: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/frontdoor/
Question: 260 CertyIQ
You have an Azure virtual network named Vnet1.
You need to ensure that the virtual machines in Vnet1 can access only the Azure SQL resources in the East US
Azure region. The virtual machines must be prevented from accessing any Azure Storage resources.
Which two outbound network security group (NSG) rules should you create? Each correct answer presents part of
the solution.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.

A. a deny rule that has a source of VirtualNetwork and a destination of Sql
B. an allow rule that has the IP address range of Vnet1 as the source and destination of Sql.EastUS
C. a deny rule that has a source of VirtualNetwork and a destination of 168.63.129.0/24
D. a deny rule that has the IP address range of Vnet1 as the source and destination of Storage
Answer: BD
Explanation:
The correct answer is BD. Here's a detailed justification:
B. An allow rule that has the IP address range of Vnet1 as the source and destination of Sql.EastUS: This
rule allows the virtual machines within Vnet1 to reach Azure SQL resources specifically in the East US region.
Network Security Groups (NSGs) act as virtual firewalls, controlling inbound and outbound traffic. By
specifying the IP address range of Vnet1 as the source and Sql.EastUS as the destination, you're whitelisting
traffic destined for Azure SQL databases located in East US from your virtual network. The destination service
tag Sql.EastUS represents the IP address prefixes used by the Azure SQL service in that region. This enables
connectivity only to the desired SQL resources.
D. A deny rule that has the IP address range of Vnet1 as the source and destination of Storage: This rule
prevents the virtual machines in Vnet1 from accessing Azure Storage resources. NSGs process rules in order
of priority. A deny rule overrides any implicit or lower-priority allow rules. By setting the source as the Vnet1 IP
range and the destination as Storage service tag, you are explicitly blocking all outbound traffic from VMs in
Vnet1 that attempt to connect to Azure Storage endpoints. This fulfills the requirement of preventing access
to Azure Storage.
Option A is incorrect because there is no "Sql" destination in NSG. Option C is incorrect because
168.63.129.0/24 is the Virtualized Host IP address for Azure infrastructure, not all Storage Accounts.
In summary, the allow rule permits access to Azure SQL in East US, and the deny rule prevents access to
Azure Storage, fulfilling the requirements.
Supporting Links:
Azure Network Security Groups: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/network-security-
groups-overview
Azure Service Tags: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/service-tags-overview
Question: 261 CertyIQ
Note: This question is part of a series of questions that present the same scenario. Each question in the series
contains a unique solution that might meet the stated goals. Some question sets might have more than one correct
solution, while others might not have a correct solution.
After you answer a question in this section, you will NOT be able to return to it. As a result, these questions will not
appear in the review screen.
You have an Azure subscription that contains the following resources:
✑ A virtual network named Vnet1
✑ A subnet named Subnet1 in Vnet1
✑ A virtual machine named VM1 that connects to Subnet1
✑ Three storage accounts named storage1, storage2, and storage3
You need to ensure that VM1 can access storage1. VM1 must be prevented from accessing any other storage
accounts.
Solution: You configure the firewall on storage1 to only accept connections from Vnet1.
Does this meet the goal?
A. Yes

B. No
Answer: B
Explanation:
The provided solution does not fully meet the goal because simply configuring the firewall on storage1 to
accept connections from Vnet1 is insufficient to prevent VM1 from accessing other storage accounts (storage2
and storage3). While the firewall setting on storage1 allows access, it doesn't actively block access to the
other storage accounts.
VM1 can still potentially reach storage2 and storage3 if the network security group (NSG) associated with
Subnet1 (or the VM's NIC) allows outbound traffic to the internet or to Azure Storage service in general. Azure
Storage endpoints are usually public. If an NSG rule doesn't specifically block outbound traffic to these
endpoints (or if the default outbound rule is still in effect), VM1 will be able to connect to them.
To definitively prevent access to storage2 and storage3, more restrictive measures are needed, such as:
1. Service Endpoints/Service Tags: Configure a service endpoint on Subnet1 for Microsoft.Storage.
Configure the firewall of storage1 to only allow access from this endpoint. Then, change the NSG to
block outbound access to storage accounts except the service endpoint.
2. Network Security Group (NSG) Rules: Create NSG rules on Subnet1 that explicitly block outbound
traffic to the storage endpoints of storage2 and storage3. Specifically, block traffic to the regional
Azure Storage endpoints or use Service Tags to accomplish this.
3. Azure Private Link: The most secure solution is to use Azure Private Link to expose storage account
privately through the VNet, as this completely removes the public endpoint.
Therefore, configuring the firewall on only storage1 does not guarantee that VM1 will be prevented from
accessing storage2 and storage3. The goal requires an active block on access to the undesired storage
accounts, which this solution fails to provide.
References:
Azure Storage firewall configuration
Azure Network Security Groups
Azure Service Endpoints
Azure Private Link
Azure Service Tags
Question: 262 CertyIQ
Note: This question is part of a series of questions that present the same scenario. Each question in the series
contains a unique solution that might meet the stated goals. Some question sets might have more than one correct
solution, while others might not have a correct solution.
After you answer a question in this section, you will NOT be able to return to it. As a result, these questions will not
appear in the review screen.
You have an Azure subscription that contains the following resources:
✑ A virtual network named Vnet1
✑ A subnet named Subnet1 in Vnet1
✑ A virtual machine named VM1 that connects to Subnet1
✑ Three storage accounts named storage1, storage2, and storage3
You need to ensure that VM1 can access storage1. VM1 must be prevented from accessing any other storage
accounts.
Solution: You create a network security group (NSG) and associate the NSG to Subnet1.
Does this meet the goal?

A. Yes
B. No
Answer: B
Explanation:
The proposed solution of creating a network security group (NSG) and associating it with Subnet1 will not
solely meet the goal of allowing VM1 to access storage1 while preventing access to storage2 and storage3.
While NSGs can control network traffic, they operate at the IP address and port level. They cannot
differentiate between specific storage accounts based on their names or account properties directly.
To achieve the goal, more granular control is required. One approach is to use Service Endpoints for Azure
Storage. Service Endpoints extend the virtual network private address space and provide direct connectivity
to Azure services over an optimized route over the Azure backbone network. By enabling a service endpoint
for Azure Storage on Subnet1, traffic destined for Azure Storage will be routed directly and securely.
Then, you can configure the Azure Storage firewall for storage1 to only allow traffic from Vnet1/Subnet1. This
will effectively restrict access to storage1 to resources within Subnet1, which includes VM1. Since storage2
and storage3 will not have this firewall rule, VM1 will be blocked from accessing them.
Alternatively, Private Endpoints provide a network interface with a private IP address from within your VNet
for your Azure Storage account. This effectively brings the storage service directly into your VNet. However,
using Private Endpoints will require DNS configuration and would be an overkill in this case.
Simply using an NSG to block outbound traffic from VM1 to the public IP addresses of storage2 and storage3
would not be a robust solution as the IP addresses could change, and it does not guarantee that only storage1
is accessible. Furthermore, it does not leverage the secure, direct routing offered by Service Endpoints.
Therefore, the suggested solution using only an NSG does not fulfill the complete requirements, and a more
refined approach using service endpoints and storage account firewalls is needed.
References:
Azure Storage firewalls and virtual networks: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-
us/azure/storage/common/storage-network-security
Azure Service Endpoints: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/service-endpoints-
overview
Azure Private Endpoints: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/private-link/private-endpoint-overview
Question: 263 CertyIQ
Note: This question is part of a series of questions that present the same scenario. Each question in the series
contains a unique solution that might meet the stated goals. Some question sets might have more than one correct
solution, while others might not have a correct solution.
After you answer a question in this section, you will NOT be able to return to it. As a result, these questions will not
appear in the review screen.
You have an Azure subscription that contains the following resources:
✑ A virtual network named Vnet1
A subnet named Subnet1 in Vnet1 -
✑ A virtual machine named VM1 that connects to Subnet1
✑ Three storage accounts named storage1, storage2, and storage3
You need to ensure that VM1 can access storage1. VM1 must be prevented from accessing any other storage
accounts.
Solution: You create a network security group (NSG). You configure a service tag for Microsoft.Storage and link the
tag to Subnet1.

Does this meet the goal?
A. Yes
B. No
Answer: B
Explanation:
B. No
Service Tag for Microsoft.Storage: Configuring a service tag for Microsoft.Storage in a network security
group (NSG) and linking it to Subnet1 would allow VM1 to access all storage accounts within the Azure
Storage service, not just storage1. This does not meet the requirement of preventing VM1 from accessing any
other storage accounts.
Preventing Access to Other Storage Accounts: To ensure that VM1 can only access storage1 and no other
storage accounts, you would need to implement more specific network controls, such as using a Virtual
Network Service Endpoint for Azure Storage and configuring firewall rules on the storage account itself to
restrict access to only the IP address or virtual network associated with VM1.
Question: 264 CertyIQ
You need to use Traffic Analytics to monitor the usage of applications deployed to Azure virtual machines.
Which Azure Network Watcher feature should you implement first?
A. NSG flow logs
B. IP flow verify
C. Connection monitor
D. Packet capture
Answer: A
Explanation:
The correct answer is A, NSG flow logs. Traffic Analytics directly consumes NSG flow logs as its data source.
To use Traffic Analytics, you must first enable NSG flow logs on the network security groups (NSGs)
associated with your Azure virtual machines. NSG flow logs record information about traffic flowing through
an NSG, including source and destination IP addresses, ports, and allowed/denied status.
Traffic Analytics then processes these flow logs to provide insights into network traffic patterns, application
usage, and potential security threats. Without enabling NSG flow logs, Traffic Analytics will have no data to
analyze and cannot provide the desired monitoring capabilities.
IP flow verify helps diagnose connectivity issues between a virtual machine and an endpoint. Connection
Monitor monitors connectivity between endpoints over time, but it doesn't provide the comprehensive traffic
analysis offered by Traffic Analytics. Packet capture is used for deeper packet-level analysis, which isn't the
primary requirement for initial setup of Traffic Analytics. Thus, enabling NSG flow logs is the foundational
step to begin leveraging Traffic Analytics for application usage monitoring.
Further reading:

Azure Network Watcher Traffic Analytics Overview: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/network-
watcher/traffic-analytics/traffic-analytics-overview
NSG Flow Logs: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/network-watcher/network-watcher-nsg-flow-logs-
overview

Question: 268 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains a user named Admin1 and a resource group named RG1.
RG1 contains an Azure Network Watcher instance named NW1.
You need to ensure that Admin1 can place a lock on NW1. The solution must use the principle of least privilege.
Which role should you assign to Admin1?

A. User Access Administrator
B. Resource Policy Contributor
C. Network Contributor
D. Monitoring Contributor
Answer: A
Explanation:
The correct answer is A, User Access Administrator. Here's why:
The goal is to grant Admin1 the ability to place a lock on the NW1 Network Watcher resource within RG1,
adhering to the principle of least privilege. Resource locks are managed through Azure Resource Manager
(ARM). ARM controls access to Azure resources.
The User Access Administrator role is specifically designed to manage access to Azure resources. This role
allows users to grant access to other users, manage Azure roles, and, crucially, manage resource locks.
Granting a lock fundamentally modifies access rights, as it restricts who can delete or modify a resource.
Option B, Resource Policy Contributor, primarily focuses on creating and managing Azure Policies. Azure
Policies enforce organizational standards and assess compliance at scale. While important for governance,
they don't directly control resource locking.
Option C, Network Contributor, provides permissions to manage network resources, such as virtual networks,
subnets, network interfaces, etc. However, it doesn't include the rights needed to manage resource locks.
Network Watcher itself is a network resource, but the locking mechanism is separate from the actual network
configuration.
Option D, Monitoring Contributor, allows viewing and managing monitoring data. This role is geared towards
understanding the performance and health of Azure resources and services, not controlling access through
locks.
The User Access Administrator is the most appropriate role because it provides the granular permissions
needed to manage access, including resource locks, without granting broader, unnecessary permissions like
the ability to manage network configurations. Using the User Access Administrator role aligns with the
principle of least privilege by granting only the rights necessary to place a lock.
Further reading on Azure RBAC and resource locking:
Azure role-based access control (Azure RBAC): https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/role-based-access-
control/overview
Azure Resource Locks: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/azure-resource-manager/management/lock-
resources
User Access Administrator Role: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/role-based-access-control/built-in-
roles#user-access-administrator
Question: 269 CertyIQ
You have a network security group named NSG1.
You need to enable network security group (NS) flow logs for NSG1. The solution must support retention policies.
What should you create first?
A. A standard general-purpose v2 Azure Storage account
B. An Azure Log Analytics workspace

C. A standard general-purpose v1 Azure Storage account
D. A premium Block blobs Azure Storage account
Answer: A
Explanation:
The correct answer is A. A standard general-purpose v2 Azure Storage account.
NSG Flow Logs capture network traffic data passing through an NSG. This data needs a destination to be
stored for analysis and auditing. While Azure Monitor and Log Analytics workspaces are crucial for analyzing
and visualizing the logs, they aren't the initial storage location when retention policies are required.
Azure Storage accounts provide durable, scalable, and secure storage for various data types. For NSG Flow
Logs with retention policies, a general-purpose v2 storage account is the recommended and cost-effective
solution. General-purpose v2 accounts support the lifecycle management features needed for retention,
meaning that after a defined period (e.g., 30 days), the logs can be automatically deleted or archived. General-
purpose v1 accounts are older and don't offer the same level of features and cost optimization as v2. Premium
Block Blob Storage accounts are optimized for high-throughput, low-latency blob storage, and are more
expensive than necessary for the relatively infrequent write operations of NSG Flow Logs. Therefore, they are
not an optimal choice when retention policies are the key requirement.
To summarize, creating a general-purpose v2 Azure Storage account first allows you to store the NSG flow
logs and configure retention policies to manage the log data's lifecycle effectively, which is directly aligned
with the question's requirements. The logs are then stored in this account, and you can analyze them using
tools like Azure Network Watcher or Log Analytics workspace.
References:
NSG Flow Logs Overview
Azure Storage Account Overview
Configure NSG Flow Logs
Question: 270 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains the following resources:
• A virtual network named Vnet1
• Two subnets named subnet1 and AzureFirewallSubnet
• A public Azure Firewall named FW1
• A route table named RT1 that is associated to Subnet1
• A rule routing of 0.0.0.0/0 to FW1 in RT1
After deploying 10 servers that run Windows Server to Subnet1, you discover that none of the virtual machines
were activated.
You need to ensure that the virtual machines can be activated.
What should you do?
A. On FW1, create an outbound network rule that allows traffic to the Azure Key Management Service (KMS).
B. On FW1, create an outbound service tag rule for Azure Cloud.
C. Deploy a NAT gateway.
D. Deploy an application security group that allows outbound traffic to 1688.
Answer: A
Explanation:

The correct answer is A. On FW1, create an outbound network rule that allows traffic to the Azure Key
Management Service (KMS).
Here's a detailed justification:
Windows Server virtual machines activated in Azure require access to the Azure Key Management Service
(KMS) servers for activation. By default, Azure Firewall blocks all outbound traffic unless explicitly permitted.
Since the VMs in Subnet1 cannot activate, it indicates that the traffic to the KMS servers is being blocked by
FW1. The route table RT1 directs all outbound traffic (0.0.0.0/0) from Subnet1 to FW1.
To enable activation, you must create a network rule in FW1 that allows outbound traffic to the KMS servers.
This rule should specify the destination IP addresses or FQDNs associated with the Azure KMS service.
Without this rule, the VMs cannot reach the KMS servers, and activation fails. Option B is not the best option
because it is too broad and can cause security risks. Options C is used when you want to hide IP address. and
D is not suitable because it's related to internal network configurations and KMS activation in the cloud.
Therefore, creating a network rule specifically for KMS traffic on FW1 is the most direct and secure solution to
resolve the activation issue.
Here are some authoritative links for further research:
Azure Firewall rules: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/rule-processing
Azure KMS Activation: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows-server/get-started/vm-activation-
troubleshooting
Azure Key Management Service (KMS): https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/key-vault/
Question: 271 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains a virtual network named Vnet1. Vnet1 contains a virtual machine
named VM1 and an Azure firewall named FW1.
You have an Azure Firewall Policy named FP1 that is associated to FW1.
You need to ensure that RDP requests to the public IP address of FW1 route to VM1.
What should you configure on FP1?
A. a network rule
B. URL filtering
C. a DNAT rule
D. an application rule
Answer: C
Explanation:
The correct answer is C. a DNAT rule.
DNAT (Destination Network Address Translation) is specifically designed to translate the destination IP
address and port of incoming traffic. In this scenario, the goal is to forward RDP requests (typically on port
3389) arriving at the Azure Firewall's public IP address to the internal IP address of VM1. DNAT rules within
the Azure Firewall Policy can achieve this by modifying the destination IP address of the incoming traffic from
the firewall's public IP to VM1's private IP address. This redirection allows the firewall to effectively act as a
port forwarder for RDP traffic, enabling external access to VM1 via the firewall.
A network rule generally controls traffic based on source and destination IP addresses, ports, and protocols,
but doesn't inherently modify the destination address for forwarding purposes like DNAT does. URL filtering is
relevant for controlling access to websites based on URLs, not for forwarding specific types of traffic to

internal resources. An application rule filters traffic based on application-layer attributes such as Fully
Qualified Domain Names (FQDNs), which isn't suitable for routing RDP based on its port. Therefore, only a
DNAT rule allows the necessary translation of destination address and port to correctly route RDP requests to
VM1.
Further reading on Azure Firewall DNAT rules can be found at the following link:
https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/dnat-rules

Question: 273 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains the following resources:
• A virtual network named Vnet1
• Two subnets named subnet1 and AzureFirewallSubnet
• A public Azure Firewall named FW1
• A route table named RT1 that is associated to Subnet1
• A rule routing of 0.0.0.0/0 to FW1 in RT1
After deploying 10 servers that run Windows Server to Subnet1, you discover that none of the virtual machines
were activated.
You need to ensure that the virtual machines can be activated.
What should you do?
A. On FW1, create an outbound network rule that allows traffic to the Azure Key Management Service (KMS).

B. On FW1, create an outbound service tag rule for Azure Cloud.
C. Deploy a NAT gateway.
D. On FW1, configure a DNAT rule for port 1688.
Answer: A
Explanation:
Here's a detailed justification for why the answer is A, along with supporting information:
The core problem is that virtual machines in Subnet1 cannot activate their Windows Server licenses. Windows
Server activation relies on the Key Management Service (KMS). Since Subnet1 traffic is routed through Azure
Firewall (FW1) via the 0.0.0.0/0 route in RT1, all outbound traffic, including KMS activation attempts, is being
inspected and potentially blocked by FW1.
Option A suggests creating an outbound network rule on FW1 to specifically allow traffic to the Azure KMS.
This is the correct approach because it explicitly opens the necessary communication path for license
activation. KMS uses a specific port (typically 1688) and resolves to Azure's KMS servers. By creating a rule
permitting traffic to the KMS service, we allow the VMs to contact the licensing servers and activate.
Option B, creating an outbound service tag rule for AzureCloud, is too broad. While it allows VMs to reach
many Azure services, it doesn't guarantee KMS connectivity if the service tag definition hasn't included it.
Plus, opening up too many services is less secure than specific traffic rules.
Option C, deploying a NAT gateway, allows VMs without public IPs to access the internet. This isn't directly
solving the KMS activation problem, as the traffic still needs to be permitted through FW1. NAT Gateway only
provides outbound internet access translation, not a firewall exception.
Option D, configuring a DNAT rule for port 1688, is incorrect. DNAT (Destination Network Address Translation)
translates inbound traffic. KMS activation is outbound traffic initiated by the VMs. DNAT won't help them
reach the KMS servers.
Therefore, explicitly creating a rule on Azure Firewall that allows outbound network traffic to the Azure Key
Management Service (KMS) is the most direct and secure solution to enable Windows Server activation in the
given scenario. This approach ensures only the necessary traffic for activation is permitted while minimizing
the attack surface.
In summary, Azure Firewall is blocking KMS traffic. Configuring a specific outbound rule targeting the KMS
will allow the virtual machines to activate without unnecessarily opening the firewall to broad network ranges
or services.
Here are some authoritative links for further research:
Azure Firewall Network Rules: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/rule-processing
Azure Firewall Service Tags: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/service-tags
Key Management Services (KMS) Client Activation: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows-server/get-
started/kms-client-activation-keys
Azure NAT Gateway: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/nat-gateway/nat-overview

-
You need to create an Azure Firewall instance named FW1 that meets the following requirements:
• Has an IP address from the address range of 10.1.255.0/24
• Uses a new Premium firewall policy named FW-policy1
• Routes traffic directly to the internet
To complete this task, sign in to the Azure portal.
Answer:

Explanation:
Step 1: Create a Resource.
Action: On the Azure portal menu or from the Home page, select Create a resource.
Purpose: This initiates the process of creating a new Azure resource.

Step 2: Search for Firewall.
Action: Type firewall in the search box and press Enter.
Purpose: This filters the available resources to those related to firewalls.
Step 3: Select Firewall and Create
Action: Select Firewall and then select Create.
Purpose: This starts the process of creating a new firewall instance.
Step 4: Configure the Firewall
Action: On the Create a Firewall page, configure the firewall using the provided table.
Name: Enter FW1.
Firewall management: Select Use a Firewall Policy to manage this firewall.
Firewall policy: Select Add new, and enter FW-policy1.
Choose a virtual network: Select Create new.
Purpose: These settings define the basic configuration of the firewall, including its name, management policy,
and associated virtual network.
Step 4.1: Enter or Select Values
Action: Enter or select the appropriate values for:
Subscription: Select your Azure subscription.
Resource group: Specify the resource group.
Name: Enter a name for the virtual network.
Region: Select the Azure region.
Purpose: These values specify the subscription, resource group, and region where the firewall will be
deployed.
Step 4.2: Configure IP Addresses
Action: Select Next: IP addresses.
Purpose: This step moves the configuration to the IP address settings.
Step 4.3: IPv4 Address Space
Action: For IPv4 Address space, accept the default 10.0.0.0/16.
Purpose: This sets the IP address range for the virtual network.
Step 4.4: Configure Subnet
Action: Under Subnet, select default.
Subnet name: Enter a name for the subnet.
Address range: Type 10.1.255.0/24.
Purpose: This configures the subnet within the virtual network.

Step 4.5: Save Configuration
Action: Select Save.
Purpose: This saves the subnet configuration.
Step 4.6: Review and Create
Action: Select Review + create.
Purpose: This step allows you to review all configurations before creating the firewall.
Step 4.7: Create Firewall
Action: Select Create.
Purpose: This finalizes the creation of the firewall with the specified configurations.
Step 5: Configure Public IP Address
Action: Back to the Create a Firewall page:
Public IP address: Select Add new.
Purpose: This adds a public IP address to the firewall.
Step 6: Accept Default Values and Review
Action: Accept the other default values, then select Review + create.
Purpose: This ensures all configurations are correct before final creation.
Step 7: Create the Firewall
Action: Review the summary, and then select Create to create the firewall.
Purpose: This completes the process and deploys the firewall with the specified settings.

Question: 277 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains a virtual network named Vnet1. Vnet1 contains 20 subnets and 500
virtual machines. Each subnet contains a virtual machine that runs network monitoring software.
You have a network security group (NSG) named NSG1 associated to each subnet.
When a new subnet is created in Vnet1 an automated process creates an additional network monitoring virtual
machine in the subnet and links the subnet to NSG1.
You need to create an inbound security rule in NSG1 that will allow connections to the network monitoring virtual
machines from an IP address of 131.107.1.15. The solution must meet the following requirements:
• Ensure that only the monitoring virtual machines receive a connection from 131.1071.15.
• Minimize changes to NSG1 when a new subnet is created.
What should you use as the destination in the inbound security rule?
A. an application security group
B. a service tag
C. a virtual network
D. an IP address
Answer: A
Explanation:
The correct answer is A. an application security group (ASG). Here's why:

The requirement is to allow inbound connections from a specific IP address (131.107.1.15) only to the network
monitoring virtual machines across all subnets within Vnet1. Furthermore, the solution must minimize NSG
changes when new subnets are created.
Application Security Groups (ASGs) are the best choice because they enable you to group virtual machines
based on application roles, regardless of the subnets they reside in. You can create an ASG, add all the
network monitoring virtual machines to this ASG, and then create an NSG rule that allows inbound traffic from
131.107.1.15 to the ASG. When a new network monitoring VM is deployed in a new subnet, it can be added to
the ASG, and the existing NSG rule automatically applies without needing modification. This adheres to the
requirement of minimizing changes to NSG1 upon subnet creation.
Service tags represent predefined groups of IP addresses for Azure services. They aren't applicable here
because the requirement specifies a single, specific IP address, not a group of IP addresses belonging to an
Azure service.
Virtual Network as a destination would allow traffic from the specified IP address to any virtual machine
within the entire Vnet1, regardless of whether it's a monitoring VM. This violates the requirement to only allow
connections to the monitoring VMs.
IP address as the destination would necessitate creating an NSG rule for each network monitoring VM. This is
not scalable, especially considering that new subnets (and therefore, new monitoring VMs) are created
automatically. This contradicts the requirement to minimize NSG changes.
Using an ASG allows centralized management and consistent security policies for the network monitoring
VMs, regardless of their location within the virtual network. It simplifies management and reduces the risk of
misconfigurations.
Authoritative Links:
Application Security Groups: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/application-security-
groups
Network Security Groups: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/network-security-groups-
overview
Service Tags: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/service-tags-overview
Question: 278 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains the resources shown in the following table.
Subnet1 contains three virtual machines that host an app named App1. App1 is accessed by using the SFTP
protocol.
From NSG1, you configure an inbound security rule named Rule2 that allows inbound SFTP connections to ASG1.
You need to ensure that the inbound SFTP connections are managed by using ASG1. The solution must minimize
administrative effort.
What should you do?
A. From NSG1, modify the priority of Rule2.
B. From each virtual machine, associate the network interface to ASG1.

C. From Subnet1, create a subnet delegation.
D. From ASG1, modify the role assignments.
Answer: B
Explanation:
B: From each virtual machine, associate the network interface to ASG1.
To manage inbound SFTP (Secure File Transfer Protocol) connections using Application Security Groups
(ASGs) while minimizing administrative effort, you should associate the network interfaces of the virtual
machines with the relevant ASG (in this case, ASG1). This approach ensures that the security rules defined for
ASG1 will automatically apply to the associated virtual machines.
Question: 279 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains the resources shown in the following table.
Users on HP1 connect to App1 by using a URL of https://app1.contoso.com.
You need to ensure that the IDPS on FW1 can identify security threats in the connections from HP1 to Server1.
Which two actions should you perform? Each correct answer presents part of the solution.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
A. Enable TLS inspection for FW1.
B. Import a server certificate to KV1.
C. Enable threat intelligence for FW1.
D. Add an application group to HP1.
E. Add a secured virtual network to FW1.
Answer: AB
Explanation:
A. Enable TLS inspection for FW1: TLS (Transport Layer Security) inspection allows the firewall to inspect
encrypted traffic. By enabling TLS inspection on FW1, the firewall can decrypt the traffic, inspect it for
threats, and then re-encrypt it before forwarding it to its destination. This is essential for identifying threats in
encrypted connections.
B. Import a server certificate to KV1: To enable TLS inspection, the firewall needs to have a trusted certificate
to decrypt and re-encrypt the traffic. Importing a server certificate to Key Vault (KV1) allows the firewall to
use this certificate for TLS inspection. The certificate must be trusted by both the client (HP1) and the server
(Server1) to avoid any security warnings or connection issues.

Question: 280 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT
-
Case Study
-
This is a case study. Case studies are not timed separately. You can use as much exam time as you would like to
complete each case. However, there may be additional case studies and sections on this exam. You must manage
your time to ensure that you are able to complete all questions included on this exam in the time provided.
To answer the questions included in a case study, you will need to reference information that is provided in the
case study. Case studies might contain exhibits and other resources that provide more information about the
scenario that is described in the case study. Each question is independent of the other questions in this case study.
At the end of this case study, a review screen will appear. This screen allows you to review your answers and to
make changes before you move to the next section of the exam. After you begin a new section, you cannot return
to this section.

Overview
-
Contoso, Ltd. is a consulting company that has a main office in San Francisco and a branch office in Dallas.
Contoso recently purchased an Azure subscription and is performing its first pilot project in Azure.
Existing Environment
-

-
Contoso has an Azure Active Directory (Azure AD) tenant named contoso.com.
The Azure subscription contains the virtual networks shown in the following table.
Vnet1 contains a virtual network gateway named GW1.

-
The Azure subscription contains virtual machines that run Windows Server 2019 as shown in the following table.
The NSGs are associated to the network interfaces on the virtual machines. Each NSG has one custom security
rule that allows RDP connections from the internet. The firewall on each virtual machine allows ICMP traffic.
An application security group named ASG1 is associated to the network interface of VM1.

o Two container groups that connect to Vnet6
o Three virtual machines that connect to Vnet6
o Allow VPN connections to be established to Vnet6

o Allow the resources in Vnet6 to access KeyVault1, DB1, and Vnet1 over the Microsoft backbone network.
•The virtual machines in Vnet4 and Vnet5 must be able to communicate over the Microsoft backbone network.
•A virtual machine named VM-Analyze will be deployed to Subnet1. VM-Analyze must inspect the outbound
network traffic from Subnet2 to the internet.

•Configure Azure Active Directory (Azure AD) authentication for Point-to-Site (P2S) VPN users.
•Enable NSG flow logs for NSG3 and NSG4.

rules shown in the following table.

rules shown in the following table.

Question: 282 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT
-
You have an Azure subscription that contains 10 virtual machines. The virtual machines are assigned private IP
addresses. The subscription contains the resources shown in the following table.
You need to configure FWPolicy1 to meet the following requirements:
•Allow incoming connections to the virtual machines from the internet on port 4567.
•Block outbound connections from the virtual machines to an FQDN of *.fabrikam.com.
What should you configure in FWPolicy1? To answer, select the appropriate options in the answer area.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.

Answer:
Explanation:
1.A DNAT Rule.

DNAT rules are used to allow inbound traffic from the internet to a specific private IP address and port behind
the firewall. They translate the public IP address and port of the firewall to the private IP address and port of
the internal resource.
For example, if you want to allow external users to access a web server hosted behind the firewall, you would
create a DNAT rule to forward traffic from the firewall's public IP to the private IP of the web server.
2. An Application Rule.
Application rules can be used to block outbound traffic based on FQDNs.
For example, if you want to block access to specific websites or services, you would create an application rule
to deny traffic to those FQDNs.
Question: 283 CertyIQ
DRAG DROP
-
You have an Azure subscription that contains an Azure VPN gateway named GW1. GW1 provides Point-to-Site (P2S)
VPN connectivity.
Users connect to GW1 from a Windows 11 device by using an SSTP connection.
You need to ensure that the P2S VPN connections support Azure AD authentication.
Which three actions should you perform in sequence? To answer, move the appropriate actions from the list of
actions to the answer area and arrange them in the correct order.
NOTE: More than one order of answer choices is correct. You will receive credit for any of the correct orders you
select.
Answer:
Explanation:
1. Grant the Azure VPN Application Admin Consent to the Azure AD Tenant.
Before using Azure AD for VPN authentication, the Azure VPN application must be granted admin consent in
the Azure AD tenant. This allows the VPN service to authenticate users using their Azure AD credentials.
2. For the Point-to-Site Configuration of GW1, Set Authentication Type to Azure Active Directory and Set
Tunnel Type to IKEv2 and SSTP (SSL).

This action configures the virtual network gateway (GW1) to use Azure AD for authentication and specifies the
VPN tunnel protocols.
3. Download the Azure VPN Client Profile Configuration Package and Distribute the Package to the Users.
After configuring the VPN gateway, you need to create and distribute a VPN client profile to users. This
profile contains the necessary settings for users to connect to the VPN.

Question: 285 CertyIQ
Note: This question is part of a series of questions that present the same scenario. Each question in the series
contains a unique solution that might meet the stated goals. Some question sets might have more than one correct
solution, while others might not have a correct solution.
After you answer a question in this section, you will NOT be able to return to it. As a result, these questions will not
appear in the review screen.
You have an Azure subscription that contains an Azure Front Door Premium profile named AFD1 and an Azure Web
Application Firewall (WAF) policy named WAF1. AFD1 is associated with WAF1.
You need to configure a rate limit for incoming requests to AFD1.
Solution: You configure a managed rule for WAF1.
Does this meet the goal?
A.Yes
B.No
Answer: B
Explanation:
The proposed solution suggests using a managed rule in the Azure WAF policy to configure a rate limit.
However, managed rules are pre-defined sets of rules provided by Microsoft or other security vendors and are
not customizable for specific rate-limiting needs. Rate limiting requires creating a custom rule within the WAF
policy. Custom rules allow precise control over the criteria for triggering rate limits (e.g., IP address, URL) and
the specific actions to take (e.g., block requests). Managed rules primarily focus on addressing common web
application vulnerabilities and are not designed for granular rate limiting as required by the scenario.
Therefore, using a managed rule alone does not fulfill the requirement of configuring a rate limit for incoming
requests to Azure Front Door.
For more information, refer to the following resources:

Azure Web Application Firewall custom rules: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/web-application-
firewall/afw-custom-rules
Azure Web Application Firewall rate limiting: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/web-application-
firewall/how-to-configure-rate-limiting

Question: 287 CertyIQ
Note: This question is part of a series of questions that present the same scenario. Each question in the series
contains a unique solution that might meet the stated goals. Some question sets might have more than one correct
solution, while others might not have a correct solution.
After you answer a question in this section, you will NOT be able to return to it. As a result, these questions will not
appear in the review screen.
You have an Azure subscription that contains an Azure Front Door Premium profile named AFD1 and an Azure Web
Application Firewall (WAF) policy named WAF1. AFD1 is associated with WAF1.
You need to configure a rate limit for incoming requests to AFD1.
Solution: You configure a custom rule for WAF1.
Does this meet the goal?
A.Yes
B.No
Answer: A
Explanation:
Yes, the solution meets the goal. Azure Web Application Firewall (WAF) policies associated with Azure Front
Door Premium profiles support custom rules that can be configured to implement rate limiting. By creating a

custom rule within the WAF policy (WAF1), you can define criteria based on request characteristics (e.g., IP
address, HTTP header) and specify a threshold for the number of requests allowed within a given time period.
When the threshold is exceeded, the WAF can take actions like blocking the requests or redirecting them.
This effectively allows you to control the rate of incoming requests to your Azure Front Door (AFD1) endpoint.
Custom rules provide the flexibility needed to tailor rate limiting to specific application requirements.
For authoritative documentation, see:
Custom rules for Web Application Firewall v2 on Azure Front Door
Rate limiting rules in Azure Front Door WAF
Question: 288 CertyIQ
Note: This question is part of a series of questions that present the same scenario. Each question in the series
contains a unique solution that might meet the stated goals. Some question sets might have more than one correct
solution, while others might not have a correct solution.
After you answer a question in this section, you will NOT be able to return to it. As a result, these questions will not
appear in the review screen.
You have an Azure subscription that contains an Azure Front Door Premium profile named AFD1 and an Azure Web
Application Firewall (WAF) policy named WAF1. AFD1 is associated with WAF1.
You need to configure a rate limit for incoming requests to AFD1.
Solution: You add a rule to the rule set of AFD1.
Does this meet the goal?
A.Yes
B.No
Answer: B
Explanation:
The proposed solution of adding a rule to the rule set of AFD1 to configure a rate limit for incoming requests is
incorrect. Azure Front Door's rate limiting capabilities are managed through the Web Application Firewall
(WAF) policy, not directly within the Front Door profile itself. Rate limiting is specifically a WAF feature
designed to mitigate denial-of-service (DoS) attacks and manage traffic spikes by restricting the number of
requests from a single IP address within a specific time window.
Since AFD1 is associated with WAF1, the rate limiting configuration should be implemented within WAF1.
Specifically, rate limiting is configured by adding custom rules or managed rule sets to the WAF policy. These
rules define the criteria for triggering rate limiting, such as the number of requests per IP address, the time
window, and the action to take when the limit is exceeded (e.g., blocking the request). Therefore, modifying
the AFD1 profile directly will not achieve the desired rate-limiting functionality.
Therefore, the correct answer is No.
Supporting documentation:
Azure WAF rate limiting: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/web-application-firewall/overview
Custom WAF rules: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/web-application-firewall/custom-waf-rules
Question: 289 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT
-

You have an Azure subscription that contains an Azure Firewall policy named FWPolicy1.
You need to configure FWPolicy1 to meet the following requirements:
•Allow traffic based on the FQDN of the destination.
•Allow TCP traffic based on the source.
Which types of rules should you use for each requirement? To answer, select the appropriate options in the answer
area.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
Answer:
Explanation:
1. Allow traffic based on the FQDN of the destination: Application only.
Fully Qualified Domain Name (FQDN) filtering is only available in Application Rules.

Application rules allow traffic to specific domains (e.g., *.contoso.com) rather than IP addresses.
Network rules do not support FQDN filtering, which is why "Application only" is the correct choice.
2. Allow TCP traffic based on the source: Network or DNAT only.
TCP traffic based on the source is handled at the Network level or DNAT rules.
Network Rules define rules based on IP addresses, ports, and protocols.
DNAT (Destination Network Address Translation) Rules allow inbound traffic redirection to specific internal
resources.
Application Rules do not process source-based TCP filtering, so the correct choice is "Network or DNAT only."

Question: 292 CertyIQ
SIMULATION
-
Username and password
-
Use the following login credentials as needed:

To enter your username, place your cursor in the Sign in box and click on the username below.
To enter your password, place your cursor in the Enter password box and click on the password below.
Azure Username: [email protected]
Azure Password: xxxxxxxxxx
-
If the Azure portal does not load successfully in the browser, press CTRL-K to reload the portal in a new browser
tab.
The following information is for technical support purposes only:
Lab Instance: 12345678
-
You need to ensure that subnet3-2 can only access resources on subnet3-1.
To complete this task, sign in to the Azure portal.
Answer:

Explanation:
Stage 1: Create a Network Security Group
This stage involves creating an NSG to secure network traffic.
Step 1: Search for Network Security Group
In the Azure portal, use the search box at the top to search for Network security group.
Select Network security group from the search results.
Step 2: Create a New NSG
Click + Create to start creating a new NSG.
Step 3: Configure Basic Settings
On the Basics tab, enter or select the following information:
Subscription: Select your Azure subscription.
Resource group: Select an existing resource group (e.g., test-rp).
Name: Enter a name for the NSG (e.g., nsg-1).
Region: Select a region (e.g., East US 2).
Step 4: Review and Create
Click Review + create to validate the configuration.
Click Create to create the NSG.
Stage 2: Associate the Network Security Group with a Subnet
This stage involves linking the NSG to a subnet in your virtual network.
Step 1: Search for Network Security Group

In the Azure portal, search for Network security group again.
Select the NSG you created (e.g., nsg-1).
Step 2: Navigate to Subnets
In the NSG's settings, select Subnets from the left-hand menu.
Step 3: Associate the NSG with a Subnet
Click + Associate to link the NSG to a subnet.
Under Associate subnet, select the virtual network (e.g., vnet-1) and the subnet (e.g., subnet-2).
Click OK to complete the association.
Stage 3: Create Security Rules
This stage involves creating security rules to control inbound and outbound traffic.
Step 1: Navigate to Outbound Security Rules
In the NSG's settings, select Outbound security rules from the left-hand menu.
Step 2: Add a New Outbound Rule
Click + Add to create a new outbound security rule.
Step 3: Configure the Outbound Rule
Create a rule that allows traffic to a specific subnet (e.g., subnet-3-1).
Name: Enter a name for the rule.
Protocol: Select the protocol (e.g., Any).
Source: Specify the source IP addresses or subnets.
Destination: Specify the destination IP addresses or subnets.
Destination Ports: Specify the ports (e.g., * for all ports).
Action: Select Allow or Deny.
Step 4: Save the Rule
Click Add to save the rule.

Question: 294 CertyIQ
Note: This question is part of a series of questions that present the same scenario. Each question in the series
contains a unique solution that might meet the stated goals. Some question sets might have more than one correct

solution, while others might not have a correct solution.
After you answer a question in this section, you will NOT be able to return it. As a result, these questions will not
appear in the review screen.
You have an Azure subscription that contains an Azure Virtual WAN named VWAN1. VWAN1 contains a hub named
Hub1.
Hub1 has a security status of Unsecured.
You need to ensure that the security status of Hub1 is marked as Secured.
Solution: You implement an Azure Front Door profile.
Does this meet the requirement?
A.Yes
B.No
Answer: B
Explanation:
The provided solution does not meet the requirement. The goal is to secure an Azure Virtual WAN hub (Hub1)
that currently has an "Unsecured" security status. Implementing an Azure Front Door profile is not directly
related to securing a Virtual WAN hub. Azure Front Door is a global, scalable entry point that uses the
Microsoft global network to deliver fast, secure, and widely scalable web applications. It's primarily used for
application delivery and security at the edge.
The security status of a Virtual WAN hub primarily relates to the presence and configuration of a security
solution, typically Azure Firewall or a Network Virtual Appliance (NVA) deployed within the hub. Hub1 is likely
showing as "Unsecured" because it doesn't have an associated Azure Firewall or NVA providing security
policies.
To change the status to "Secured", you would need to deploy and configure Azure Firewall within the hub or
connect a supported NVA to the hub. The presence of Azure Firewall or an NVA provides the necessary
security controls that result in the Hub being marked as "Secured". Implementing Front Door won't deploy the
Firewall, therefore the Hub status won't be changed.
Therefore, the provided solution is incorrect because Azure Front Door focuses on web application security
and delivery at the application layer, while securing a Virtual WAN hub necessitates network-level security
solutions such as Azure Firewall integrated directly within the Virtual WAN environment.
Supporting Documentation:
Azure Virtual WAN Security: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-wan/howto-secure-virtual-hub
Azure Firewall: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/overview
Azure Front Door: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/frontdoor/front-door-overview
Question: 295 CertyIQ
Note: This question is part of a series of questions that present the same scenario. Each question in the series
contains a unique solution that might meet the stated goals. Some question sets might have more than one correct
solution, while others might not have a correct solution.
After you answer a question in this section, you will NOT be able to return it. As a result, these questions will not
appear in the review screen.
You have an Azure subscription that contains an Azure Virtual WAN named VWAN1. VWAN1 contains a hub named
Hub1.
Hub1 has a security status of Unsecured.
You need to ensure that the security status of Hub1 is marked as Secured.
Solution: You implement Azure Firewall.
Does this meet the requirement?

A.Yes
B.No
Answer: A
Explanation:
The solution proposes implementing Azure Firewall to secure an Azure Virtual WAN hub (Hub1). This meets
the requirement of changing the security status of Hub1 from "Unsecured" to "Secured". Azure Firewall is a
managed, cloud-based network security service that protects Azure Virtual Network resources. Deploying
Azure Firewall within the virtual hub (Hub1) makes it a secured hub.
Azure Firewall provides features such as threat intelligence-based filtering, application filtering, and network
address translation (NAT), which enhances the security posture of the virtual hub. By implementing Azure
Firewall, all traffic passing through the hub can be inspected and filtered based on defined security rules. This
prevents unauthorized access, mitigates potential threats, and ensures compliance with security policies.
Therefore, implementing Azure Firewall is a valid approach to securing a Virtual WAN hub.
Therefore, the solution is correct as implementing Azure Firewall within Hub1 will result in the hub being
considered as secured within Azure Virtual WAN.
Here's a reference link for further research: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-wan/howto-
secure-virtual-hub
Question: 296 CertyIQ
Note: This question is part of a series of questions that present the same scenario. Each question in the series
contains a unique solution that might meet the stated goals. Some question sets might have more than one correct
solution, while others might not have a correct solution.
After you answer a question in this section, you will NOT be able to return it. As a result, these questions will not
appear in the review screen.
You have an Azure subscription that contains an Azure Virtual WAN named VWAN1. VWAN1 contains a hub named
Hub1.
Hub1 has a security status of Unsecured.
You need to ensure that the security status of Hub1 is marked as Secured.
Solution: You implement Azure NAT Gateway.
Does this meet the requirement?
A.Yes
B.No
Answer: B
Explanation:
The provided solution does not meet the requirement. The security status of an Azure Virtual WAN hub being
marked as "Secured" is primarily influenced by the presence of a firewall or security solution integrated with
the hub, such as Azure Firewall Manager or a third-party Network Virtual Appliance (NVA). Implementing
Azure NAT Gateway alone does not address the core security aspects required to change the hub's security
status from "Unsecured" to "Secured". Azure NAT Gateway provides outbound internet connectivity for virtual
machines within the hub or connected spoke virtual networks, masking their private IP addresses. It enhances
outbound connectivity and helps prevent direct exposure of VMs to the internet, but it doesn't provide the

comprehensive threat protection, traffic inspection, and security rules enforcement that a firewall does.
Therefore, while NAT Gateway contributes to a secure networking architecture, it doesn't fulfill the criteria
for a "Secured" hub status in Virtual WAN. To achieve a "Secured" status, a proper firewall solution must be
implemented and associated with the hub.
Relevant documentation:
Azure Virtual WAN security: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-wan/howto-secure-virtual-hub
Azure Firewall Manager: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall-manager/overview
Azure NAT Gateway: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/nat-gateway/nat-overview
Question: 297 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription.
You plan to deploy Azure Firewall Premium, enable all the Premium features, and configure both network and
application rules.
Which type of rule will the firewall process first?
A.network
B.application
C.threat intelligence
D.infrastructure
Answer: C
Explanation:
The correct answer is C. threat intelligence.
Azure Firewall Premium processes rules in a specific order to optimize security and performance. The
processing order ensures that the most critical and potentially malicious traffic is handled first.
Azure Firewall Premium's processing order is as follows:
1. Threat intelligence filtering: The firewall first checks the traffic against threat intelligence feeds to
identify and block known malicious IPs and domains. This is a crucial first step in preventing attacks.
Threat intelligence-based filtering happens even before any configured network or application rules
are applied.
2. Network rules: After threat intelligence, the firewall evaluates network rules. These rules are based
on source and destination IP addresses, ports, and protocols.
3. Application rules: Finally, if the traffic matches a network rule permitting it, the firewall then
processes application rules. Application rules use fully qualified domain names (FQDNs), URLs, and
HTTP/HTTPS headers to filter traffic.
4. Infrastructure Rules: Infrastructure rules allow management traffic to Azure infrastructure.
Therefore, threat intelligence rules are always processed first, providing a crucial first layer of defense
against known threats. Network and application rules are processed in subsequent stages.
Supporting documentation:
Azure Firewall Premium features
Azure Firewall rule processing

Question: 298 CertyIQ
Note: This question is part of a series of questions that present the same scenario. Each question in the series
contains a unique solution that might meet the stated goals. Some question sets might have more than one correct
solution, while others might not have a correct solution.
After you answer a question in this section, you will NOT be able to return it. As a result, these questions will not
appear in the review screen.
You have an Azure subscription that contains an Azure Virtual WAN named VWAN1. VWAN1 contains a hub named
Hub1.
Hub1 has a security status of Unsecured.
You need to ensure that the security status of Hub1 is marked as Secured.
Solution: You implement Azure Web Application Firewall (WAF).
Does this meet the requirement?
A.Yes
B.No
Answer: B
Explanation:
The provided solution of implementing Azure Web Application Firewall (WAF) does not directly address the
requirement of securing an Azure Virtual WAN hub to change its security status to "Secured."
While WAF is a crucial security component for protecting web applications from common web exploits and
vulnerabilities (like SQL injection, cross-site scripting, etc.), it operates at Layer 7 of the OSI model and
focuses on HTTP/HTTPS traffic. Virtual WAN hub security encompasses broader aspects of network security,
including traffic filtering, routing policies, and overall network architecture.
To achieve a "Secured" status for an Azure Virtual WAN hub, you typically need to implement an Azure
Firewall within the hub. Azure Firewall is a managed, cloud-based network security service that protects your
Azure Virtual Network resources. It provides stateful firewall capabilities for Virtual WAN hub, offering
features like network and application rule processing, threat intelligence-based filtering, and DNAT support.
Deploying and configuring Azure Firewall in the hub is the primary method to secure the hub and ensure its
status is reflected as "Secured." While the specifics might also involve NSGs, they primarily work at the
subnet level and less so on the hub directly.
Therefore, WAF is not the appropriate solution to directly secure a Virtual WAN hub to change its security
status to "Secured." It's an application-level firewall and not a network-level firewall designed to protect the
entire hub infrastructure.
Relevant documentation:
Azure Virtual WAN security: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-wan/howto-secure-virtual-hub
Azure Firewall: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/
Azure Web Application Firewall: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/web-application-firewall/
Question: 299 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains an Azure App Service web app named WebApp1 and an Azure Front
Door profile named FDProfile1. FDProfile1 forwards requests addressed to https://www.contoso.com to WebApp1.
You need to ensure that only requests addressed to https://www.contoso.com/users/* are forwarded to WebApp1.
What should you modify in FDProfile1?
A.the routes

B.the origin group
C.the endpoint
D.the domain
Answer: A
Explanation:
The correct answer is A. the routes.
Routes in Azure Front Door define how traffic from the client (in this case, https://www.contoso.com) is
directed to the backend origin(s) (WebApp1). Specifically, routes allow you to configure pattern matching
using URL paths to control which requests are forwarded. You need to modify the route associated with the
contoso.com domain to include a path pattern of /users/*. This ensures that only requests with URLs starting
with https://www.contoso.com/users/ will be forwarded to WebApp1.
Modifying the origin group (B) would change which backend WebApp1 belongs to, but it doesn't filter traffic
based on URL paths. Modifying the endpoint (C) changes the overall Front Door environment settings such as
the hostname or SSL certificate configuration, which is irrelevant to the URL path filtering requirement.
Modifying the domain (D) would change the domain name that Front Door is listening on, also irrelevant to the
path filtering requirement. Routes are designed to achieve granular control over traffic flow based on URL
paths, hostnames, protocols, and other request attributes, making it the ideal solution for forwarding only
requests to https://www.contoso.com/users/*.
https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/frontdoor/front-door-routing-
methodshttps://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/frontdoor/front-door-route-configuration
