Question: 302 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains a virtual machine named VM1 and a network security group (NSG)
named NSG1. NSG1 has the default rules configured. VM1 runs Windows Server 2022 and contains a single NIC
named NIC1. NIC1 is associated with NSG1.
You need to prevent access to the Azure Instance Metadata Service (IMDS) REST API on VM1. The solution must
minimize administrative effort.
What should you add to NSG1?
A.an outbound rule that blocks traffic to an IP address.
B.an inbound rule that blocks traffic to an IP address.
C.an inbound and outbound rule that blocks traffic to an application security group.
D.an outbound rule that blocks traffic to a service tag.
Answer: D
Explanation:
The correct answer is D. an outbound rule that blocks traffic to a service tag.
To prevent access to the Azure Instance Metadata Service (IMDS) on VM1, you should block outbound traffic
from the VM to the IMDS endpoint. The IMDS endpoint is a well-known IP address (169.254.169.254), but
Azure provides a more manageable and recommended way to block access using service tags.
Service tags represent groups of IP address prefixes for a given Azure service. Using service tags, you don't
need to know the specific IP addresses, which might change over time. Azure automatically updates the
service tag as the IP addresses of the corresponding service change. In this case, the
AzureInstanceMetadataService service tag encompasses the IP addresses used by the IMDS.
An outbound rule added to NSG1, configured to deny traffic to the AzureInstanceMetadataService service tag,
will effectively block any attempts by VM1 to access the IMDS. Because the solution aims to minimize
administrative effort, service tags are ideal compared to manually managing individual IP addresses that may
vary across regions or with Azure infrastructure updates. An inbound rule would not be relevant as it controls
incoming traffic to the VM, not outgoing traffic to the IMDS. Also, application security groups are useful for
grouping VMs, but in this case, they do not provide a direct way of blocking traffic to a specific service like
IMDS. Thus, blocking outbound traffic to a service tag associated with IMDS is the most efficient solution.
References:
Azure service tags overview
Securing access to the Azure Instance Metadata Service (IMDS)

When enabled, Azure Firewall automatically blocks traffic to and from IP addresses and domains that are
identified as malicious by Microsoft's threat intelligence.
This is ideal for blocking traffic to malicious addresses without manually configuring rules.

Application rules allow or deny traffic based on FQDNs (e.g., facebook.com, twitter.com). They are specifically
designed for HTTP/HTTPS traffic.
You can create an application rule to block traffic to specific FQDNs associated with social networking sites.
This is ideal for blocking access to specific websites or categories of websites, such as social networking
sites.

Question: 308 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains an instance of Azure Firewall Standard named AzFW1.
You plan to enable the following:
•TLS inspection
•Threat intelligence
•A network intrusion detection and prevention system (IDPS)
What can you enable by using AzFW1?
A.TLS inspection only
B.threat intelligence only
C.TLS inspection and the IDPS only
D.threat intelligence and the IDPS only
E.TLS inspection, threat intelligence, and the IDPS
Answer: B
Explanation:
Here's a detailed justification for why the correct answer is B (threat intelligence only) when using Azure
Firewall Standard (AzFW1) with plans to enable TLS inspection, threat intelligence, and a network intrusion
detection and prevention system (IDPS).
Azure Firewall Standard offers a foundational set of network security features. Crucially, Azure Firewall
Standard does not support TLS inspection or IDPS functionality. These features are only available in the Azure
Firewall Premium tier. TLS inspection requires the ability to intercept and decrypt traffic, a capability reserved
for the more advanced Premium SKU due to its higher processing demands and added security complexities.
IDPS, similarly, needs deeper packet inspection and signature matching capabilities, which Standard lacks.
Threat intelligence filtering, conversely, is available in Azure Firewall Standard. This feature allows you to
configure your firewall to alert and deny traffic from/to known malicious IP addresses and domains. The
threat intelligence feeds are regularly updated to keep your network protected against emerging threats.
Therefore, out of the functionalities listed (TLS inspection, threat intelligence, and IDPS), only threat
intelligence can be enabled directly by using Azure Firewall Standard. The other two require an upgrade to
Azure Firewall Premium.
Authoritative links for further research:
Azure Firewall features: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/features

Azure Firewall Premium: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/premium-features
Azure Firewall Threat Intelligence: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/threat-intel

Question: 310 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains a resource group named RG1 and a virtual network named VNet1.
You need to deploy Azure Firewall to RG1. The solution must minimize administrative effort.
What should you do first?
A.Create a secured virtual hub named AzureFirewallHub.
B.Create a new virtual network named AzureFirewallNetwork.
C.Create a new resource group named AzurFirewellRescurceGroup.
D.On VNet1, create virtual subnet named AzureFirewallSubnet
Answer: D
Explanation:
The correct first step is to create a subnet named AzureFirewallSubnet within the existing VNet1. Azure
Firewall is a network security service that needs a dedicated subnet named "AzureFirewallSubnet" within the
virtual network where it will operate. This subnet is where the Azure Firewall instance will be deployed. This
approach adheres to Microsoft's best practices and enables the firewall to manage and inspect traffic flowing
through VNet1.
Option A (creating a secured virtual hub) is relevant to Azure Firewall Manager and hub-and-spoke network
architectures, which are not strictly required for deploying a simple Azure Firewall instance. Option B

(creating a new virtual network) increases administrative overhead unnecessarily, as the requirement
specifies using the existing VNet1. Option C (creating a new resource group) isn't necessary, as the
requirement is to deploy the firewall to the existing RG1. Creating the AzureFirewallSubnet directly in VNet1
minimizes administrative effort while complying with the mandatory subnet name for Azure Firewall
deployments. Without this subnet, Azure Firewall deployment will fail.
Further Resources:
Azure Firewall documentation
Azure Firewall deployment guide

The values provided (1,500 to 8,000) represent different possible scenarios or time periods for data
transmission.

Question: 314 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains a virtual network named VNet1. VNet1 contains an Azure Virtual
Desktop host pool named Pool1.
You need to implement Azure Firewall and TLS inspection for all the outbound traffic from Pool1.
Which two resources should you configure? Each correct answer present part of the solution.
NOTE: Each correct answer is worth one point.
A.an Azure Private DNS zone
B.a private endpoint
C.an Azure key vault
D.an Azure NAT gateway
E.a Microsoft Entra enterprise app
F.a managed identity
Answer: CF
Explanation:
The correct answer is C (an Azure Key Vault) and F (a managed identity). Here's why:
Azure Key Vault: TLS inspection requires access to certificates. Azure Firewall needs to be able to securely
store and retrieve the certificate used for TLS inspection. Azure Key Vault provides a secure and centralized
repository for secrets, keys, and certificates. The Firewall can retrieve the required certificate from the Key
Vault. Without storing the certificate in Key Vault, Azure Firewall will not be able to perform TLS inspection.
[https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/premium-features#tls-inspection]
Managed Identity: To access the Azure Key Vault and retrieve the TLS inspection certificate, Azure Firewall
needs authentication. Managed identities provide an automatically managed identity in Microsoft Entra ID
(formerly Azure Active Directory) for Azure services to use when connecting to resources that support
Microsoft Entra authentication. A managed identity assigned to the Azure Firewall allows it to authenticate to
Key Vault without needing to store credentials directly in the Firewall configuration. This is a best practice for
security and avoids hardcoded credentials. The managed identity needs to be granted the necessary
permissions within Key Vault to retrieve the certificate. [https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/active-
directory/managed-identities-azure-resources/overview]

Why other options are incorrect:
A (an Azure Private DNS zone): Private DNS zones are used for name resolution within a private network.
While important for internal services, they don't directly enable TLS inspection or certificate management.
B (a private endpoint): Private endpoints provide private connectivity to Azure services. While helpful for
securing access to other services, they're not directly involved in the TLS inspection process within Azure
Firewall.
D (an Azure NAT gateway): A NAT gateway enables outbound internet connectivity for virtual network
resources. While the traffic from Pool1 will likely use a NAT gateway for outbound access (if not already doing
so through the public IP of the firewall), it doesn't directly contribute to enabling TLS inspection.
TLS inspection is only available with Azure Firewall Premium. Azure Firewall Premium requires a managed
identity to access the TLS cert in KeyVault for TLS inspection.
Question: 315 CertyIQ
You have an Azure virtual machine named VM1.
You need to capture all the network traffic of VM1 by using Azure Network Watcher.
To which locations can the capture be written?
A.a file path on VM1 only
B.General purpose v2 standard only
C.a Block blob premium account only
D.General purpose v2 standard and a file path on VM1 only
E.General purpose v2 standard and a Block blob premium account only
F.blob storage, a file path on VM1, and a Block blob premium account
Answer: D
Explanation:
D. General purpose v2 standard and a file path on VM1 only
Azure Network Watcher Packet Capture supports writing to only two types of destinations:
A local file path on the VM
You can write directly to a directory on VM1 (e.g., C:\captures on Windows or /var/log/pcap on Linux).
A Standard general-purpose storage account
General-purpose v1 or v2 (Standard) accounts are supported.
Question: 316 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains the following resources:
•A virtual network named Vnet1
•Two subnets named subnet1 and AzureFirewallSubnet
•A public Azure Firewall named FW1
•A route table named RT1 that is associated to Subnet1
•A rule routing of 0.0.0.0/0 to FW1 in RT1
After deploying 10 servers that run Windows Server to Subnet1, you discover that none of the virtual machine
operating systems were activated.
You need to ensure that the virtual machines can be activated.

What should you do?
A.Deploy a NAT gateway.
B.On FW1, create an outbound network rule that allows traffic to the Azure Key Management Service (KMS).
C.To Subnet1, associate a network security group (NSG) that allows outbound access to port 1688.
D.Deploy an Azure Standard Load Balancer that has an outbound NAT rule.
Answer: B
Explanation:
The problem is that virtual machines in Subnet1 cannot activate their Windows Server licenses. This suggests
that the VMs cannot reach the Azure Key Management Service (KMS) to validate their licenses. Given the
existing Azure Firewall (FW1) and the default route sending all traffic from Subnet1 to FW1, the VMs'
outbound traffic is being inspected and potentially blocked by the firewall.
Option A, deploying a NAT gateway, wouldn't directly resolve the license activation issue if the firewall is
blocking the traffic. While a NAT gateway provides outbound internet connectivity, the firewall would still
need to permit KMS-related traffic.
Option B, creating an outbound network rule on FW1 to allow traffic to Azure KMS, is the correct solution.
Windows Server activation requires communication with the Azure KMS service. By default, Azure Firewall
blocks all outbound traffic unless explicitly allowed. Configuring an outbound rule on FW1 to permit traffic to
the KMS service endpoints (specifically, port 1688) will enable the VMs to communicate with KMS and
activate their licenses.
Option C, associating an NSG to Subnet1 to allow outbound access to port 1688, would be ineffective because
the route table RT1 is directing traffic to the firewall before any NSG rules are applied on the subnet. The
firewall still needs to allow the traffic. Azure Firewall processes traffic after NSGs applied at the subnet level.
Option D, deploying an Azure Standard Load Balancer with an outbound NAT rule, introduces unnecessary
complexity. It doesn't directly address the problem of the firewall blocking KMS traffic and adds extra cost
and management overhead. Also, similar to the NAT Gateway, this only addresses the NAT problem, not the
firewall blocking.
Therefore, allowing traffic to the Azure KMS through the firewall is the most direct and efficient solution to
enable Windows Server activation.
Relevant Links:
Azure Key Management Service (KMS) Activation: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows-server/get-
started/kmsclientkeys
Azure Firewall Network Rules: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/rule-processing
Azure Firewall Best Practices: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/best-practices

Question: 322 CertyIQ
You have an Azure virtual network named Vnet1 that has one subnet. Vnet1 is in the West Europe region.
You deploy an Azure App Service app named App1 to the West Europe region.
You need to provide App1 with access to the resources in Vnet1. The solution must minimize costs.
What should you do first?
A. Create a private link.
B. Create a new subnet.
C. Create a NAT gateway.
D. Create a gateway subnet and deploy a virtual network gateway.
Answer: B
Explanation:
The correct answer is B. Create a new subnet.
Here's the justification:
To integrate an Azure App Service app (App1) with an Azure Virtual Network (Vnet1) while minimizing costs,
the best approach is to use Regional Virtual Network Integration. This feature allows your app to access
resources in your virtual network using the private IP addresses of the resources, without exposing your app
to the public internet.
Before configuring Regional Virtual Network Integration, you need a dedicated subnet within Vnet1 that's
delegated to the Microsoft.Web/serverFarms service. This delegation is essential because App Service needs
exclusive access to the subnet to manage the integration and routing of network traffic. The existing subnet
in Vnet1 might already be in use or not suitable for delegation. Therefore, creating a new, dedicated subnet is
necessary.
Option A (Private Link) is more suited for scenarios where you want to expose specific services in your virtual
network securely to external parties, and it generally incurs more cost. Option C (NAT Gateway) provides
outbound connectivity for resources within the virtual network but doesn't enable App Service to access
internal resources securely. Option D (Virtual Network Gateway) is for connecting virtual networks across
Azure regions or to on-premises networks and is not relevant for this scenario.
By creating a new subnet and delegating it to App Service, you prepare the virtual network for Regional
Virtual Network Integration, the most cost-effective solution for integrating App Service with a virtual
network in the same region. The subsequent steps would involve configuring the App Service to integrate with
this newly created subnet.
For further research, refer to these resources:
Integrate your app with an Azure virtual network: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/app-service/web-
sites-integrate-with-vnet
Azure Virtual Network Subnet Delegation: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/subnet-
delegation-overview

Question: 323 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that is linked to an Azure Active Directory (Azure AD) tenant named
contoso.onmicrosoft.com. The subscription contains the following resources:
✑ An Azure App Service app named App1
✑ An Azure DNS zone named contoso.com
✑ An Azure private DNS zone named private.contoso.com
✑ A virtual network named Vnet1
You create a private endpoint for App1. The record for the endpoint is registered automatically in Azure DNS.
You need to provide a developer with the name that is registered in Azure DNS for the private endpoint.
What should you provide?
A. app1.contoso.onmicrosoft.com
B. app1.private.contoso.com
C. app1.privatelink.azurewebsites.net
D. app1.contoso.com
Answer: C
Explanation:
The correct answer is C. app1.privatelink.azurewebsites.net.
Here's why:
When you create a private endpoint for an Azure App Service app, such as App1, the DNS record is
automatically registered in a special DNS zone managed by Azure for private endpoints. This zone follows a
specific naming convention. This convention helps ensure that private endpoint DNS resolution functions
correctly within the virtual network without interfering with public DNS resolution. The privatelink subdomain
is a standard naming convention used by Azure Private Link to clearly distinguish the records associated with
private endpoints.
Specifically, for Azure App Service apps, the DNS record is registered in a zone that resembles
privatelink.azurewebsites.net. Consequently, the full DNS name of the private endpoint for App1 would be
app1.privatelink.azurewebsites.net. It will not be registered directly in your custom private.contoso.com Azure
private DNS zone automatically. The private endpoint integrates with Azure DNS to create the necessary DNS
records for the private IP address allocated to the endpoint within your VNet. Without the correct name,
resolution will fail.
Option A is incorrect because contoso.onmicrosoft.com is the Azure AD domain and is not related to the DNS
name for the private endpoint. Option B is incorrect because while private.contoso.com is a private DNS zone,
the App Service's private endpoint record isn't automatically registered there. You would have to manually
create a CNAME record in this DNS zone to point to the automatically created record in the privatelink zone for
name resolution. Option D is incorrect because contoso.com is the public DNS zone, and the private endpoint
should not be exposed to the public internet.Authoritative Links:
What is Azure Private Link?
Azure Private Endpoint DNS configuration

Question: 328 CertyIQ
You have an Azure Front Door instance named FD1 that is protected by using Azure Web Application Firewall
(WAF).
FD1 uses a frontend hast named app1.contoso.com to provide access to Azure web apps hosted in the East US
Azure region and the West US Azure region.
You need to configure FD1 to block requests to app1.contoso.com from all countries other than the United States.
What should you include in the WAF policy?
A. a custom rule that uses a match rule
B. a frontend hast association
C. a custom rule that uses a rate limit rule
D. a managed rule set
Answer: A
Explanation:
The correct answer is A: a custom rule that uses a match rule. Here's why:
The requirement is to block requests from all countries except the United States for a specific hostname
(app1.contoso.com) served through Azure Front Door. This requires examining the origin of the requests and
acting accordingly.
A custom rule allows you to define specific conditions and actions based on those conditions. A match rule
within the custom rule is crucial here. It enables you to inspect the incoming request's properties, specifically
the originating country (determined by the request's IP address and Geo-IP databases). By configuring the
match rule to check if the originating country is NOT the United States, you can trigger an action, such as
blocking the request.
Option B, a frontend host association, simply associates the WAF policy with a specific hostname. It doesn't
provide the granularity to inspect request properties like the originating country.
Option C, a custom rule that uses a rate limit rule, is designed to mitigate denial-of-service attacks by limiting
the number of requests from a particular source within a defined time window. It's not related to geographic
blocking.
Option D, a managed rule set, provides pre-defined rules for common web application vulnerabilities. While
some managed rule sets might include geo-filtering capabilities, relying solely on them might not provide the
specific customization needed to block all countries except the United States for a specific hostname.
Furthermore, using a custom rule gives more control over the specific filtering criteria. Custom rules are
designed for requirements like this where pre-built rules don't perfectly match needs.
Therefore, a custom rule with a match rule is the most precise and appropriate method to achieve the desired

geo-filtering for a specific hostname within Azure Front Door WAF. You would configure the match rule to
inspect the originating country of each request and block any request originating from a country other than
the US.
Here are some authoritative links for further research:
Azure Web Application Firewall (WAF) with Azure Front Door: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/web-
application-firewall/afw/afw-overview
Custom rules in Azure WAF: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/web-application-firewall/afw/waf-
custom-rules
Match Conditions in WAF Custom Rules: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/web-application-
firewall/afw/waf-custom-rules-match-conditions
Question: 329 CertyIQ
You are planning the IP addressing for the subnets in Azure virtual networks.
Which type of resource requires IP addresses in the subnets?
A. Azure DDoS Protection for virtual networks
B. private endpoints
C. Azure Virtual Network NAT
D. service endpoint policies
Answer: B
Explanation:
The correct answer is B. private endpoints. Here's why:
Private endpoints are Azure resources that use a private IP address from your virtual network subnet to
privately and securely connect to an Azure service. They essentially bring the Azure service into your VNet.
Because they need to reside within your VNet, they must consume IP addresses from a subnet within that
VNet. Each private endpoint requires at least one IP address from the subnet it is deployed into.
Let's examine why the other options are incorrect:
A. Azure DDoS Protection for virtual networks: While DDoS Protection is associated with a virtual network, it
doesn't directly consume IP addresses within subnets of that VNet. DDoS Protection is a service layered on
top of the network, offering protection at the network layer without assigning specific IP addresses to its
functions within the subnet.
C. Azure Virtual Network NAT: While VNet NAT connects to a subnet, it does not consume IP addresses from
the subnet itself. Instead, it utilizes a public IP address or a public IP prefix associated with the NAT gateway
to provide outbound connectivity for the VMs within that subnet.
D. service endpoint policies: Service endpoint policies are used to filter egress traffic when using service
endpoints. These policies don't consume IP addresses within the subnets. The service endpoint itself is
enabling network traffic from your subnet towards Azure service and it requires the IP from the resources
consuming the azure service.
In summary, only private endpoints, by their nature of being deployed directly within a subnet to establish a
private connection, necessitate the allocation of IP addresses from that subnet.
For further research, refer to the following Microsoft documentation:

What is Azure Private Endpoint?
Azure Virtual Network NAT
Azure DDoS Protection
Service endpoint policies for Azure Storage

Question: 336 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains an Azure Front Door named FD1.
You plan to deploy an app named App1 by using Azure App Service. Users will access App1 by using FD1.
You need to provide FD1 with access to App1. The solution must meet the following requirements:
•Ensure that users can only access App1 by using FD1.
•Ensure that users cannot access App1 directly from the internet.
What should you create for App1?
A.an access restriction
B.a private endpoint
C.a subnet delegation
D.a service endpoint
Answer: B
Explanation:
The correct answer is B. a private endpoint.
Here's a detailed justification:
A private endpoint establishes a secure, private connection between your Azure Front Door (FD1) and the
Azure App Service (App1) over the Azure backbone network. This connection is established using private IP
addresses from a subnet within your virtual network, eliminating the need for exposing App1 to the public

internet. This directly addresses the requirement of ensuring users can only access App1 through FD1 and
preventing direct access from the internet.
Access restrictions (Option A) are rules you can configure to allow or deny traffic based on IP addresses,
service tags, or virtual network service endpoints. While access restrictions can be used to restrict access to
specific IP addresses (including Front Door's), they do not inherently remove the public endpoint from the App
Service. It would be possible to bypass the restriction if the IP address is known. They also don't provide the
same level of security and isolation as a private endpoint. Access restrictions alone are insufficient to
guarantee that users cannot directly access App1 from the internet.
Subnet delegation (Option C) allows you to designate a subnet for a specific Azure service. It doesn't establish
a private connection between Front Door and App Service, and thus does not prevent direct internet access to
App1.
Service endpoints (Option D) provide secure and direct connectivity to Azure services from within your virtual
network. They extend your virtual network private address space to the Azure service. While they enhance
security by only allowing traffic from your virtual network, they still require a public endpoint and would not
completely prevent internet access in the way that private endpoints do.
By implementing a private endpoint, you effectively create a private link, enabling FD1 to securely connect to
App1 without exposing App1's public endpoint. FD1 would be configured to route traffic to the private
endpoint address. Users accessing App1 through FD1 will be routed over Azure's backbone network, ensuring
the traffic remains private and secure. This fulfills both requirements: restricting access to FD1 only and
preventing direct internet access.
Authoritative links for further research:
Azure Private Link: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/private-link/private-link-overview
Use Azure Front Door with Private Link: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/frontdoor/standard-
premium/how-to/private-link
Azure App Service Private Endpoints: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/app-
service/networking/private-endpoint

Question: 338 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains four virtual machines. The virtual machines host an app named App1.
You deploy an Azure Standard Load Balancer named LB1 to load balance incoming HTTPS requests to App1.
You need to reduce how long it takes for LB1 to stop sending App1 traffic to failed servers. The solution must
minimize administrative effort.
What should you modify?
A.the Backend pools settings
B.the Diagnostic settings
C.the Load-balancing rules
D.the Health probes settings
Answer: D
Explanation:
Here's a detailed justification for why the answer is D (Health probes settings) and why the other options are
incorrect, focusing on Azure Standard Load Balancer behavior:
The core requirement is to minimize the time it takes for the load balancer to stop sending traffic to unhealthy
servers hosting App1. This directly relates to how the load balancer detects server failures. Health probes are
the mechanism Azure Load Balancer uses to determine the health status of backend instances (virtual
machines).
By modifying the health probe settings, specifically the interval and unhealthy threshold, you can control how
frequently the load balancer checks the health of the backend servers and how many consecutive failures are
required before a server is considered unhealthy and removed from the rotation. A shorter interval and a lower
unhealthy threshold will result in faster detection and removal of failed servers. This minimizes the period
during which the load balancer sends traffic to failing instances, thus addressing the problem statement
directly.
A. Backend pools settings: Backend pools define the group of virtual machines that the load balancer
distributes traffic to. While important for overall load balancing functionality, they don't directly influence
how quickly unhealthy servers are detected and removed. Modifying backend pool settings won't address the
latency in failing over from unhealthy servers.
B. Diagnostic settings: Diagnostic settings are for collecting and analyzing logs and metrics. These are
valuable for monitoring and troubleshooting but do not directly impact the load balancer's decision-making
process regarding server health or traffic routing. Diagnostic settings are primarily reactive, not proactive, in
addressing the speed of failover.
C. Load-balancing rules: Load-balancing rules define how incoming traffic is distributed to the backend pool.
They configure port mappings, session persistence, and floating IP but don't determine how the load balancer
assesses the health of the backend servers. Modifying load-balancing rules won't decrease the time to detect
a failing server.
In summary, health probes are the active component continuously monitoring the health of backend
instances. Adjusting their frequency and failure threshold provides the direct and most effective control over
how quickly the load balancer reacts to unhealthy servers.
Authoritative Links:
Azure Load Balancer Health Probes: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/load-balancer/load-balancer-
custom-probe-overview

Azure Load Balancer Overview: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/load-balancer/load-balancer-
overview
Question: 339 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains a virtual network named VNet1. VNet1 contains the following
subnets:
•AzureFirewallSubnet

dedicated subnet is required for the application gateway. You can have multiple instances of a given
application gateway deployment in a subnet. You can also deploy other application gateways in the subnet.
But you can't deploy any other resource in the application gateway subnet.Subnet 3.

Question: 342 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains the following resources:
•A virtual network named Vnet1
•Two subnets named subnet1 and AzureFirewallSubnet
•A public Azure Firewall named FW1
•A route table named RT1 that is associated to Subnet1
•A rule routing of 0.0.0.0/0 to FW1 in RT1
After deploying 10 servers that run Windows Server to Subnet1, you discover that none of the virtual machines
were activated.
You need to ensure that the virtual machines can be activated.
What should you do?
A.Deploy a NAT gateway.
B.Deploy an Azure Standard Load Balancer that has an outbound NAT rule.
C.On FW1, create an outbound network rule that allows traffic to the Azure Key Management Service (KMS).
D.To Subnet1, associate a network security group (NSG) that allows outbound access to port 1688.
Answer: C
Explanation:
The correct solution is to create an outbound network rule on Azure Firewall FW1 that allows traffic to the
Azure Key Management Service (KMS). Here's why:
Windows Server virtual machines typically require activation via KMS. With the current configuration, all
outbound traffic from Subnet1 is routed through Azure Firewall FW1 due to the 0.0.0.0/0 route in RT1. If FW1
doesn't have a rule explicitly allowing outbound traffic to the KMS servers on the necessary port (typically
TCP port 1688), the activation requests from the Windows Server VMs will be blocked.

Option A (Deploying a NAT gateway) would provide outbound internet access, but without a specific rule on
the firewall, KMS activation might still be blocked. A NAT Gateway alone wouldn't solve the specific KMS
issue.
Option B (Deploying an Azure Standard Load Balancer with outbound NAT) similarly, allows outbound internet
access, but the firewall would still block activation unless explicitly configured to allow KMS traffic.
Option D (Associating an NSG to Subnet1 allowing outbound access to port 1688) is insufficient because the
traffic is routed through the Azure Firewall (FW1) before it would ever encounter the NSG attached to the
subnet. The firewall has the first opportunity to inspect and block the traffic. Therefore, allowing traffic on the
NSG won't bypass the existing firewall rule which is blocking traffic from KMS. The correct approach is to
permit the traffic on FW1.
Creating an outbound network rule on FW1 specifically for KMS allows the activation requests to reach the
KMS servers, enabling successful activation. This solution addresses the root cause of the problem, which is
the firewall blocking necessary traffic.
Relevant Links:
Azure Firewall: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/overview
Key Management Services (KMS) Activation: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows-server/get-
started/kms-client-activation-keys
Azure Network Security Groups: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/network-security-
groups-overview
Azure NAT Gateway: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/nat-gateway/nat-overview
Azure Load Balancer: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/load-balancer/load-balancer-overview

Question: 345 CertyIQ
You have an Azure Private Link service named PL1 that uses an Azure load balancer named LB1.
You need to ensure that PL1 can support a higher volume of outbound traffic.
What should you do?
A.Increase the number of frontend IP configurations for LB1.
B.Increase the number of NAT IP addresses assigned to PL1.
C.Deploy an Azure Application Gateway v2 instance to the source NAT subnet.
D.Redeploy LB1 with a different SKU.
Answer: B
Explanation:
The correct answer is B, increasing the number of NAT IP addresses assigned to PL1. Here's why:
Azure Private Link services leverage Network Address Translation (NAT) to allow multiple client connections
from the Private Endpoint to communicate with backend resources behind the Private Link service. The load
balancer associated with the Private Link service uses a pool of NAT IP addresses for outbound connections.
When a client initiates a connection through a Private Endpoint, one of these NAT IP addresses is used to
route traffic from the client's subnet, via the Private Link service and the backend load balancer, to the
appropriate backend instance.
Each NAT IP address can support a limited number of concurrent connections before it becomes exhausted. If
the Private Link service is experiencing high volumes of outbound traffic, it's possible that the existing NAT IP
addresses are reaching their connection limits.
Increasing the number of NAT IP addresses directly increases the capacity of outbound connections that the
Private Link service can handle. This is because each additional IP address provides a new set of ports for
establishing connections, effectively multiplying the connection capacity.
Option A is incorrect because increasing the number of frontend IP configurations for the load balancer does
not directly increase the number of outbound connections available through the Private Link service's NAT.
Frontend IP configurations are more relevant for inbound traffic distribution.
Option C is not appropriate as deploying an Azure Application Gateway in front of the source NAT subnet
would introduce unnecessary complexity and is not the intended way to scale outbound connections for a
Private Link service. Application Gateway is primarily used for web application traffic and not for general
outbound NAT scaling.
Option D is not directly related to increasing outbound capacity. Redeploying the load balancer with a
different SKU might offer some performance improvements but doesn't inherently address the limitation of
NAT IP address exhaustion. While a different SKU could potentially offer different connection limits, the most
direct and manageable solution is simply to increase the number of NAT IP addresses.
Therefore, scaling the number of NAT IP addresses is the most direct and efficient way to ensure that PL1 can
support a higher volume of outbound traffic. It directly addresses the potential bottleneck of NAT IP address
exhaustion when dealing with a high volume of connections.
For more information, refer to these resources:
Azure Private Link documentation: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/private-link/private-link-overview
Azure Load Balancer documentation: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/load-balancer/

Question: 346 CertyIQ
You have an on-premises network named Site1.
You have an Azure subscription that contains a virtual network named VNet1 and a storage account named
storage1.
Site1 and VNet1 are connected by using a Site-to-Site (S2S) VPN.
You need to ensure that the servers in Site1 can connect to storage1 by using the S2S VPN. The solution must
minimize administrative effort.
What should you create on VNet1?
A.an Azure application gateway
B.an Azure Private Link service
C.a service endpoint
D.a private endpoint
Answer: D
Explanation:
The correct answer is D. a private endpoint. Here's why:
The goal is to allow on-premises servers (Site1) to securely access Azure Storage Account (storage1) via the
existing Site-to-Site VPN connection (S2S) between Site1 and VNet1. We need to ensure that the traffic to
storage1 traverses the VPN tunnel and avoids going over the public internet.
A private endpoint provides private connectivity to Azure services, including storage accounts, using a private
IP address from your virtual network. This private IP is within VNet1's address space. When Site1 initiates a
connection to storage1 via the VPN, the DNS resolution, properly configured, will resolve the storage account
name to the private IP address of the private endpoint. This ensures the traffic routes directly through the S2S
VPN tunnel to VNet1 and then to the storage account, without ever touching the public internet. It secures the
connectivity within the private network infrastructure.
Let's analyze why the other options are incorrect:
A. Azure Application Gateway: Application Gateway is primarily a web traffic load balancer and web
application firewall. While it can provide secure access to web applications, it doesn't directly provide private
connectivity to Azure Storage Accounts from an on-premises network. It's not designed for this scenario.
B. Azure Private Link service: A Private Link service is used to expose your services privately to other
consumers (e.g., customers) through their virtual networks. It's the opposite of what we need here. We're
trying to consume an Azure service (storage1) privately, not expose a service.
C. Service endpoint: Service endpoints provide secure and direct connectivity from your virtual network to
Azure service resources (like storage) without requiring a private IP address in the VNet. While service
endpoints secure the connection from the VNet, they still use a public IP address for the storage account. This
means that the traffic from Site1 via the S2S VPN would still need to be NAT'ed to a public IP to reach the
storage account's public endpoint, which isn't the desired secure approach (requiring a NSG rule allowing
internet access) and won't go through the S2S VPN (unless custom routing and NSGs are configured).
Furthermore, access would still be controlled by the storage account firewall, allowing traffic from the
specific VNet subnet, but not from on-premises networks using their internal private IPs without potentially
further configuration. We want direct and private IP-based connectivity from Site1, which a service endpoint
doesn't directly offer.Using a private endpoint associated with VNet1 and ensuring proper DNS resolution for
storage1 from Site1 will ensure traffic between Site1 and storage1 flows via the S2S VPN using private IPs on

both sides. This fulfills the requirement of minimizing administrative effort while providing secure and private
access.
Relevant documentation:
Azure Private Endpoint: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/private-link/private-endpoint-overview
Azure Service Endpoint: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/service-endpoints-overview

Question: 348 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains the following resources:
•A virtual network named Vnet1
•Two subnets named subnet1 and AzureFirewallSubnet
•A public Azure Firewall named FW1
•A route table named RT1 that is associated to Subnet1
•A rule routing of 0.0.0.0/0 to FW1 in RT1
After deploying 10 servers that run Windows Server to Subnet1, you discover that none of the virtual machines
were activated.
You need to ensure that the virtual machines can be activated.
What should you do?
A.Add an internet route to RT1 for the Azure Key Management Service (KMS).
B.On FW1, create an outbound service tag rule for Azure Cloud.
C.Deploy a NAT gateway.
D.On FW1, configure a DNAT rule for TCP port 1688.
Answer: B
Explanation:

The correct answer is B: On FW1, create an outbound service tag rule for Azure Cloud.
Here's why:
The problem is that the Windows VMs in Subnet1 cannot activate. Windows activation relies on
communicating with a Key Management Service (KMS) server, which often resides on the internet (Azure
KMS). Since RT1 routes all internet-bound traffic (0.0.0.0/0) to FW1, the firewall is blocking the activation
traffic.
Option A is incorrect because adding an internet route for KMS to RT1 would create a routing loop, bypassing
the firewall and effectively disabling its security controls, which is undesirable in a secure network design.
The existing 0.0.0.0/0 route to the firewall is already intended for all internet traffic.
Option C, deploying a NAT Gateway, would provide outbound internet connectivity for the VMs, but it wouldn't
specifically allow traffic to the Azure KMS servers, and the traffic would still be blocked by FW1 unless rules
are configured. It adds unnecessary cost and complexity when a simpler solution is available.
Option D, configuring a DNAT rule for TCP port 1688 on FW1, is incorrect because KMS activation traffic
originates from the VMs as outbound traffic. DNAT is used for inbound traffic to translate public IP addresses
and ports to private IP addresses and ports within the VNet.
By creating an outbound service tag rule on FW1 for the AzureCloud service tag, we allow outbound traffic
from the VMs in Subnet1 specifically to Azure's global infrastructure, which includes the KMS servers. Service
tags represent a group of IP address prefixes from a given Azure service. Using the AzureCloud service tag
ensures that your rule automatically stays up-to-date as Azure's IP addresses change. This approach ensures
the virtual machines can reach the KMS servers without compromising the overall network security provided
by the firewall. The AzureCloud service tag is documented by Microsoft to include the necessary IPs for
services like KMS.
In summary, using the AzureCloud service tag on FW1 is the most direct, secure, and cost-effective solution to
allow Windows VM activation while maintaining the integrity of the network security design.
Supporting links:
Azure Firewall Service Tags: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/firewall/service-tags
Azure Key Management Services (KMS) activation: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows-server/get-
started/kms-client-activation-keys

Question: 351 CertyIQ

You need to ensure that the resources connected to Subnet1 can access only storage1 and storage3. The solution
must minimize administrative effort.
What should you configure?

A.an application security group
B.Azure Private Link
C.a service endpoint policy
D.a service tag
Answer: C
Explanation:
C. a service endpoint policy.

Storage, from a given subnet. By configuring a service endpoint policy on Subnet1, you can specify which

Question: 352 CertyIQ
You have an on-premises network named Site1.
You have an Azure subscription that contains a storage account named storage1 and a virtual network named
VNet1. VNet1 contains a subnet named Subnet1. A private endpoint for storage1 is connected to Subnet1. Site1 is
connected to VNet1 by using a Site-to-Site (S2S) VPN.
You need to control access to storage1 from Site1 by using network security groups (NSGs),
What should you do first?
A.Configure a network policy for private endpoints on Subnet1.
B.Create a subnet delegation on Subnet1.
C.Associate a route table with Subnet1.
D.Associate a NAT gateway with Subnet1.
Answer: A
Explanation:
The correct answer is A: Configure a network policy for private endpoints on Subnet1.
Here's why:
The scenario requires controlling access to Azure Storage account storage1 from the on-premises network
Site1 through a private endpoint connected to Subnet1 in VNet1. Regular NSG rules don't directly apply to
traffic destined for a private endpoint. Private endpoints, while appearing to be within a subnet, utilize a
special type of IP address that isn't directly managed by NSGs in the standard manner. Network policies for
private endpoints provide a mechanism to apply rules that govern the traffic flowing to the private endpoint
specifically.
Configuring a network policy for private endpoints on Subnet1 allows you to define rules that control which
traffic sources (in this case, Site1, which is connected via S2S VPN) are permitted to access the private
endpoint of storage1. This is the only method to apply security controls on traffic using a private endpoint.
Options B, C, and D are incorrect:
B. Create a subnet delegation on Subnet1: Subnet delegation allows assigning a specific Azure service to a

subnet, granting it exclusive control over that subnet. This is not directly relevant to controlling access to the
storage account. Subnet delegation has very specific uses, and it doesn't affect the access control to the
service behind a private endpoint.
C. Associate a route table with Subnet1: Route tables control the routing of traffic leaving the subnet. While
you can use route tables to redirect traffic, it does not directly help control the traffic going to the private
endpoint from the on-premises network. A route table would influence where the traffic goes after it is
allowed to reach the subnet, not whether it is allowed to reach it in the first place.
D. Associate a NAT gateway with Subnet1: NAT gateways are used for outbound connectivity from a subnet
to the internet. This is irrelevant to the requirement of controlling inbound access from Site1 to the private
endpoint. NAT gateway masks the internal subnet IPs with a public IP for outbound traffic.
Therefore, using a network policy for private endpoints is the appropriate way to secure access to the private
endpoint from the on-premises network and fulfil the stated need.
References:
Azure Private Endpoint Network Policies: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/private-link/disable-
private-endpoint-network-policy
What is Azure Private Link? https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/private-link/private-link-overview
Question: 353 CertyIQ
DRAG DROP
-
You have two Azure subscriptions named Sub1 and Sub2 that contain the resources shown in the following table.
VNet1 and VNet2 are NOT connected.
You plan to create an Azure Private Link service named Link1 that will be used to connect VNet1 and VNet2.

•Prevents VM1 from connecting to the other resources that are connected to VNet2

to the correct virtual networks. Each resource may be used once, more than once, or not at all. You may need to
drag the split bar between panes or scroll to view content.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.

Answer:
Explanation:
VNet1: A Private Endpoint.
Private Endpoints allow secure, private access to Azure services over a private IP within a VNet.
This is typically used when you want to connect to services like Azure Storage, Azure SQL, or Web Apps
without exposing them to the public internet.
It helps isolate traffic to a specific Virtual Network (VNet1) and prevents direct internet access to the
resource.

Private Endpoint enables private access from VNet1.

A Private/Internal Load Balancer is used within Azure VNets for internal traffic distribution.

distribution.

Question: 357 CertyIQ

Question: 359 CertyIQ

You have an Azure subscription that contains the resources shown in the following table.
You create a service endpoint policy that has the following settings:
•Associated subnets: Subnet1
•Service: Microsoft.Storage
•Scope: Single account
•Resource: storage1
Which resources can VM1 access?
A.storage1 in the East US Azure region and its replica in the paired region
B.storage1 in the East US Azure region only
C.storage1 and storage2 in the East US Azure region only
D.storage1 and storage2 in the East US Azure region and their replicas in the paired region
Answer: A
Explanation:
A. storage1 in the East US Azure region and its replica in the paired region.
Azure Storage accounts often use geo-redundant storage (GRS) or read-access geo-redundant storage (RA-
GRS) to replicate data to a secondary region for redundancy and disaster recovery. This means that:
Primary and Secondary Access: When VM1 is allowed access to storage1 in the East US Azure region, it also
inherently has access to the replica of storage1 in the paired region. This is because geo-redundancy ensures
data availability and accessibility in both regions.
Replication: The primary region (East US) and its paired secondary region work together to ensure data
consistency and availability. Therefore, access to the storage account in the primary region implies access to
its replica in the secondary region.

Question: 361 CertyIQ
You have an Azure subscription that contains a virtual network named VNet1.
You plan to deploy Point-to-Site (P2S) VPN access to VNet1.
You need to recommend an authentication method for the deployment. The solution must meet the following
requirements:
•Require the use of a FIDO2 security key
•Analyze user sign-in behavior
•Require a user to change their password if their sign-in behavior is identified as risky
What should you recommend?
A.RADIUS
B.Microsoft Entra
C.smart card
D.Azure certificate
Answer: B
Explanation:
The correct answer is Microsoft Entra (formerly Azure Active Directory). Here's why:

FIDO2 Security Key Support: Microsoft Entra ID natively supports authentication using FIDO2 security keys,
fulfilling the first requirement. Users can register their FIDO2 keys with their Azure AD accounts.
Analyze User Sign-in Behavior: Microsoft Entra ID Protection provides risk-based conditional access policies.
This service analyzes user sign-in behavior, detecting anomalies such as sign-ins from unusual locations or
devices.
Require Password Change for Risky Sign-ins: When Microsoft Entra ID Protection detects a risky sign-in, it
can automatically require the user to change their password, meeting the final requirement.
RADIUS Incompatibility: RADIUS (Remote Authentication Dial-In User Service) is primarily used for network
access authentication. While it can be integrated with Azure AD, it doesn't directly offer FIDO2 support or
risk-based sign-in analysis.
Smart Cards Limitations: Smart cards provide strong authentication but don't offer the same level of built-in
risk analysis as Microsoft Entra ID Protection.
Azure Certificate Drawbacks: Azure certificates primarily handle machine or application authentication, not
user authentication with FIDO2 keys and risk analysis.
Microsoft Entra provides a comprehensive solution with its native FIDO2 support and advanced security
features like risk-based conditional access, making it the ideal choice for this scenario.
Authoritative Links:
Microsoft Entra ID: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/active-directory/fundamentals/whatis
FIDO2 Security Keys in Azure AD: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/active-
directory/authentication/concept-authentication-passwordless
Microsoft Entra ID Protection: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/active-directory/identity-
protection/overview-identity-protection

Question: 363 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT
-
You have an Azure subscription. The subscription contains two virtual machine scale sets that host two apps
named App1 and App2, an Azure Private Link service named PLS1, and an Azure load balancer named LB1. PLS1

uses LB1 and has TCP Proxy V2 disabled. PLS1 provides access to App1 only.
You need to perform the following actions:
•Provide access to App1 and App2.
•Increase the number of supported private endpoint connections.

supported connections? To answer, select the appropriate options in the answer area.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
Answer:
Explanation:
App2: Azure Load Balancer inbound NAT rules.
Supported connections: The Azure Load Balancer frontend IP configuration.
To increase the number of supported private endpoint connections on a standard Azure load balancer, you can
create multiple Private Link Services on the same load balancer. Create new Private Link services while
specifying the existing standard load balancer as the target, ensuring you select the appropriate frontend IP
configuration for each new service within the load balancer settings.

https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/private-link/create-private-link-service-portal?tabs=dynamic-
ip#create-private-endpoint

Question: 366 CertyIQ
DRAG DROP
-
You have an Azure subscription that contains the resources shown in the following table.
VNet1 and VNet2 are peered with each other. Subnet1 contains only VM1.

You need to configure Subnet11 to support 500 IP addresses. The solution must ensure that all traffic is routed
between VNet1 and VNet2 and users can access App1.
Which three actions should you perform in sequence? To answer, move the appropriate actions from the list of
actions to the answer area and arrange them in the correct order.
Answer:

Explanation:
1. Stop and deallocate VM1
A running VM is bound to a NIC configuration that can’t be modified in real time for some networking changes.
You must stop and deallocate a VM to:
Move its NIC to another subnet.
Change its private IP address allocation.
Modify subnet addressing.
What happens when you deallocate:
The VM’s compute resources are released, and its NIC configuration can then be changed safely.
2. Increase the size of Subnet11
If Subnet11 runs out of IP addresses, or you need to include a larger range, you must resize (increase) the
subnet address range.
Prerequisites:
The VNet’s address space must have unused IP range contiguous to Subnet11.
You can only expand into free space that doesn’t overlap another subnet.

Important note:
This operation is online — you don’t have to delete or recreate the subnet if it’s an expansion (only contraction
requires resource movement).
3. Sync the peerings between VNet1 and VNet2
When you modify a VNet’s address space (for example, you add a new subnet or extend an existing one), you
must re-sync the peering to make sure both VNets recognize the updated address ranges.
Otherwise:
Connectivity may fail because one side still references the old network configuration.
How to do it:
Azure Portal → VNet → Peerings → click “Sync”

Question: 368 CertyIQ
HOTSPOT
-
You have an Azure subscription that contains the resources shown in the following table.
Policy1 has the following settings:
•Service: Microsoft Storage
•Allowed Resources: storage1
Subnet1 has the following settings:
•Name: Subnet
•Subnet address range: 10.0.0.0/24
•NAT gateway: None
•Network security group: None
•Route table: None
•Service Endpoints
oServices: Microsoft Storage
oService endpoint policies: Policy1
•Subnet delegation
oDelegate subnet to a service: None
Subnet2 has the following settings:

•Name: Subnet2
•Subnet address range: 10.0.1.0/24
•NAT gateway: None
•Network security group: None
•Route table: None
•Service Endpoints
oServices: 0 selected
•Subnet Delegation
oDelegate subnet to a service: None
For each of the following statements, select Yes if the statement is true. Otherwise, select No.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
Answer:
Explanation:
1. Devices on Subnet1 can access storage1 (Yes)
The Configuration: Subnet1 has been granted access to the storage account via a Service Endpoint or a Virtual
Network Rule.
Why it works: When you configure a storage account to allow traffic from a specific subnet, Azure adds that
subnet’s ID to the storage account’s firewall. Any resource (like a VM) inside Subnet1 now has a "green light"
to pass through the storage firewall.
2. Devices on Subnet2 can access storage1 (Yes)
The Configuration: Similar to Subnet1, Subnet2 has also been explicitly added to the allowed networks list for
storage1.

Why it works: Network security in Azure storage is additive. You can whitelist multiple subnets from the same
VNet or different VNets. Since both subnets are marked "Yes," they are both part of the storage account's
"Firewalls and virtual networks" allow-list.
3. Devices on Subnet1 can access storage2 (No)
The Configuration: Subnet1 has not been added to the whitelist for storage2, or storage2 has its firewall set to
"Disabled" or "Selected networks" without Subnet1 included.
Why it fails: By default, when you secure an Azure Storage account to specific networks, all other traffic is
blocked. Even though Subnet1 can see storage1, it is treated as an external, untrusted source by storage2.
Question: 369 CertyIQ
DRAG DROP
-
You have two Azure subscriptions named Sub1 and Sub2. Each subscription is linked to a separate Microsoft Entra
tenant. Sub1 contains a virtual machine scale set that hosts an app named App1. Sub2 contains a virtual network
named VNet1.
You need to connect VNet1 to App1 by using an Azure Private Link service.
Which four actions should you perform in sequence? To answer, move the appropriate actions from the list of
actions to the answer area and arrange them in the correct order.
Answer:

Explanation:
Configure DB1 to run behind a Standard Azure load balancer.
Why: A Private Link service (custom) exposes your service through a front-end (the load balancer) that
distributes traffic to the backend VMs/instances (your DB). Azure requires the Standard SKU load balancer for
custom Private Link services. Make sure DB VM NICs (or VMSS) are in the LB backend pool, health probes and
rules are configured for the DB port(s).
In Sub1, create a Private Link service to reference the load balancer.
Why: The provider (Sub1) creates the Private Link service resource and points it to the load balancer/backend.
This resource is what consumers will connect to; it exposes a service ID (resource ID) that consumers use to
request connectivity.
In Sub2, create a private endpoint by using the Private Link service ID.
Why: The consumer (Sub2) creates a Private Endpoint resource in their VNet and specifies the provider’s
Private Link service ID (or alias). This creates a NIC in the consumer VNet with a private IP and initiates a
connection request to the provider’s service.
Approve the connection request.
Why: The provider must accept the incoming connection request (portal/CLI/PowerShell) unless auto-approval
was configured. Once approved, the private endpoint is linked to the provider’s service and traffic flows
privately over the Azure backbone to the load balancer → DB backend.
Question: 370 CertyIQ
DRAG DROP
-
You have two Azure subscriptions named Sub1 and Sub2. Each subscription is linked to a separate Microsoft Entra
tenant. Sub1 contains a virtual machine scale set that hosts an app named App1. Sub2 contains a virtual network
named VNet1.

You need to connect VNet1 to App1 by using an Azure Private Link service.
Which four actions should you perform in sequence? To answer, move the appropriate actions from the list of
actions to the answer area and arrange them in the correct order.
Answer:
1. Create an internal load balancer in Sub1.2. Create a Private Link service that references the internal load
balancer.3. Create a private endpoint in Sub2.4. Approve the private endpoint connection for the Private Link
service.

Hub Virtual Network (where the ExpressRoute Virtual Network Gateway is deployed), IS NOT SUPPORTED.
FastPath only supports connectivity to Private Endpoints/Link services deployed to the Hub Virtual Network.

Introductory Info Case Study -
This is a case study. Case studies are not timed separately. You can use as much exam time as you would like to
complete each case. However, there may be additional case studies and sections on this exam. You must manage

case study. Case studies might contain exhibits and other resources that provide more information about the
scenario that is described in the case study. Each question is independent of the other questions in this case study.
At the end of this case study, a review screen will appear. This screen allows you to review your answers and to
make changes before you move to the next section of the exam. After you begin a new section, you cannot return
to this section.
To start the case study -
To display the first question in this case study, click the Next button. Use the buttons in the left pane to explore
the content of the case study before you answer the questions. Clicking these buttons displays information such
as business requirements, existing environment, and problem statements. When you are ready to answer a
question, click the Question button to return to the question.
Overview -
Litware, Inc. is a financial company that has a main datacenter in Boston and 20 branch offices across the United
States. Users have Android, iOS, and Windows
10 devices.
Existing Environment -
Hybrid Environment -
The on-premises network contains an Active Directory forest named litwareinc.com that syncs to an Azure Active
Directory (Azure AD) tenant named litwareinc.com by using Azure AD Connect.
All offices connect to a virtual network named Vnet1 by using a Site-to-Site VPN connection.
Azure Environment -
Litware has an Azure subscription named Sub1 that is linked to the litwareinc.com Azure AD tenant. Sub1 contains
resources in the East US Azure region as shown in the following table.

A diagram of the resource in the East US Azure region is shown in the Azure Network Diagram exhibit.
There is bidirectional peering between Vnet1 and Vnet2. There is bidirectional peering between Vnet1 and Vnet3.
Currently, Vnet2 and Vnet3 cannot communicate directly.
Azure Network Diagram -

Requirements -
Business Requirements -
Litware wants to minimize costs whenever possible, as long as all other requirements are met.
Virtual Networking Requirements -
Litware identifies the following virtual networking requirements:
Direct the default route of 0.0.0.0/0 on Vnet2 and Vnet3 to the Boston datacenter over an ExpressRoute circuit.
Ensure that the records in the cloud.litwareinc.com can be resolved from the on-premises locations.
Automatically register the DNS names of Azure virtual machines to the cloud.litwareinc.com zone.
Minimize the size of the subnets allocated to platform-managed services.
Allow traffic from VMScaleSet1 to VMScaleSet2 on the TCP port 443 only.
Hybrid Networking Requirements -
Litware identifies the following hybrid networking requirements:
Users must be able to connect to Vnet1 by using a Point-to-Site (P2S) VPN when working remotely. Connections
must be authenticated by Azure AD.
Latency of the traffic between the Boston datacenter and all the virtual networks must be minimized.
The Boston datacenter must connect to the Azure virtual networks by using an ExpressRoute FastPath connection.
Traffic between Vnet2 and Vnet3 must be routed through Vnet1.
PaaS Networking Requirements -
Litware identifies the following networking requirements for platform as a service (PaaS):
The storage1 account must be accessible from all on-premises locations without exposing the public endpoint of
storage1.

The storage2 account must be accessible from Vnet2 and Vnet3 without exposing the public endpoint of storage2.
Question You need to provide access to storage1. The solution must meet the PaaS networking requirements and
the business requirements.
What should you include in the solution?
A. a private endpoint
B. Azure Traffic Manager
C. Azure Front Door
D. a service endpoint
Answer: A
Explanation:
Private Endpoint: A private endpoint allows you to connect your virtual network (VNet) to Azure services
privately. With a private endpoint, the traffic between your VNet and the service (in this case, storage1) stays
within the Azure backbone network, providing secure and private connectivity.
The storage1 account must be accessible from all on-premises locations without exposing the public endpoint
of storage1.
Question: 373 CertyIQ
You need to provide access to storage2. The solution must meet the PaaS networking requirements and the
business requirements.
Which connectivity method should you use?
A. a private endpoint
B. Azure Firewall
C. Azure Front Door
D. a service endpoint
Answer: A
Explanation:
The correct answer is A: a private endpoint. Here's why:
The scenario emphasizes PaaS networking requirements and business needs for secure access to storage2
(an Azure Storage account). Private endpoints directly address these requirements by providing private IP
addresses within your VNet for accessing Azure services. This eliminates the need to expose the storage
account publicly, enhancing security.
Service endpoints (option D) also provide secure access to Azure services, but they work by adding a route to
the storage account's public IP address range, allowing traffic originating from the specified VNet. While
secure, this approach doesn't completely isolate the service from the public internet like a private endpoint
does.
Azure Firewall (option B) provides centralized network security and can filter traffic, but it primarily protects
against outbound traffic and doesn't inherently establish private connectivity to PaaS services. It could be
used in conjunction with a private endpoint but isn't the direct solution for private access.

Azure Front Door (option C) is a global, scalable entry point that uses the Microsoft global edge network to
deliver fast, reliable, and secure access to web applications, APIs, and content. It's not primarily designed to
provide private network connectivity to PaaS services.
Private endpoints directly map to the need for PaaS networking and secure private access to storage2. They
provide the most secure option because all traffic between the VNet and the storage account remains within
the Microsoft Azure backbone network. This is preferred for security and compliance and aligns well with the
PaaS networking requirement. They enable you to lock down storage accounts from public internet access
entirely, enhancing security posture.
Further Reading:
Microsoft Documentation on Private Endpoints: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/private-link/private-
endpoint-overview
Microsoft Documentation on Service Endpoints: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-
network/service-endpoints-overview

Question: 375 CertyIQ
You need to configure GW1 to meet the network security requirements for the P2S VPN users.
Which Tunnel type should you select in the Point-to-site configuration settings of GW1?
A. IKEv2 and OpenVPN (SSL)
B. IKEv2
C. IKEv2 and SSTP (SSL)
D. OpenVPN (SSL)
E. SSTP (SSL)
Answer: D
Explanation:
The correct answer is D. OpenVPN (SSL). Here's why:
The question focuses on network security requirements for Point-to-Site (P2S) VPN users. OpenVPN (SSL)
offers robust security features essential for this scenario.
SSL/TLS Encryption: OpenVPN leverages the well-established SSL/TLS protocol suite for encryption. This
encryption secures the data transmitted between the client and the VPN gateway (GW1), preventing
eavesdropping and unauthorized access.
Flexibility and Customization: OpenVPN is highly configurable, allowing administrators to fine-tune security
parameters like cipher suites, key lengths, and authentication methods to meet specific security needs.
Firewall Traversal: OpenVPN can operate over UDP or TCP ports, making it easier to traverse restrictive
firewalls and Network Address Translation (NAT) devices, which is critical for ensuring reliable connectivity
for P2S VPN users connecting from various locations.

Strong Authentication: OpenVPN supports various authentication methods, including certificates and
usernames/passwords, enabling multi-factor authentication for enhanced security.
While IKEv2 offers good performance and security, it primarily focuses on site-to-site VPNs. SSTP (SSL), while
also using SSL, is known to have some potential security vulnerabilities compared to OpenVPN and might not
be as widely supported across different client operating systems. The combination options (A and C) include
IKEv2, which isn't the primary choice for enhanced security focused P2S VPN scenarios and also adds
unnecessary protocol complexity for this specific use case.
Therefore, OpenVPN (SSL) provides the best balance of security, flexibility, and client compatibility for
securing P2S VPN connections.
Authoritative Links:
About Point-to-site VPN: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/vpn-gateway/vpn-gateway-about-point-to-
site-vpn
Configure OpenVPN for P2S VPN: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/vpn-gateway/vpn-gateway-
howto-openvpn
Question: 376 CertyIQ
What should you implement to meet the virtual network requirements for the virtual machines that connect to
Vnet4 and Vnet5?
A. a private endpoint
B. a routing table
C. a service endpoint
D. a private link service
E. a virtual network peering
Answer: E
Explanation:
The correct answer is E, virtual network peering. Let's justify this choice:
Virtual network peering establishes a direct connection between two virtual networks, enabling resources in
each network to communicate with each other as if they were in the same network. This satisfies the
requirement for VMs connected to Vnet4 and Vnet5 to communicate. Virtual network peering supports both
intra-region and inter-region connections, and traffic between the virtual networks stays within the Microsoft
Azure backbone network, ensuring secure and low-latency communication.
Private endpoints (A) securely connect to an Azure service via a private IP address from your virtual network.
They are for connecting to services outside the VNet, not for connecting two VNets. Routing tables (B) control
the routing of traffic within a virtual network, but they don't create the actual connection between networks.
Service endpoints (C) provide secure and direct connectivity to Azure services from within your virtual
network. They are similar to private endpoints, allowing access to services like Azure Storage or Azure SQL
Database, but not for inter-VNet communication. Private link service (D) enables you to expose your own
service behind a standard Azure load balancer and make it available to others privately. This is useful for
service providers to offer services privately to their customers.
Therefore, virtual network peering is the most suitable solution for directly connecting Vnet4 and Vnet5 to
facilitate communication between the virtual machines deployed in these networks. Peering offers a direct,

secure, and performant connection.
For more information, refer to:
Azure virtual network peering: https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-network/virtual-network-
peering-overview

Which three actions should you perform in sequence for Vnet1? To answer, move the appropriate actions from the
list of actions to the answer.

Answer:
Explanation:

The existing VPN GW1 GatewaySubnet is too small with /29.
Users must be able to connect to Vnet1 by using a Point-to-Site (P2S) VPN when working remotely.
Connections must be authenticated by Azure AD.
Litware wants to minimize costs whenever possible, as long as all other requirements are met.

Step 2: Set the subnet mask of Gateway Subnet to /27.
Step 3: Create a VPN gateway by using the VPN GW1 SKU.
Reference:
https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/vpn-gateway/vpn-gateway-about-vpngateways
https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/expressroute/how-to-configure-coexisting-gateway-portal
Question: 378 CertyIQ
You need to connect Vnet2 and Vnet3. The solution must meet the virtual networking requirements and the
business requirements.
Which two actions should you include in the solution? Each correct answer presents part of the solution.
NOTE: Each correct selection is worth one point.
A. On the peering from Vnet1, select Allow gateway transit.
B. On the peerings from Vnet2 and Vnet3, select Use remote gateways.
C. On the peerings from Vnet2 and Vnet3, select Allow gateway transit.
D. On the peering from Vnet1, select Use remote gateways.
E. On the peering from Vnet1, select Allow forwarded traffic.
Answer: AB
Explanation:
The correct answer is A and B. Here's why:
Virtual Network Peering with Gateway Transit: The core problem is to connect Vnet2 and Vnet3 while
leveraging the existing VPN gateway in Vnet1. This requires configuring Vnet Peering with gateway transit.
Gateway transit enables Vnet2 and Vnet3 to use the VPN gateway located in Vnet1.
Allow Gateway Transit on Vnet1's Peering: To enable Vnet1 to share its gateway, you need to allow gateway
transit on the peering from Vnet1 to each of the other VNets (Vnet2 and Vnet3). This means that from the
Vnet1 side of the peering, it is allowing the other side to use its gateway.
Use Remote Gateways on Vnet2 and Vnet3's Peerings: Vnet2 and Vnet3 need to specify that they should
use the remote gateways. This setting is applied on each of the peerings from Vnet2 to Vnet1 and from Vnet3
to Vnet1. In essence, Vnet2 and Vnet3 are configured to look for a gateway in the peered network (Vnet1).
Why C is incorrect: Selecting "Allow gateway transit" on the peerings from Vnet2 and Vnet3 to Vnet1 is
incorrect. Vnet2 and Vnet3 are not providing the gateway; they are using a gateway in a remote network. The
"Allow gateway transit" setting is for the network that owns the gateway (Vnet1).
Why D and E are incorrect: Option D, "Use remote gateways" on Vnet1's peering, is backward in logic. Vnet1 is
the one providing the gateway. Option E, "Allow forwarded traffic", is a more general setting related to traffic
flow within and between peered VNets and isn't specific to gateway transit.

In summary: Enabling gateway transit involves two complementary actions: the gateway-owning VNet (Vnet1)
must allow gateway transit, and the gateway-using VNets (Vnet2 and Vnet3) must be configured to use
remote gateways.
Authoritative Links:
About VPN gateway transit for virtual network peering: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/vpn-
gateway/vpn-gateway-peering-gateway-transit
Create a virtual network peering using the Azure portal: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-
network/tutorial-connect-virtual-networks-portal - Note this article contains examples of how to configure
peering, including the gateway transit option.

https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/vpn-gateway/openvpn-azure-ad-tenant

Introductory Info
Case Study -
This is a case study. Case studies are not timed separately. You can use as much exam time as you would like
to complete each case. However, there may be additional case studies and sections on this exam. You must
manage your time to ensure that you are able to complete all questions included on this exam in the time
provided.
To answer the questions included in a case study, you will need to reference information that is provided in
the case study. Case studies might contain exhibits and other resources that provide more information about
the scenario that is described in the case study. Each question is independent of the other questions in this
case study.
At the end of this case study, a review screen will appear. This screen allows you to review your answers and
to make changes before you move to the next section of the exam. After you begin a new section, you cannot
return to this section.
To start the case study -
To display the first question in this case study, click the Next button. Use the buttons in the left pane to
explore the content of the case study before you answer the questions. Clicking these buttons displays
information such as business requirements, existing environment, and problem statements. When you are
ready to answer a question, click the Question button to return to the question.
Overview -
Contoso, Ltd. is a consulting company that has a main office in San Francisco and a branch office in Dallas.
Contoso recently purchased an Azure subscription and is performing its first pilot project in Azure.
Existing Environment -
Azure Network Infrastructure -
Contoso has an Azure Active Directory (Azure AD) tenant named contoso.com.
The Azure subscription contains the virtual networks shown in the following table.
Vnet1 contains a virtual network gateway named GW1.
Azure Virtual Machines -
The Azure subscription contains virtual machines that run Windows Server 2019 as shown in the following
table.

The NSGs are associated to the network interfaces on the virtual machines. Each NSG has one custom
security rule that allows RDP connections from the internet. The firewall on each virtual machine allows ICMP
traffic.
An application security group named ASG1 is associated to the network interface of VM1.
Azure Network Infrastructure Diagram
Azure Private DNS Zones -
The Azure subscription contains the Azure private DNS zones shown in the following table.
Zone1.contoso.com has the virtual network links shown in the following table.

Other Azure Resources -
The Azure subscription contains additional resources as shown in the following table.
Requirements -
Virtual Network Requirements -
Contoso has the following virtual network requirements:
Create a virtual network named Vnet6 in West US that will contain the following resources and
configurations:
- Two container groups that connect to Vnet6
- Three virtual machines that connect to Vnet6
- Allow VPN connections to be established to Vnet6
- Allow the resources in Vnet6 to access KeyVault1, DB1, and Vnet1 over the Microsoft backbone network.
The virtual machines in Vnet4 and Vnet5 must be able to communicate over the Microsoft backbone network.
A virtual machine named VM-Analyze will be deployed to Subnet1. VM-Analyze must inspect the outbound
network traffic from Subnet2 to the internet.
Network Security Requirements -
Contoso has the following network security requirements:
Configure Azure Active Directory (Azure AD) authentication for Point-to-Site (P2S) VPN users.
Enable NSG flow logs for NSG3 and NSG4.
Create an NSG named NSG10 that will be associated to Vnet1/Subnet1 and will have the custom inbound
security rules shown in the following table.
Create an NSG named NSG11 that will be associated to Vnet1/Subnet2 and will have the custom outbound

security rules shown in the following table.
Question: 380 CertyIQ
Introductory Info Case Study -
